The RED PAGE
AUGUST.
A fat moon leers in the coppery sky,
And the fruit’s a-rot on the cabbage-
leaves ;
A thick Augean stable steam
From its blistered asphalt Holborn heaves
To the stars that are tired, on an air that is
sick;
And the water’s warm in the drinking-
trough.
How is it here in Hell? —Well, 'ot,
And the oldest .Rotten’un calls it tough.
For the tiles that look so cool to the eye
Would grill you holes in a hippo’s hide ;
And the great Unwashed, that has sworn off
soap,
Is lyric and loud in his noisome pride;
And the sawdust, spread for the leaky of lip,
Hops with a small, black, nimble plague,
Whose cannibal methods of waging war
Lack the imprimatur of The Hague;
And the huge coke cooking-fires that see
The seasons in, and the seasons out,
And never die, are a searing curse
That would make an Andaman Islander
shout;
And the fizzling bloater’s viscous smoke
Hangs, a blue net, our heads above,
And a million obscene, pest-drunk flies
Buzz in the acrid meshes thereof;
And .the tables’ litter, with spilth of grease,
The well-gnawed bone and the ill-gnawed
crust,
The stale tea-leaves and the spatters of
milk—
Knew Dante a circle called Disgust?...
How the sick gorge dreams of a far-back
day.
Of a green-bowered inn by' the Thames’
cool blue,
Of a cantaloup franne , a salmon trout,
And a bottle of Hock!
And that’s Hell. too.
W. A. Mackenzie, Rowton House Rhymes.
Out of the Depths
Since The Ballad of Reading; Gaol no book
of verse lias come out of England which has
been charged so fully with bitter personal
experience -as the Rowton House Rhymes of
William Andrew Mackenzie (Blackwood).
Wilde fell from a life of comfort and elegance
into disgrace and gaol. The drop to Row-
ton House, a night refuge of London, was
not so far, yet far enough to shake up a
sensitive man who had known better things.
Mackenzie does not state his own experi-
ences ; but the uncompromising vigor with
which he pictures phases of low life, and the
state of mind of the “dead-beat,” the hell 6:
Hunger and the elysium of Alcohol, imply
an acquaintance at first hand with the “doss’
and its frequenters. The author was as cer
tainly down as his culture shows that he had
previously been up.
The regular and some artificial forms of
verse are handled with ease. There is a
ballade of one who has secured a lodging for
the night:—
Here’s a bench by the Bridge and the peeler’s
away;
I lodge at the Sign of the Beautiful Star,
a pantoum for everybody, which begins :
Art is the game of an hour and a minute;
Love is a jest of a night or a day;
Life is a top and Death doth spin it;
Hunger and thirst are the things that
stay.
and a “Sestina of the Shuffling Feet” more
despairing than Lawson’s “Faces in the
Street”
"Fleeing yourself? Hood luck, mate!” says
the street.
at the joysome jig of dodging woe?
Lord. Ive seen generations come and go
Along my dingy length, and heard them beat
lhe hurried rhythm of flight with shuffling
feet.
Did ever one escape? Ido not know.
“God may. But old Despair must surely
know;
He s lurking round the corner,” laughs the
_ street.
With ‘what’s your game?’ to stay all sus-
pect feet,
And keep them marking time till up comes
Woe,
Despair s point-dutyman on Sorrow's beat,
lie swings the traffic hell-ward —down you
go!”
The poem called “Alcohol” should be read
and digested by temperance advocates, to
whom, also, “Bagster, French Polisher,” has
a word to say on behalf of Rum as a Fact
in a world of unrealities:—
BAGSTER DESCANTS:
Wot do I want? Fects, Fects. Nali,
Rum’s a feet;
Rum’s somethin’ solid in a wobbly world,
it s cheap. It’s there. It’s never fur aw’y.
It nips yer stummick. an’ it ’eats yer blood ;
Gives ye the notion that you’re still alive;
lips ye “Good-night, me lord, an’ pleasant
dreams!”
Mikes ye fergit that Luck ’as turned ye
dahn;
Mikes ye ferget yer pore ol’ woman’s stiff,
xer Jack’s a lifer, an’ yer Liz a ’ore.
Mikes ye fergit the d’ys w’en you was quite
Content to wu’lc to yet enough to eat — 1
l o ’ave the stren'th —like' all the other mugs.
Rum. Rum’s a feet!
That’s w’y you find me ’ere
Mos’ d’ys a-lappin of it up. A man
’As got to ’ave ’is Feet. Ruin's mine —wot’s
yours?
ALCOHOL.
For mistress you had fortune; I,
Fame with her lure and kibsely-knotted
zone;
And you, the wing-foot nymph whose name
is Art.
We gave them youth, we gave them hope and
heart,
For them we pledged our harvests yet un-
sown—
And they deceived us traitorously.
Drink’s now the hawd of us that weep
Our cheated past, our future spent ere won.
Her kiss is lowly priced. Forgetfulness
We have of her; and when her paid arms
press,
We are clutched into the brute oblivion
Of stertorous unliaunted sleep.
We do not bring her song of flowers;
We do not tremble when she deigns to smile,
Nor catch our breath should she decide to
frown ;
She is the common woman of the town :
Foul, faithful, free of smirk or painted wile,
She takes our silver, and is ours.
Sleep—one night’s sleep—profound, un-
stirred :
Nirvana after mons of despair;
Nepenthe after fever-rave and -rack;
A paradise where all i thank God !) is black ;
A silence deep as Tophet; and an air
Unbroken of any mocking bird ;
Such boons we beg on bended knees
From Heaven high as we are hell-deep low;
And what is Heaven's answer to our prayer V
Another blinding billow of despair,
Another clanging dawn ot hot-brained woe,
Another plague of memories.
Alcohol's kinder. So to red
Rum and to gin, the Circe virgin-white,
We turn —-to all the brands of stoppered
hell
Man brews to kill his ache intolerable,
Numb the raw rankle of the world’s barbed
slight,
And drown yon drumming in the head.
There's poison in her kiss? We know
There’s death within her cup? And do you
think
Death frights us, us? No—Life is what we
fear,
Life, the long journey, bitter and empty and
drear.
But in the fierce foul comradeship of Drink
We can, at least, in blindness go
Down the lone leagues and the sad slopes,
And over the interminable plain,
And, witting nothing, slide into the abyss
Where long have lain suake-Conscience with
her hiss,
And bludgeoned Faith, Love gashed and
slashed and slain,
And our dead youth, and our dead hopes.
“Comrades” states the ennobling power of
mateship, by which the souls of a harlot and
a thief are cleansed white as they beat up
the wind for judgment. The other extremity
—a rancid waster playing on a fellow-dere-
lict and squeezing him dry—is given in “My
Friend—Mr. Spunge,” which is, perhaps, the
most striking poem in a book of uncommon
power. There is little beauty, but there is
poetry in this book,, and it is poetry which,
like Galsworthy’s plays, will bring some ugly
realities home to people who would not other-
wise see them.
MY FRIEND—MR. SPtNGE.
He had a cough
As hollow as his hollow heart;
The wheeze was Nature, but the choke was
Art.
He said : “I knew you for a toff
First time I saw you.” Playing chess we
were,
And I was Blackburne’s master, Lasker's
equal,
And Lord knows all what else for sequel.
He played me soft as any dulcimer;
His touch
Was much
Like Pachmann’s, coaxing Chopin, no wild
clutch,
No brutal pound—he gentled me. •
He stirred among my various strings'
A zephyr in an aspen tree
That moves to song and at the same time
sings.
And Oh, the lungy way he spat!
And Oh, the cheesy sob .' —a thing to marvel
at.
* * * *
I gave my boots. I gave my shirt,
My coat I gave
•To hold this splash of London dirt
Back from the grave.
* * * *
Because I was joy whose name was Mud,
He sucked my blood.
Because 1 Would stay out and get the rain,
His task was easy and plain.
And if I 'choose to play Almighty God in
little
To him, the cringer, the lickspittle—
Well, then, I had to pay.
To that I’ve naught to‘say.
BUT —when, by some tongue-slip,
He learned my secret (all men have one), ■
And hawked it round the House for drinks—
My pain, my sorrow, for a pot of ale!—
I laid the whip
Of such hot scorn as the worm thinks
Must melt the hook, about the pale
And pasty head, and bade him run
Ere I had— o Lord, yes, he ran!
He ran
Just like —a man !
And as he sprinted he let fall a book.
“Marcus Aurelius,” you guess?
“Tristram of Lyonesse?”"
Or Omar’s wisdom? He had kissed the feet
Of all them, the cheat!
I picked it up and stole a book:
“Post Oliice Savings Bank” —his name.
I peeped inside, and felt no shame:
He’d £2B 13s. to his credit!
And so 1 knew the Thing—and I had fed It.
History as She is Wrote
The Clarendon Press, Oxford, issues a new
School History of England , by C. R. L.
Fletcher, with some verses by Kudyard Kip-
ling, and. pictures by Henry Ford. In less
than 250 pages the leading events are des-
cribed in a rough and ready fashion evi-
dently intended for junior classes. Fletcher
treats the big battles, and some of the most
picturesque events, very curtly. At times
he has a vivid phrase, as when he speaks of
Napoleon's army for the invasion of Eng-
land
If they had tried to get across unprotected
a single British warship could have pounded
them into red rice pudding in a few minutes.
Some of the later Sovereigns are summed up
tersely enough. George 11. was “a short,
ridiculous, red-faced person, with great goggle
eyes. ... He at least knew how to swear in
English, and he was rather too fond of swear-
ing.” George IV’. was “naturally clever, and
had some kind of selfish good-nature ; but he
was mean, cowardly, and an incredible liar.
He was succeeded in 1830 by a stupid,
honest old gentleman, his brother, William
IV., who as a young man had been named
‘Silly Billy.’ ” On the other hand, the late
Queen is called “Victoria the Great,” and is
ranked as one of the half-dozen greatest
“makers of England,” which is absurd.
The style would at any rate be welcomed
by kiddies after the formal duluess of most
school histories; but the fault of the book
is that it tries to explain things. Children
do not want the elements of Jingoism as
history-—they want a set of moving pictures
and a band ; the meaning of events can be
given in the senior classes.
An ideal history for children would present
the salient features of the past in a series
of short stories, somewhat as Kipling has
done in Puck of Poole’s Hill, and Rewards
and Fairies. Kipling’s contributions to this
history are not equal to the verses in those
books; but liis name will help considerably
in the sales. This is part of his version of
the story as told by the Thames: —
I w r alk my beat before London town,
Five hours up and seven down.
Up I go and I end my run
At Tide-end-town, which is Teddington.
Down I come with the mud in my hands
And plaster it over the Maplin Sands.
But I’d have you know that these waters of
mine
Were once a branch of the River Rhine,
When hundreds of miles to the East I went
And England was joined to the Continent.
I remember the bat-winged lizard-birds.
The Age of Ice and the mammoth herds,
And the giant tigers that stalked them down
Through Regent’s Park into Camden Town
And 1 remember like yesterday
The earliest Cockney who came my way,
When he pushed through the forest that lined
the Strand,
With paint on his face and a club in his
hand.
These are stanzas from another poem J
There are four good legs to my father's
chair—
Priest 'and People and Lords and Crown ;
I sit on all of ’em fair and square,
And that is the reason it. don’t break down.
When your time comes to sit in my chair,
Remember your father’s habits and rules,
Sit on all four legs, fair and square,
And never be tempted by one-legged stools
It is not often that a first book has the
fine qualities of The White Peacock, by D.
H. Lawrence (Dymock’s). The story is the
old one of two men and one girl. It is told
by the girl’s brother, but the way in which
the masculine beauty of the farmer’s son is
contrasted with the elegance of the wealthier
suitor says that D. 11. Lawrence is a lady.
The dialogue throughout is clever, and the
study of the principal girl is an excellent
one. She is attracted and repelled by the
rustic Hercules, who develops rapidly under
her influence ; but the other man gets her.
He too makes love in manly fashion, as wit-
ness this scene :—-
“Suppose now we were two angels?”
“Oh, dear —a sloppy angel!”
“Well, don’t interrupt me now —suppose
I were one—like the ‘Blessed Damosel?’”
“With a warm bosom!”
“Don’t be foolish, now —I a ‘Blessed
Damosel,’ and you kicking the brown beach
leaves below, thinking”
“Wl:at are you driving at?”
“Would you be thinking—thoughts like
pra v'-rs?”
“What on earth do you ask that for? Oh
—I think I’d be cursing, eh?”
“No —saying fragrant prayers—-that your
thin soul might mount up”——-
“riang thin souls, Lettie! I’m not one of
your souly sort. I can’t stand pre-Raphael-
ites. *You—you’re not a Burne-Jonesess —
you’re an Albert Moore. I think there’s
more in the warm touch of a soft body than
in a prayer. I’ll pray with kisses.”
“And when you can’t?”
“I’ll wait till prayer-time again. By Jove.
I’d rather feel my arms full of you; I’d
rather touch that red mouth—you grudger!
—than sing hymns with you in any heaven.”
“I'm afraid you’ll never sing hymns with
me in Heaven.”
“Well —I have you here —ves, I have you
now.”
“Our life is but a fading dawn?"
Liar ! Y\ ell, you called me ! Besides,
I don’t care; ‘Carpe diem,’ my rosebud, my
fawn. There’s a nice Carmen about a fawn.
‘Time to leave its. mother, and venture into
a warm embrace.’ Poor old Horace —I’ve
forgotten him.”
“Then poor old Horace.”
“Ha! Ha! —-Well, I shan’t forget you.
What’s that queer look in your eyes?”
"What is it?”
“Nay—you tell me. You are such a tease,
there’s no getting to the bottom of you.”
“You can fathom the depth of a kiss”
“I will—l will”
Some time ago the State of Missouri,
U.S.A., offered $lOOO for a song. Tlie prize
has been awarded to Mrs. L. C. Hull for the
following:—
Missouri, fair, we bring to thee
Hearts full of love and loyalty;
Thou central star, thou brightest gem,
Of all the brilliant diadem—
Missouri.
Chorus :
Then lift your voice and join the throng
That swells her praise in joyful song,
Till earth and sky reverberate:
Our own, our dear, our grand old State —
Missouri.
She came, a compromise, for peace;
Her prayer is still that strife may cease;
She mourned her blue, wept o’er her gray,
When, side by side, in death they lay—
Missouri.
Nor North, nor South, nor Bast, nor
West,
But part of each —of each the best.
Come, homeless one, come to her call;
Her arms are stretched to shelter all —
Missouri.
A, Bulletin correspondent at the University
of Missouri writes that “the Australian con-
tributors' efforts were pronounced by the
committee to be of high poetical value, but
their authors lacked the knowledge of local
conditions necessary for an acceptable inter-
pretation of Missouri ideals.”
The State now offers $5OO for a musical
setting of the prize song. The composer
should send his manuscript signed by a nom
de plume only, his full name and address
to go (with the nom de plume) in a separate
envelope. The competition closes on the 30th
November next, and MSS. should be sent to
Professor W. H. Pommer, University of Mis-
souri, Columbia, Miss.
At one time Albert Dorrington counted as
an artist in letters. That was before he left
Australia. Now his brilliant future is
definitely behind him; and he is finding his
level amongst the purveyors of cheap, sensa-
tional fiction in England. They are a hard-
driven Lot, for “speeders up” from America
make the pace hotter every year. Still, some
of them turn out yarns which arrest atten-
tion by ingenious plot or coherent adven-
ture. Our Lady of the Leopards \ Sydney
Book Club) is painfully disjointed and un-
convincing. The native princess of fabu-
lous wealth, who runs a menagerie and a
monkey-god in a lonely Indian valley, aided
only by a half-caste girl and. a Chow; the
showmen’s agents who want to annex the
god; the Irishman who filled the monkey-
god’s part at another place; and good old
“Bully” Hayes might have been made believ-
able with a little care. As it is, they do
not impose upon the reader for a moment;
the jadoo business by which the princess
executes her vengeance is ridiculous, and
Our Lady of the Leopards does not rise to
the level of an average “Deadwood Dick”
yarn.
While Dorrington is degenerating, G. B.
Lancaster is improving. The crudities of
her early stories, in which a girl strained
after the manly note, had almost disappeared
in Jim of the Ranges, and there are pas-
sages in that book which throw a scene or
action before the reader’s eyes as forcibly as
any in Kipling. Unfortunately, just as Miss
Lancaster was getting into the way of writ-
ing good Australian novels, she joined the
London contingent, and started to write about
England. In The Hon. Peggy (Constable)
there are phrases of the original quality.
The heroine says, when motoring through
avenues of beeches : “I feel as if I were being
perpetually poured down a green funnel.”
But here the action is not described so
much as reproduced, and it is more subtle
work than any in her previous novels
Peggy’s stepfather, the Colonel, had charge
of two young men—one brought up in Eng-
land as a gentleman, the other taken from
the gutter, and left to rough it in Canada-
and, before disposing of his fortune, he
wished to ascertain which was the better
man. He puts them to the test in a motor
trip through England and Wales and into
Scotland, each “claimant” taking his turn as
chauffeur. Peggy is thrown into the scale
too. and a very searching test is made.
The story is a good one, but it is over-
laden with an amazing amount of guide-
book information. This may be due to the
enthusiasm of the raw colonial for the storied
dust of Britain, and the bo r 'k is dedicated
“to all who wish to know and love the ‘open
roads’ of England.”

How French Women |
Develop
fshe . . .
The absence of a full,
well-rounded bust takes
away four-fifths of a
woman s charm.
Emaciation is really one
form of disease, and the
inability to add sufficient
tissue to round out and give to the
body a symmetrical appearance
is, in most cases, evidence that
some ailment is interfering with
the digestive and assimilative
processes.
How often have you watched
the tremendous social progress of
some of your female acquaint-
ances who, though not so fortu-
nate maybe as yourself in intel-
lectual attainments, but possess-
ing charm because of an excep-
tional figure, have carried all
before them.
As the magnet draws the steel,
so the influence created by a distinctive figure
attracts man.
Let two women enter a room together, both
strangers to the assembled guests ; let one be
as pretty as a picture in the face, thin and
slight-chested ; the other full-bosomed,
•V
/
well-rounded shoulders, hips developed pro-
portionately for height, and a full, rounded
(even though plain) face. Which receives
the admiration of the gathering ? She with
the pretty figure every time ! The pretty
face is a bad second.
You are not to blame for being thin ; but
if you remain thin, scrawny and undeveloped,
the blame rests entirely on your own shoulders.
Thousands of weak, nervous, run-down,
anaemic women who, when they
came to ns, were mere shadows
of what Nature intended them
to be, are now 7 , as the result of
a few weeks’ proper guidance,
perfect examples of female devel-
opment —healthy, strong and
permanently restored to vigorous
womanhood.

you, free of any cost, how you
may develop your bust. We

VARIOVS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ballad of His Lady’s Bays.
When I slip away from these sunlit places
And joy no more in the wind and the rain ;
When my eyes are shut to the dear loved faces,
And the dark draws down on my strife and pain ;
When I’ve done with losing and done with gain,
And am lost to the comforts that now stand nearest ;
When the dust is dead in my heart and brain,
I shall still be your glory and shame, my dearest.
When I’m cold to the night’s appealing graces
And deaf to old Ocean's grave refrain ;
When I laugh no more where the gladsome chase is,
But am hidden away in the vast Inane;
When all my desires and designs prove vain
And that star sets for ever that once shone clearest,
I shall hold you fast in a deathless chain. . . .
I shall still be your glory and shame, my dearest.
AVhen Hope from her record my name effaces,
And neither my deeds nor my dreams remain,
And I’m one with the rest of the hopeless cases
That stand in men’s minds as a fading stain ;
When your chance, grudged thought is my blight
and bane
And I’m weighed by sleek Folly (that judge
severest),
You shall be meshed in with my tangled skein. . . .
I shall still be your glory and shame, my dearest.
Mistress! however you fence or feign,
You know (and admit, when your mood’s sin-
cerest)
At the last I shall hold you tight-clasped again ;
I shall still be your glory and shame, my dearest.
MAORILAND.
FRANK MORTON.
Royals Three.
From sunlit vales of Arcadee '■
(A land where Love and Joy roam free),
Among the ships that cross the sea,
A ship is sailing straight to me
Under royals three, royals three,
Stuns’ls aloft and royals three.
My ship with melody is sweet
From ratlin taut to straining sheet,
From the swinging truck to the tinkling foam
That curls like lace on the green track home.
Her holds are filled with cargo rare,
Strong antidotes for pain and care,
And liquors rich to charm the blood
Of Avarice to generous flood.
She carries no specie in servile trust,
No dole for liars, no gems for lust,
No grist for the idler’s foolish mill—
For these no fairy royals fill.
But the toiler will see his work well done,
And the lover be glad beneath the sun.
And I’ll warrant that sorrow, and hunger, and sin
Will be no more when my ship comes in
Under royals three, royals three,
Stuns’ls aloft and royals three.
From the Leeuwin will flash the welcome word
That a ship like a snow-winged homing bird
Passed east on a day of gold and blue,
With a lading of love for me and you.
Under the stars that mocked my eyes,
That strove so long to pierce the skies,
AVhen my good ship comes safe to land
I’ll take my true love by the hand.
For stanch in halyard, brace and stay,
She’ll lift the Lonsdale light some day.
And I’ll warrant that hunger, and sorrow, and sin
Will be no more when my ship comes in.
I’ll load her heavy with human woe,
And down to Hades she may go,
And among the ships that cross the sea
She never more will sail for me
Under royals three, royals three,
Stuns’ls aloft and royals three.
VICTORIA.
HENRY TATE.
The Prodigal’s Psalm.
Drink deep ! Fill again, till your woes are forgot,
My brothers in misery! Why should we live
While the fire is unquenchcd and the worm dieth not,
When liquor can always oblivion give?
The gamut of passion is held in a flask—
From the gold of desire to the purple of pain !
And fair are the Hebes whom vine-wreaths enmask.
So, courage, my comrades! Fill up once again !
We are some of us last in the races of life ;
We are derelict some, weather-beaten and tossed;
And some are God’s warriors, downed in the strife;
But most of us gambled on Woman —and lost!
We swallow our gruel, for take it we must!
“What’s the good? Nothing matters!” is all our
refrain.
If the fruit of your life Ijas a kernel of dust,
You must moisten it more! Hearties, fill up
again!
The former-day friend one may happen to meet,
Who vacantly stares upon you and your woes
In non-recognition—it isn’t too sweet,
Or easily bearable, that, Heaven knows.
Cheer up ! Human nature’s a sordid affair,
Man’s friendship’s a difficult thing to maintain;
When status is judged by the coat that you wear,
Let friendship go hang! Hearties, fill up again !
If the lady you lost to your fancy appears,
Sigh no more! Devil knows there are women
enough !
No misty-eyed Niobe, dripping with tears,
Delilah is made of superior stuff!
And why should we trouble, the hopelessly damned,
Having nothing to lose and but little to gain?
If doors of the righteous before us are slammed,
Laugh louder, my friends, as your glasses you
drain!
There are times in the night when the curtain is
raised,
A flash of “what might have been” swiftly shot
back;
But lasses we loved and the liquors we praised
Have led us too far on the well-trodden track.
We know that it leads to the Land of the Lost;
We know it is littered with legions of slain!
Fill it up ! Fill again ! Never reckon the cost!
To oblivion drink! And then fill up again!
N.s.w.
JOHN WALTERS.

AFTER THE BATTLE.
In two N.S.W. by-elections the “Liberals” gained one seat by three votes (a recount is possible) and Labor held the other by a good majority.
Neither side feels cheerful; but a doubtful Independent or two may throw in with the Labor party and enable it to carry on.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves ) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Bditor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-

dential.

cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

AUGUST 24, 1911.
ENGLAND’S GREAT PERIL.
At the time of writing England is in the
act of emerging from the throes of
something that resembled civil war.
Blood has been freely shed, and men have
been slain in this revolt of (he working
classes against the “established” industrial
order. Various explanations have been of-
fered of the appalling and sudden outbreak.
The splendor of the Coronation ceremonies
may have had something to do with it. The
bald newspaper intimation that this senseless
mummery was responsible, one way and an-
other, for the expenditure of 40 millions
sterling (the precise sum, by the way, that
was lavished on Versailles, the monument
of royal extravagance which did so much to
expedite the French Revolution) would have
conveyed little to the poverty-stricken sec-
tion of Britain, had the crowd not been
forced to watch the vast sum being wasted,
as it were. For a little while, the people
who have everything in the world to make
life delightful paraded in view of the
millions whose existence is a ceaseless
struggle to sidestep hunger. The present-
ment of that gorgeous object-lesson in luxury
was surely bad tactics, from an administra-
tive standpoint. For years the underpaid
English worker and the famished out-of-
work have been accustomed to growl at the
disparity between their bleak lot, and that of
the nearest millionaire; but they went no
further. The British plutocrat, take him
for all in all, is about the most satisfactory
of his class extant. Possessed of a good
deal of public spirit, he does not, like the
Frenchmen of the old regime, boycott the
naval and military services of Ins native land,
and decline, to take a hand in its govern-
ment : lie shuns the sensational extrava-
gances of the rich American; he is less arro-
gant and snobbish than the German; and
he is more moral —in the sense that he takes
more pains to hide his immoralities —than
any of them. Because the “haves” in Eng-
land have forborne for many years past from
publicly obtruding their good fortune, and
because, further, they have mostly made
some effort, or pretence at an effort, to jus-
tify their existence by work, the “have nots”
have patiently endured them. Last month’s
carnival was a radical departure from the
rule of “good form” laid down by the im-
mortal Brummell, imposing on the well-bred
Briton the duty of making no vulgar display.
It must have driven home to the intelligence
of the dullest and least imaginative pauper
that its participants have not merely more,
but many thousand times more, than their
fair share. The presence of Andrew Fisher
would not have soothed the envious starv-
lings. In him the English working classes
saw a Prime Minister who had left Scotland
a pit-boy, and it is certain that they re-
marked that no such opportunities as he and
his colleagues had enjoyed lay in their way.
And then there is the educational factor.
The more Britain cultivates its masses, the
more it enlightens them on the grotesque
inequity of an arrangement whereunder 100
citizens spend their lives in penury, in order
that one man may amass more than he can
ever enjoy.
But all these things were necessarily mere
incidentals when weighed against the master-
fact that 25 per cent, of the nation is. star-
ving, while another 25 per cent, is so
hideously poor that life has no allurements to
offer them. Famine and dull, drab misery
have caused most of the popular revolts in
the world’s history, and there is enough of
both of them in England to account for the
horrors of the past week, without any addi-
tional factors. Freetrade politicians come
back to Australia, after a month or so spent
in the West End of London, and talk en-
thusiastically—as William McMillan, of
Sydney, did the other day—about “England,
the sun of the Empire’s firmament”; and
the humbug is cheered by the vacuous local
folks who make a hobby of Empire-leaguing.
A thinker of the McLinen type his
optimism on the fact that the United King-
dom is still in a large way of business as a
money-lender and investor. He does not
mention the “hunger-marchers” who have
taken to swooping down on the great cities ;
the tramps who have started to terrorise the
country parts; the city parks dotted with
human ruins ; and the crowded hovels, reek-
ing with dirt and squalor and disease, that
infest the pauper quarters of every city and
town. And, above all, the Freetrade apolo-
gist does not touch on the extraordinary
discontent which affects the classes that have
the luck to be in work. An English Labor
paper—and, strangely enough, it was a Free-
trade paper—advertised the fact, the other
day, that the average wage earned by arti-
sans in the United Kingdom amounted to
17s. 6d. per week. There must be
some plain reason why the British oper-
ative, once the most reliable and skilful
on earth, should be immeasurably worse paid
in his own country to-day than the same
class of worker is in Germany or France, not
to mention America and Australia. If he
has not grossly deteriorated it can only be
that his country’s industries can no longer
afford to pay him according to his deserts.
The dreary truth appears to be that the
British ivorker is not the man he was, nor
are British industries the live and effective
concerns they were; and both calamities are
due to the Freetrade insanity. British manu-
facturers might have little to fear from their
foreign rivals, given a fair deal. But, under a
system whereby the foreigner has his own
market reserved absolutely to himself, and,
in addition, has the British market as a rub-
bish-tip for his surplus products, they do not
get a fair deal, or anything resembling one.
The consumer in the United Kingdom has
for years been turning down locally-made
goods in favor of cheap imports. One effect
has been to vitiate a fourth of the nation.
There is nothing like enforced idleness to
sap decent men’s energy and vitality, and
transform them into wasters. The would-be
worker who finds himself persistently unem-
ployed, degenerates swiftly into an unem-
ployable, a class of which Britain already
holds a record number. Under the fiscal
system to which the Old Country clings, a
vicious circle is constantly being completed :
the factories, owing to foreign competition,
are employing fewer and fewer hands, and
the army of hopeless out-of-works is thereby
automatically swelled. A sort of side-tragedy
is that, while the Briton earns less than
ever he did, the mostly Trust-produced com-
modities from abroad, on which he has learned
to depend for sustenance, are increasing in
price. These are troubles calculated to
drive to revolt even those workers who are
getting what used to be considered good
wages.
* * * *
The peril that menaces Britain is hideously
obvious. It has delayed protecting its in-
dustries for so long that now it may be too
late. The sudden damming, by means of a
tariff, of the vast river of imports which
flows into the United Kingdom would prob-
ably mean national starvation. The work-
ing man, if his own fellow-countrymen are
to be believed, has so degenerated that it is
questionable if he could do the good work
he once did, even if Protection gave him the
chance. Also, the British capitalist has got
so used to investing his money in Australia,
the Argentine, Canada, the European Conti-
nent—anywhere rather than in England—•
that it is doubtful-if he would readily sub-
sidise home industries. Admitting that
Britain’s one hone —whichever way its
present trouble ends —of averting a revolu-
tion lies in providing employment at reason-
able wages for its out-of-works, its prospects
of salvation appear to rest on the construc-
tion of a bastard sort of Protective tariff
which will stimulate certain industries, while,
at the same time, it admits foodstuffs free.
’Phis would help the employers to pay the
living wages which, under Freetrade condi-
tions, they are unable to pay, though they
had all the good will in the world ; and a
living wage and plenty of it appears to be
Britain’s crying need just now. Further-
more, the outrageously rich —the West-
minsters, Portlands, and so forth, whose
incomes from rents alone rijn into hundreds
of thousands yearly —will doubtless have to
be taxed with a hand of iron. But these
things are all in the air. The pre-
sent dreadful positiou is that Britain
(even assuming that last week’s revolt
is effectually scotched) is in grave dan-
ger from writhin ; and at the same time Ger-
many, with a sizeable fleet, and a trained
army two millions strong is being as un-
pleasant as It knows how. One great naval
battle might cause the world’s greatest Em-
pire to crumble to pieces. It is a cheerless
thought for Australia, which has delayed de-
fending itself for a century, on the ground
that England was impregnable.
THE REFERENDA VOTE CONTINUES TO
COME HOME TO ROOST.
Towards the end of the Referenda campaign
Melbourne Age, which had shown itself a
strenuous pro-Trust advocate all through
the piece, wrote as follows: —
The only point at issue, says Mr. Hughes, is the
overthrow of the power of the Trusts, “which rule
here as they do in America.’’ This has been the cry
of the Labor party all through the campaign. It is
a hollow sham. We have no Trusts as in America.
And, about the same time, Sydney Tele-
graph cried indignantly:—
“Are the people to rule themselves?” That is the
fundamental issue. Let us see that it is not obscured
by handfuls of dust about Trusts and monopolies
that do not exist, and that show no sign of existing.
The electors appear to have believed these
mendacious statements, which were made
with great vehemence in every anti-Labor
newspaper, club and centre in the Common-
wealth. It is only last April that Australia
voted the way the local combines wished, and
already every household is feeling the effects.
Trusts, modelled slavishly on U.S. lines,
have sprung up everywhere, and those which
were already established have grown more
insolent than ever in their rapacity. It is
significant that the papers which a brief
four months ago argued in the name of
State Bights that the anti-Trust cry was a
sham, are, to-day, bitterest in their com-
plaints concerning the prevalence of Trusts
in Australia, and their peculiarly Yankee
methods of behavior. Thus the Age
(9/8/T1) wrote as follows :
We need, for our own consumption in Australia,
about 220,000 tons of sugar. We have not as yet
overtaken that amount of production, because of the
monopoly which bestrides the growers like a colossus,
cramping and crippling their operations. We im-
ported in 1909 about 99,000 tons of sugar, of the
value of £1,100,000. All that should have been
grown by ourselves, and it will be so as soon as the
industry can get released from the shackles under
which it struggles.
The same paper in the same issue had a
three-quarter column wail on the subject
of the “fruit monopoly,” which has secured,
in advance, “almost the whole of the ship-
ping space available for the carriage of Aus-
tralian fruit to Europe during the coming
season.” According to the paper which, last
April, described the Trust menace as “a
hollow sham” :
Fruit exporters will, in consequence of the forma-
tion of the Fruit Combine, be obliged to pay toll .
to this group in order to procure accommodation.
And it is not only in 1912 that the industry is to
be thus hampered. The shipping companies have
frankly notified the Minister that they intend to
continue the practice indefinitely of letting their
space to the ring, and they have positively declined
to do business with the Government.
Attempts were made for years in N. S.
Wales to form a timber ring. They all
failed till the other day, when the people’s
voice declared in favor of combines, so to
speak. Now, according to a recent issue of
the S. M. Herald:—
A close combination in the timber trade is prac-
tically assured.
The lines upon which the projected agreement will
run are the division of the trade among the mem-
bers of the combination, and the provision of penal-
ties for anv infringement of the agreement. Each
member will be allotted a certain proportion of the
trade, and it is expected that he will not exceed his
allotment. Should, however, it happen that he ex-
ceeds the limit awarded him, a penalty will be im-
posed upon him in proportion to the amount of his
excess business, and from the fund created by such
penalties recompense will be paid "to members whose
business does not reach their allotted proportions.
It is hoped by the negotiating parties that the
agreement will be concluded in time to put it into-
force by the beginning of May, and should these ex-
pectations' be fulfilled, retail prices of timber will
be raised by 10 to 20 per cent.
Fish rings exist in both N. S. Wales and
Victoria, a fact that, explains why fish, that
in this country, with its huge coast line and
its teeming waters, should be dirt cheap, is
actually available only as food for the well-
to-do. Victorian Minister for Agriculture
Graham remarked the other day :
The public has to pay very high prices for fish,
and yet the fishermen receive very little for their
hauls. I want to get at the bottom of the trouble,
and then try to remedy it. If the municipalities
will take over the control of the fish supply we will
be only too glad to let them have it. But the un-
satisfactory conditions of the past should not be
allowed to continue.
The mere fact that fishermen sometimes throw
away their hauls because they cannot get fair prices
for them, while fish continues dear in Melbourne,
is enough to show that a change in the existing
system is badly needed. So much fish was thrown
away in the bay in this way that a serious nuisance
was caused by dead fish being washed up on the
foreshores. The authorities actually had to inter-
vene and stop the practice. Now fishermen have to
go outside the Heads if they want to throw their
fish away.
N. S. Wales, with equal advantages, is
up against a similar trouble. Also there is
a Meat Trust, a Shinping Trust-, a Brick
Trust a Tobacco Trust, a Butter-Box
Trust, and various other organisations of
the kind, and their number is increasing
steadily. # #
Not even the folks who fought hardest for
a “No” vote on the Referenda questions
have the nerve to deny that the tendency is
inimical to the public welfare. As a result of
the Sugar monopoly’s arbitrary conduct in
the matter of price-raising (in the middle of
the recent strike it hoisted its charges for
raw and refined sugar by 20s. per ton —the
second advance within a fortnight), the man
in the street is called on to pay more, not
only for his household sugar, but for jams,
pickles, cakes, sweetstuffs and a thousand
other commodities. Brick and timber combines
have had as much to do with the poor man’s
difficulty in getting a dwelling of moder-
ate rental as the land boom has had. Bread
is dearer than it was, not because of Protec-
tion, nor yet because local crops have failed,
but owing to the fact that those in the busi-
ness have arrived at “honorable understand-
ings” to keep the nrice of this necessary
high. There is hardly any article of every-
day use that lias not gone up 20 or 30 per-
cent. in cost, owing chiefly to the machina-
tions of rings. Australia was as cheap a
place for the poor and “middle-class” man
to live in as Germany or France five years
ago; to-day it is almost as expensive for
these classes as America. And the fiercest
Tories are beginning to recognise that the
cause of the trouble is to be found in the
pervasion of the monopoly—that curse which
Lord Coke denounced three centuries back
as operating infallibly to “raise prices while
the commodity is not so good as before.”
Two reform-suggestions have come from
the political party which defeated the Fed-
eral Government’s proposals for dealing with
Australia’s Trusts. Sydney Telegraph.
being a severely anti-Socialistic sheet, voiced
the opinions of those who are opposed, at
any price, to State interference with pri-
vate enterprise, when it urged the public to
end the depredations of its gas, sugar aud
other vends by getting control of these con-
cerns in the ordinary way. Said the Tele-
graph blandly: “It is now open to anyone
to go into the market, and at the publicly
quoted price, buy shares.” Regarded as a so-
lution of the Trust trouble here, it is about
as satisfactory as,if the U.S. Government, in-
stead of prosecuting the Standard Oil Co..
counselled individual citizens of America to
go on Wall-street to try to buy out Rocke-
feller. Melbourne Age, throwing anti-
Socialism to the winds, advocates the for-
mation of Commonwealth Trusts as a means
of staying the voracious private enterprise
vends which are now robbing the Australian
consumer. In particular, that newspaper,
which last April did not believe that there
were any injurious Trusts in Australia,
called wildly last week for “the immediate
establishment of a national refinery as the
best method of opposition to the sugar mono-
poly.” That monopoly is a vast concern,
and one that will die hard. To establish a
Socialistic rival capable of pusting it from
its place of power would mean the expendi-
ture of a couple of millions. That expendi-
ture is quite unnecessary; and, anyhow,
under the Constitution, the Federal Govern-
ment has no power to go into the busi-
ness. The advocates of a Commonwealth re-
finery base their arguments as to the prac-
ticability of the scheme, on the enabling sec-
tion of the Constitution, which says: “The
Parliament shall, subject to this Constitu-
tion. have power to make laws for the peace,
order and good government of the Common-
wealth with resoect to trade and commerce
with other countries and among the States.”
This signifies, according to the Age, that the
Commonwealth Government may “for the
peace, order and good government of the
Commonwealth,” enter into business, at its
own discretion, as a sugar-refiner, and pre-
sumably also as a fish merchant, baker, tim-
ber-seller or what not. The absurdity of the
contention is as obvious as sin. The essence
of a Federal Constitution is that it allows
the national Government certain carefully
delimited powers, and reserves the residuum
to the States. “Peace, order and good gov-
ernment” are the admirable ideals which
every Administration is counselled to strive
after, and that is all there is to this section.
Andrew Fisher and his colleagues have no
more power under the Constitution, as it
stands, to launch out as sugar-refiners for
the- purpose of scotching private enterprise
concerns, however malign, than they have to
start Federal gaols on the plea that, in their
view, the State institutions are being in-
competently run. In any case, the appear-
ance of the Commonwealth in the field, in
opposition to each injurious Trust that man-
ifested itself would, in this couptry—where,
by the present look of things, there are soon
going to be hundreds of vends and combines
—be the costliest and insanest method o f
dealing with the evil. The cheapest and
most obvious method, on the other hand, is
that which the Federal Government pro-
posed last April, namely, that the Aus-
tralian Parliament should be empowered to
diagnose an injurious Trust on sight, re-
sume it at a fair price and run it for the
public benefit. Incidentally, this' is the only
possible means of ending the system _of
licensed robbery which is now scandalising
even the most Tory section of the com-
rrmnitv.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Blue Ruin that Refuses to Arrive.

The Sugar Strike—and After.
The movement toward the closer union of
trades and labor unions in Australia got a
long lift from the sugar workers’ strike in
Queensland. Last sugar season something
over 40 petty strikes occurred in the indus-
try, most of them engineered by separate
sectional or district unions. Many of those
strikes were failures, or, at best, brought
only local and temporary relief. As a mat-
ter of fact, the workers were almost entirely
at the mercy of unscrupulous bosses. The
agreements under which the men worked
were relics of the bad, old, black-labor days,
and were manacles, legirons and a whole
armory of fetters. In many instances, these
agreements, besides stipulating for from 60
to w'ell over 72 hours’ work at 225. 6d. a
week, empow'ered the bosses to hold 25 per
cent, of the men’s wages. If the men worked
right on to the end of the season without
resenting the open insults of bosses, and
without resorting to bad language about
food, accommodation or sanitary arrange-
ments, they were rewarded with what wms
referred to in lordly magnificence as a
bonus, the said bonus being their ow T n 25
per cent, of withheld wages. If, however,
any sugar-worker uprose and held a bit of
tainted meat suggestively beneath the boss’s
nose, or in any way sought to assert that right
of the individual which is so dear to the heart
of the fat G.S.R. dividend-drawers, he got
the sack, and the employer got the 25 per
cent, of his wages. Those agreements cov-
ered about as cruel a system of legal wage-
slavery and wage-robbery as could be con-
ceived ; yet the men, broken up into district
and sectional unions, were powerless to re-
fuse to sign them. At Townsville last De-
cember, however, the sugar-unionists were
seized with a rush of brains to the head.
They dropped their petty little penny-ha’-
penny shows, and combined under the banner
of the Amalgamated Workers’ Association.
Then they saw something of their power.
They asked the sugar-bosses to confer with
them as to future 'labor conditions in the
field and mills —a clean slate to be used. The
bosses, remembering the little unions of a
day or two before, laughed (the C.S.R. Co.
sneered) and refused. Then the men wrote
several demands on the slate, asked again
and got another refusal. When the season
opened the men declined to work under the
Md agreements: and so the trouble began.
At first the strike was looked upon as cer-
tain of the doom of the just-broken Brisbane
gas strike. But at the back of the gas-
stokers had been an unwieldy, loose-jointed
trades and Labor Council, which at the
first strong pressure crumpled up. Behind
the sugar-workers, on the other hand, was
ona bier, solid union of unions: and as the
tight continued it gathered strength. The
AAV .A. commanded the respect of the other
big unions, whicn were drawn into the
trouble; and some of these bodies—the big
transport unions of Australia —seeing that
there was no escape, “congressed” to save
themselves, with the result that they also
§aved the sugar-workers. But there was
another, and very important, outcome of the
congress. The transport workers, them-
selves at peace witn their employers, earn-
ing satisfactory wages and enjoying fair
laoor conditions, saw that they were in
danger of being dragged into other indus-
irail turmoils; and they, too, were seized
with a rush of brains to the head. “If we're
to be dragged into rows,” they said, “we
piean to have a word or twm to say in the pre-
liminary stages of the rows.” Their proposal,
therefore, now is to form a kind of strike
council, which shall have power to deal with
all future strike-proposals by affiliated bodies
throughout Australia. The establishment
of this council will have the effect of stop-
ping much reckless striking, of arranging
fair terms with employers, in minor matters,
and of assuring for every just cause the sup-
port of the whole of the affiliated unions.
But it is only one step in the right direc-
tion. If the A.W.U., the A.W.A. and the
Rural Workers’ Association were, drawn
together into one great industrial union, with
fhe support of the transport workers, also
united in one organisation, the condition of
labor in the primary-producing industries of
Australia could be fixed on a fair basis, and
almost for a certainty without any strike-
fighting—an immense saving to the workers
and to the public. And the same thing is
applicable to all other industries. The ulti-
mate object is, of course, to settle these dis-
putes by arbitration; but it is certain that
arbitration is least likely to fail when the
parties are more or less evenly matched.
When one party feels that it could lick the
other party hollow in an appeal to force,
while it might itself be licked hollow in an
appeal to reason, it will naturally dodge
the appeal to reason with all the tricks it
knows.

The Babu Spirit in Australia.
Melbourne University has chosen its new
Professor of English, and as this paper fore-
casted a month ago, he is a foreign pro-
duct. Walter Murdoch, an Australian
and an M.A. of Melbourne, has, as lecturer
in English literature, given eight of the best
years of his life to his University. His
capacity has never been questioned. By all
the laws of decency, and fair-dealing,, he
should, in the circumstances, have been given
the preference over an outsider, assuming
the merits of the two men were anywhere
near equal. The Australian and the gentle-
man from Aberdeen who has supplanted
him, are, in fact, so much on a par, in the
opinion of unbiassed judges, that the Eng-
lish committee which met in London to
consider the new appointment bracketed
them equal, out of some hundreds of appli-
cants. Thus the University Council, by its
action, says, in effect, that it will not con-
sider an Australian where there is an
equally capable man offering on the other
side of the earth; it announces to the
world that, in its opinion, the product of
Melbourne University is inferior to the pro-
duct of a fifth-rate British University; in
short, it behaves in the Babu-like fashion of
the Tory official from one end of the Com-
monwealth to the other. The province, of a
University is not merely to teach. It is ex-
pected to do something in the way of form-
ing and strengthening its members’ charac-
ters. And particularly in Australia, which
is just learning to walk in a national man-
ner of speaking,' nothing is needed so much
as that the young men should have instilled
into them a proper pride of race and coun-
try. Hence the last thing Adstralia should
tolerate from its Parliaments and Councils
is the belittling of provedly-capable local
men for the glory and benefit of total
stfangers. Among the well-kicked or sub-
ject races the tendency w'hich has just
found expression in Melbourne is rife the
world over ; the native of Ceylon has grov-
elled to his oversea rulers for so long now
that he would not be happy if he were
asked to govern himself, and the Bengalee
mostly treats the imported sahib like a god.
But Australia is no place for such abase-
ment ; and the sooner such deplorably bad
patriots as the members of the Melbourne
University Council are apprised of the fact
the better for this country’s reputation and
self-respect. So far as the Chair of Litera-
ture is concerned, the damage is done; but
at the end of the year the Chairs of Philoso-
phy and Agriculture will have to be filled.
If, as is already freely prophesied in Vic-
toria, the Council sends to a remote part of
the British Isles for these professors also,
the public, which pays Melbourne Univer-
sity £21,000 per annum, should push a
vigorous hand into the scandal.

The Defence Department.
The infrequent Australian warrior who
happens to be sufficiently grown-up to un-
derstand such things, is smiling hard at a
sort of tragi-comedy that is being presented
by an all-military caste in the Common-
wealth theatre. Spectators should know,
in order to get a proper understanding of
the plot, (1) that Senator Pearce, Minister
for Defence, is, at the time of writing,
somewhere off the northern coast of Aus-
tralia, on his way home from the Corona-
tion; (2) that Major-General Hoad, Chief
of the General Staff, is so seriously ill that
his retirement into private life is imminent;
and (3) that Colonel Bridges, the recently-
appointed Head of the Military College at
Duntroon, sees no valid reason why he
shouldn’t succeed to Hoad’s job, if Hoad
should not be able to keep it. Colonel
Bridges and Colonel Foster, R.E. (lec-
turer on Military Science at Sydney Uni-
versity), are friends; in fact, it was mainly
owing to the former that the Engineer-
Colonel came to this country. They are
both up north, at present: and unless the
local soldier, diagnoses the situation .with
lamentable inaccuracy, their object is to
head off the Minister and arrange (a) that
Colonel Bridges shall step into General
Hoad’s shoes, when they become vacant;
and (ft) that Colonel Foster shall simul-
taneously proceed to mononolise the foot-
wear of Colonel Bridges. It is against the
latter contingency that The Bulletin de-
sires to lodge a protest. Colonel Foster is,
no doubt, a brainy and knowledgable sol-
dier. But he is as English as they make
theni. and devoid of any experience in the
training .of Australian boys or men. He has
never seen.active service, and hence lacks one
of the most necessary qualifications for the
head of a military school—namely, the capac-
ity to talk to the students in the art of war
from personal experience. Above all, he is,
and always has been, out of sympathy with
the compulsory service idea on which Dun-
troon stands broad-based. The theory of
the fighting game can be inculcated by any
pedant who has had access to books and
sufficient industry to read up their contents.
The practice of it can only be taught by one
who has known the real thing. The ideal
head for the college at Duntroon would be
an Australian who had gained his know-
ledge of warfare, not merely from the text-
books, but on the tented field. There are
plenty offering, and if Rumor proves truth-
ful, and the present Director of the Dun-
troon establishment abandons his job, one
of them should be given a show'.
The Sacrifice at Homebush.
“Wonga,” on a piece of -wanton cruelty: —
Disrespectful though it was to Mr. Johnson
(imp.), the Public Works Committee fell out of
bed early the other morning, and wandered out to
see what was doing at the Homebush (Sydney)
Saleyards. They discovered quite a lot of things
calculated to cause Paterfamilias, as he read of them,
to turn aside from his succulent steak and juicy
chop. Crippled and dead sh'eep appeared to fill
much of:the landscape, and crowded pens and what-
not seemed to call Johnson a liar when he said there
was plenty of accommodation at the yards, if the
thing was managed properly. Still the im-
ported Czar wasn’t far out when, no doubt
at the instigation of some unacknowledged
understrapper, he said that the deaths in
the trucks were due mostly to owners overcrowding
their stock. Owners overcrowd all tight—jamb
them in so tight that, when they get down—well,
they stop down. And Johnson's trains are now so
rottenly slow that the stock have plenty of oppor-
tunity to get down —and stay down.
Of course it is cruel; damnably, wickedly, un-
necessarily and foolishly cruel! But there is nothing
new about it. The whole business is the sacrifice to
two gods that /the average citizen of N. S. Wales has
long worshipped—the Greatness of Sydney and Cheap-
ness. Shoving everything into Sydney—people and
the stuff to feed the people—meant a jamb some-
where, and the stock have got part of the jamb, to
the eternal glory of Sydney. They could be com-
fortably killed up country, and the carcases brought
down sweet and fresh for Sydney to eat, in refriger-
ated cars. But that wouldn’t have suited Fat in
Sydney, so a whole lot of country meatworks were
closed up for years, until the rabbits gave them a
job. And, as far as the cruelty part of the business
is concerned, it simply arose from the old-time gospel
of “cheapness”—shove as many sheep into a truck as
will go in! Owners haven’t altogether got used to
the fact that sheep are too valuable now to treat
like that.
Anyhow, why this surprise at the cruelty? Aus-
tralia is a cruel country. Anyone who knows it
from the inside knows that, and the old regime
didn’t better things. Perhaps the new will. But
Nature herself is cruel in Australia, and men who
have spent months pulling half-dead and whole-dead
sheep out of mudholes, in order to save their pelts;
have gone round on a frosty morning cutting the
throats of sheep and lambs after the crows have got
their eyes out; have seen the paddocks full of blinded
sheep, hopping about with their hoofs scorched off
by bush fire; have burnt hundreds of anthrax-rotten
carcases; have heard the endless moan of mobs of
dying cattle smiting the cloudless sky; have spent
days marking and footrotting, castrating and brand-
ing; and all the time have been riding over dying
and dead rabbits, and inhaling the stench of thou-
sands of extra-dead ones —men who have gone
through all this, or most of it.' are not going to let
the bumping of a few thirsty . sheep spoil their sleep
at night. Stockraising in Australia, especially under
its unsettled, undeveloped conditions, was one of
the cruellest callings under the sun. But that did
not interfere with the Sydney folk eating their steak
and chop in comfort.
There is no reason, though, why the bloody his-
tory of the past should run op for ever. Thanks to
the progressive land tax and a few other “SociaTstic”
contrivances, the country stands a chance of being
properly settled. Given settlement. and decentralisa-
tion, stock-raising can be carried on under humane
conditions, worthy of a civilised communifv. It is
jogging that way now, and the promise is great.
But red-blooded men don’t drop the habits of a life-
time in a moment, and, after all. we want their
blood to run red yet awhile. Still, we have just
about got rid of the old gang that shot down the
blacks when they wanted some sport, on the plea
that Binghi had burnt a bit of grass or killed a sheep
or two. The smell of that decade is still hanging
about the horizon. We have now with us the second
generation that over-stocked and stock-murdered :
that poisoned waterholes to clear the rabbits, and
go denuded the country of its natural game; that
spoiled half the country in their greed, and were gen-
erally “grand and heroic pioneers.”
They are going, but they haven’t gone. • The
slaughter at Homebush is but the unburnt offering to
their god—Greed ! But it happens where the city
can’t help noticing it —and so there is a cry of
horror ! Well, what are those who are horrified going
do about it? Are they going to kick out the poli-
ticians who want to drag everything to' Sydney, who
have blocked the opening of outports, who. have
aimed and schemed to have all the killing, canning
and freezing done, not in the country, but by their
own back door? If not, they are mere humbugs and
hypocrites.
The Age declares for a municipal re-
volution at the Melbourne civic elections
that are to happen this week. It alleges
that the Yarra village lags hopelessly behind
the times municipally, and cries out for an
infusion of new blood “into the lack-lustre
and senile organism which has so long and
so stubbornly ground down all reformative
and progressive proposals.” meaning the
present hidebound city and suburban coun-
cils in the Greater Melbourne area —coun-
cils nominated by property and controlled by
vested interests. The Age rails at its old
enemy, the gas supply, “a private monopoly
which fleeces the ratepayers to the limits of
endurance” ; it rages at the “inadequate and
over-costly electric supply” ; it calls in a
loud voice for markets and r. better admin-
istration of public health. It declares war
on the “old gang,” and calls for their ex-
tinction. It crumples up the fabric of their
dignity, spits upon their robes, defiles with
pitch their wigs, and puts hemlock in their
turtle soup bv referring in withering irony
to their “nreference for dignified repose, and
such placid, pompous and pleasing relaxa-
tions from indolence as are provided by occa-
sional opportunities which come to them
from time to time to frame and present loyal
addresses to an arriving or departing Gover-
nor.” But it hits the old regime hardest
amidships when it lays at the aldermanic
door this charge :
They oppose the fine ideal of a Greater Melbourne
as a matter of instinct and self-interest. It is odious
to them because they know that it' would sweep
many redundant councils out of existence, diminish
the number of councillors, and compel all municipal
representatives of the people to work instead of
slumbering in the service of the people. , To the
type of men we are considering, economy, efficiency 1
and activity ’are ugly bugbears. They shudder at.
the notion of the enlarged responsibilities which
public opinion demands that municipalism should
undertake. (
Yet this Age, which crows, so admirably for
a policy of whole-hog municipal Socialism.as
applied to its own particular little dunghill,
is the Age which shrieked so wildly abofft
the horrors of Socialism when the Federal
control of monopolies was proposed!

One shrieking feature of the N. S. Walds
by-elections was the free expenditure- on
halls, bands, printing, advertising and other
means to influence votes. So far, the re-
ports do not record the musical entertain-
ments and free feeds that disgraced the last
Federal elections; but the overbidding for
bands and the monopolisation of halls and
the wholesale paid organisation and can-
vassing are bad enough. That both parties
are equally deep in the mire does not make
such doings less a form of political corrup-
tion. If this kind of thing is to go on. then
it will be farewell to democratic government
—-elections will be won, as the Referenda
fight was, by the party with the heaviest
purse: and heavy purses do not fight on the
side of Democracy.

“The Old Soldier” : The first police-court
case against a young mutineer, who wouldn't
“eyes front” when his commanding officer
told him to. has ended at the Glebe
(N.S.W.) in a £2 fine. It was a bad case,
as; the young rascal used some shocking
language. But the whole business is bad,
this of trying to shove the entire Defence
preparation on to infants. Most of the
youngsters of from 14 to 16 have had a hard
day at the shop or the school, and the extra
work involved in a couple of hours’ hard
training may have serious results on imma-
ture bodies. The rotten system, or no-
system, of trying to throw the burden on the
children cannot last, and will before long
excite a spirit of resistance that cannot fail
to be dangerous, and will certainly quench
all enthusiasm in the cause of real defence.
“Dxjn Brown”: Before any more ocean
mail contracts are renewed, it would be to
the advantage of this continent if politicians
became better acquainted with the possible
routes to Europe. The Suez Canal route,
which is horribly expensive, isn’t the only
one. To illustrate how the canal dues hit
the shipowner, and consequently the ship-
owner’s clients, take the case of a steamer
trading between Bombay and the U.K. —a
steamer I was interested in. She was 3250
tons gross. Canal dues amounted to over £950
each way, and, with light, harbor and other
charges added, every passage through the
canal cost well on to £llOO. This sum had
to be paid in cash either at Port Said or
Suez by the owners’ agent, w T ho then drew
upon the owners, of course collecting com-
mission for the advance. Such heavy char-
ges ought of themselves to set us consider-
ing whether we must go that way. And
there is no “must” about it. The Cape route
(32,207 miles from London to Adelaide) is
quite as economical. A vessel averaging IS
knots would accomplish the distance in 284
days. One averaging 20 knots would do it
in three days less. On other grounds the
Cape route demands signal preference. It
is an ocean route with no canal and land-
locked seas to cause trouble, in the event of
European complications; and it has the
complete approval of naval and military
authorities, who perceive its enormous • ad-
vantages in war time.

“N.Z.” LOOKS in again:—
I want to contradict The Bulletin,' which contra-
dicted me when I contradicted it syme time ago on
the position of Labor in Maoriland: I stated then
that Labor in M.L. had already started in on ah in-
dependent Labor policy, and was going to score.
Since then Labor has elected about 20 pledged men
to municipal councils, and four to Harbor Boards,
and has got about another dozen ready for the gen-
eral elections. The Trades Councils have further
engaged Walter Thomas Mills, of Milwaukee, for
six months to push a Federation scheme, whereby
- all wages men will be classed into 12 groups, the
whole to be used as a machine for capturing , the
Government. The scheme is (leaving details out' of con-
sideration) one of the most complete ever formulated,
and is catching on like wildfire.. Only the most
revolutionary of the red-raggers are standing out,
and they stand out. in sheer cussedness. Mills ex-
presses himself as highly satisfied, and says 25 years
would be the minimum time required to accomplish
as much elsewhere. Tlip prospects of getting a
number of sitting M’s.H.II. to endorse the nropo-v
tion are also distinctly good, and, generally, the
future of Labor in M.L. is bright.
To which The Bulletin remarks once
more that it will be extremely gratified to
Kids.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hi, there, Tommy, get your rifle! Yes, we
know you’re small a trifle,
But you've got to leave your marbles, and
must learn to liump a gun.
Whilst your Dad is at the races, backing
"stiffened” mokes for places,
You must learn to be a soldier—Shoulder
arms! Step lively! ’Shun!
Brother Bill is playing cricket; whilst he’s
busy at the wicket,
Whilst he’s fooling round in flannels, you
must learn to be a Man ;
You’re Australia’s sole defender, though
you're weak and mighty tender,
You must hump Australia’s burden —that’s
this nation’s splendid plan!
Uncle Jim is pigeon-shooting, brother Jack
is football-rooting—
Hear the cheering and the jeering, whilst
you learn your bit of drill.
There's a row that calls for hoeing ; whilst
the “half-time” beers are flowing,
You are “forming fours” and marching—
hear the umpire’s whistle shrill!
Brother Bill is bravely batting, making
runs along the matting;
Dad is cursing mokes and bookies, tearing
tickets as he goes ;
Whilst the slaughtered pigeons flutter o’er
the jerking trap and shutter —
You £(lone are getting ready for To-morrow’s
Certain foes!
Bill the mighty, Bill the hero, seems to
me to slump to zero —
You're the only Man Who Matters, though
you’re very small and young;
In these days when peace grows brittle,
march the solders extra-little—
And I reckon brother William might as
well be drowned or hung!
Uncle Jim the pigeon-shooter, brother Jack
the football-rooter —
They’re a pair of service-dodgers, ne’er a
drill prescribed for them;
Also, Dad, the pony-backer, he’s a useless
sort of slacker,
You're the only White Australian on the
nation’s job pro tern.!
All the rest are dodging service; very strong
to me their nerve is—
“ Sports” are they, whose frenzied worship
hath no sacramental frill;
Ilorse and football, dog and cricket —yea,
their altar is the wicket,
And a special sort of halo floats above the
sainted Bill !
Whilst the manhood of the nation yells its
wrath or approbation,
Whilst the umpire streaks for shelter, only
Tommy humps his gun ;
Bill and Jack nave got no rifles, can’t be
bothered with such trifles—
So the school-kid serves Australia whilst the
sands eternal run !
Yea, the cheerful kid goes tramping whilst
the football herds are stamping—
He is marching back and forwards whilst
his elder brethren play ;
Whilst the kid is marching slicker, Uncle
James, the pigeon-sticker,
Murders poultry like a Christian every
blessed Saturday !
Dad likewise pursues the ponies—very sad
his vesper groan is,
“Mever backed a blasted winner,” and his
cash supports the Yids ;
Whilst the football fools are braying, seems
to me Australia’s saying:
".Since my manhood’s cheap and worthless,
i must put my trust in kids!”
VICTORIA.
GRANT HERVEY.
At Last!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
For ages gone the sweated slaves
Of Britain’s wealth-production
Have sung “Britannia Rules the Waves,”
And rarely raised a ruction;
Scant food they earned in former years,
Whilst yet the country grew it;
Lean workers sweated bitter tears —
They might have struck, restrained by
fears,
They never rose to do it.
Then Freetrade came, and merchants
smiled,
But farmers groaned and snorted :
Black clouds of smoke the land defiled
Whose food was now imported.
Old Cobden’s creed became the rage,
And no keen eyes saw through it;
The Kings of Commerce on the stage
Bade Labor kneel to kiss its wage —
And Labor knelt to do it.
Though foreign competition vext
The ruthless fatted spoilers.
Still sticking to the Cobden text,
They bled the timid toilers;
The “classes” mocked the “masses’ ” pain,
Nor thought they’d ever rue it;
But workers, seeking long in vain
The quickest way their end to gain,
Have found the way to do it.
The city merchant goes in dread
Of lacking meat for dinner;
He dreams of Famine when in bed,
And when he wakes he’s thinner.
He’d have no use for djsh, or plate,
Or pepper from the cruet
If Labor ceased to feed the State,
And let the wolf in at the gate —
O Lord ! should Labor do it!
The waves that Britain rules in song
Are Britain’s sleepless gaolers,
Who kindly bear her food along
In vessels manned by sailors ;
The transport workers, down at heels,
Were strong, but never knew it,
Till—biff! and now the nation reels:
Proud Capital to Labor kneels,
And begs him not to Mo it!
The starved have turned the starvers. pale,
And Heaven’s bells are ringing ;
Old Fatman’s ships that brave the gale
To Britain’s shores are bringing
The food which is the Sweater’s life —
But shall the Sweater chew it?
The transport striker holds the knife,
The snirit of revenge is rife—
And if need be he’ll do it.

instead of colliding with itself round every

encouraged by the optimism of W. T. Mills,
who stated in Dunedin some days back that
“he had been in the country now for about
five weeks, and was convinced that there
could be no question that the people
of M.L. were more than ready to make a
very earnest and conclusive fight for the
adoption at the earliest possible day of all
the proposals made by either the Socialist or
Labor parties.” If he had been in the coun-
try five years, such an opinion might have
been valuable. But in that case he probably
wouldn’t have had it.
“lluffin Guffin” : The other day I met
the farmer for whom I did my first work as
a boy, over 30 years ago. He has the same
farm, and carries it on in much the same
way as he did back in 1872, when he selected
in Western Victoria. He reckoned that per-
haps he was a bit behind the times; but he
excused himself by adding that he did not
reach out for more acres than he could man-
age. No, he had no labor troubles. He was
satisfied with the return from practically his
own labor. He was a farmer of land, and
not of men ; he got his living out of his own
soil, and not out of others’ toil. Scarcity of
labor, therefore, didn’t worry him. So far
as he was concerned, there wasn’t any
scarcity. I never before saw so clearly what
this “scarcity of labor” really is. When a
man complains of it, he means something
like this: “I want to make a lot of money
quickly. I expected to make a lot out of
men whom I hoped to employ. But I can’t
get them, so I’m disappointed.” Then the
employers, or would-be employers, start com-
peting for the labor that is offering; if they
can’t grab the whole loaf they had
expected, some of them decide to try
to land half of it, by whacking the
expected profits with the workmen. Thus
wages go up, and soon the howl starts:
“Scarcity of labor.” In short: “I can’t find
as many tools as I want to make as much
money as I want.” So the disappointed
would-be Fatman fumes, and blames every-
body but himself. My old bpss may have an
idea that he is out of date, but he is really
ahead of it. He has found the way, and the
only way, to avoid labor troubles.
♦
“Lollypop” : “Virtue’s Reward” (B.
17/8/T1) deals only with the girls who
serve sweetstuffs for a paltry wage, and
“do” for themselves. A far larger class live
at home and use the cash they earn for
pocket-money. Private Enterprise in bigger
spheres of action than the confectionery
trade makes huge profit out of the practice
of dealing out mock wages to complaisant
workers of both sexes. In drapery and mil-
linery shops the absurdly underpaid girl ranks
as a matter of course, and in warehouses
and offices the services of male “juniors” are
got dirt-cheap for the reason that they are,
in many cases, not even bound to buy their
own clothes. It is these bogus workers who
are to blame, in great part, for the unfortu-
nates who, lacking comparatively-speaking
affluent parents, are driven to eke out joy-
less lives on seven half-crowns a week or
thereabouts. The system is rotten fore and
aft and amidships; it is equally demoralis-
ing to the pushful employer who takes ad-
vantage of it, and to the careless young
slave to “respectability ” who allows him or
herself to be exploited by it; and its evil
effects on the national life are obvious. Some
day the tiger “Socialism” will wade in and
insist that every adult who is useful enough
to be employed at all, shall, ipso facto, be
paid a living wage—and that regardless of
sex or of the class of business which does
the employing. But the time is not yet.
The craze tor the Genteel in life —the
fatuous passion which prevented the clerks
from unionising for so long—stands like 14
lions in the path.
Another well-brined sailor, Captain
Stewart, was entered in the log of Eter-
nity at Dunedin (M.L.) recently. It was
he who saved the Dunedin Harbor Board’s
big dredge from dropping into Davy Jones’s
cellar with all hands. The pumps had become
choked in a rush of dirty weather in the
Bay of Biscay, and the dredge filled. There
was one chance left, and the skipper took
the risk himself. He dived into the bilges
and with superhuman effort cleared the
pumps. The seamen didn’t give him three
cheers then, because they hadn’t time, but
they’ve been doing it ever since.
John Burns’s position in the British
Cabinet must have been an awkward one
when the question came forward of bringing-
troops to overawe strikers, led by his old
comrades —Tom Mann, of the “clanking
chains,” and the velvet-coated Tillett. But,
as a fact, Burns, though a great strike
leader, never initiated or advocated a strike,
lie was always called in when the strike
was under weigh, and the strikers were in
need of an undoubted leader. Twenty-one
vears ago. in the dockers’ strike, Burns
maintained order in a remarkable way. Be-
fore that, however, he had been twice “in
trouble.” In 1887, in the unemployed dem-
onstration in Trafalgar Square, he and Cun-
ninghnme Graham were sent to gaol for “un-
lawful assembly” after having had then-
heads battered while trying to break through
the police cordon. In those proceedings,
Asquith, Burns’s present chief, defended Gra-
ham, and Burns defended himself. Earlier,
m 1886, Burns had been arrested for an in-
flammatory speech in Trafalgar Square, but
was let off. In personal appearance, Burns
is a small, vigorous, bearded, grizzled, pock-
marked man, who is white enough to be
taken for twice his actual age—s 3.

“SMOKING IN THE ARMY.”
The recent report on the health of the Army brings
;he question of cigarette smoking to the forefront, in
,iew of the statement that many of the rejections of
otherwise strong recruits are caused by disordered
action of the heart, which, in the case of young
soldiers, is greatly aggravated, if not induced, by
excessive smoking of inferior cigarettes. The in-
crease of this habit year by year has been the subject
of remark in several commands, and there is a possi-
bility, if not an actual probability, of strong mea-
sures being taken by the authorities to bring about
a marked diminution in these affections as a whole.
If every smoker would only choose some good, reliable
brand of tobacco, and roll his own cigarette, using
the well-known “A.G.” Papers—which contain no-
‘hing of an injurious nature—we should hear much
less about the mischief of cigarette smoking. *

H. B. Irving has gone, trailing laurel
wreaths and suchlike tokens of appreciation
in his wake. There is a possibility that he
may return and show in “The Bells” and
“Jekyll and Hyde.” To-night (Thursday)
“Miss Gibbs” appears at Her Majesty’s for
one of the shortest seasons on record, and on
Saturday week the Melba opera co. opens in
“Traviata.”
*- * * *
One of the most alluring and artistic cir-
cumstances in “The Merry Widow,” which
still holds the fort at Sydney Royal, is Miss
Dorothy Brunton, who took over the role of
Miss Lottie Sargent last week —-the latter
having been ordered away for a rest-cure.
Miss Brunton is a daughter of the late John
Brunton, scenic artist, and a protegee of
Mrs. Hugh Ward.
** * *
“The Man from Outback” has been filling
Sydney Criterion nightly, and—the public
craving for bellowdrama being what it is—
would undoubtedly continue to fill it till
such time as the fabled Maorilander took
his stand on the North Shore Bridge and
gazed upon the ruins of the Ferry monopoly,
were he left to himself. However, the
theatre is booked for the Ethel Irving co.
from next Saturday night, and so, on Fri-
day, the uproarious creation of Bailey and
Duggan must hump his bluey. He will drop
it at the Palace Theatre next week.

Quite a number of people can now find
Sydney Y.M.C.A. Hall without assistance.
Joseph Blascheck and Mildred Wrighton, the
twin star performers, have been sparkling
there for more than three weeks; and _so
many astronomers and others have pilgrim-
aged to see the new constellation that the
bare temple in the wilderness may be said
to have a large and regular congregation.

Clarke and Mostol, comedians and dancers,
duly arrived at Sydney Tivoli last Saturday,
and agitated their talented feet; but Louie
Tracy, “the Dapper, Dandy Boy,” was too
ill to appear, and was scratched for one
week. Meanwhile, the bill has been bright-
ened by the return of Fred Bluett, who al-
ways contrives to be diverting. Alexander
Prince, of the concertinas, back for a brief
farewell season, is squeezing out impressive,
organ-like strains; and Lamberti has a
charnge of programme which is well worth
seeing. The other principals of the show
are entertainer Bach, ventriloquist Max,
Marguerite Ismay and Girls (dancers). Pas-
tor and Merle (comedians), and Irving Sayles
(smiler).
