The late Cardinal has been the man of the
hour at the picture shows. Spencer’s, at
the Lyceum, produced a series on Monday
night. One film showed the prelate’s last
important public appearance, at the laying
of the foundation stone of St. Mary’s High
School. Others pictured the lying-in-state
at the Cathedral, and the imposing funeral
procession on Sunday—the latter an excel-
lent reproduction. The “Margaret Catch-
pole” drama has been in its last nights ; and
there is the usual assortment of humorous
and educational subjects.

Royal pictures, this time showing the King-
in Edinburgh; “Chemical Action,” an ab-
sorbingly interesting scientific film; and a
dozen or so more. The West season at the
Glaciarium begins shortly.

Her Majesty’s (Melb.), swept, garnished
and greatly changed in its interior construc-
tion, will reopen on Saturday with “Ham-
let.” H. B. Irving is to introduce himself
to local notice as the loquacious Dane ; also
Miss Baird will appear as the demented
spinster who sought death amongst the
water-lilies and tadpoles, and the rest of the
co. will assist to repeat the Sydney per-
formance of a few weeks back. Melbourne
ought to be ready for another meal of classic
drama, anyhow. Seeing that the approach-
ing feast of murder, suicide, manslaughter
and immortal spectre is to be framed in a
new proscenium, a furious demand for seats
may be taken for granted.
* * * -*
At the Royal this is the last night of
“Lady Frederick” and her four suitors.
There is a growing conviction that the pretty
widow closes with the best offer of marriage.
The elderly, spasmodic Admiral is really out
of the question the moment he pops it; if the
lady had given her hand to the millionheir
of Aaron Levitzky, she would probably have
slain him with her noble birth; and the
hard, metallic lordling who loved her for her
face-powder must have maddened her with
his submission the first time she flared up.
But in Paradine Fouldes she will find a nice,
easy master, a rich man, and a good old pal.
For these last nights Miss Ethel Irving has
ceased to play Dolly of the Little Bills as an
afterpiece to “Lady Frederick.” Dolly now
comes as a curtain-raiser, and Miss Rosslyn
Vane takes the name part in lieu of the star,
with the result that the comedy changes its
appearance and sounds rather like a roaring
farce. However, this alteration obviated a
lot of misunderstanding among the audience.
Previously there was a tendency to regard
“Dolly’s Bills” as the last act of “Lady Fre-
derick,” and several softheaded citizens are
said to have gone home wondering what it
all meant.

Of Spencer’s shower of blessings at Mel-
bourne Olympia last Saturday, this paper
preferred “The Queen of Nineveh,” in which
a couple of neople showed that poisoning,
like Art, has its tricks and technique. In

ively loyal folk Spencer also screened “The
Investiture at Carnarvon of the Prince of
Wales”-—a young man who must by now
realise that royalty’s jobs, consist mainly of
walking on carpets and bobbing to crowds.

The Plimmer-Denniston Co. finished a good
season at Adelaide Royal with the “Tan-
queray” tragedy, which is possibly destined
to live as the typical drama of the present
age. Mrs. Brough seemed to emphasise the
whimsical and humorous Side of the char-
acter more than of old. She had to make a
speech on the farewell night, and told an in-
terviewer that the higher-class drama seems
to be more of a financial success than it
used to be. Plimmer —a sound Aubrey Tan-
queray—promises to be back soon with new
plays. Denniston, by the way, contented
himself with the small part of Ardale, leav-
ing to Sweeney the “fat” lines of Cayley
Drummle. The Tivoli has a strong bill,
headed by the gracefully-powerful Alcide
Capitaine and the Binns musical tramps;
also, Arthur Croxon and the Charmeroy lady
monologue with much effect, and the Dogs
continue to stand on one paw and wave
three and a tail towards the ceiling. The
Wondergraph has been starring films of the
Jacquerie, France’s long-past preliminary
canter for a Revolution —finely stage-mana-
ged. West’s, Vitoscope and Empire com-
pete keenly, and all the suburban halls break
out in a rash of pictures at intervals. Other
shows have varied from boxing at the Sta-
dium to lectures by Patrick Glynn on “Sin-
cerity in Literature”—clever but heavy—and
by Frederick Bevan on his old friend Sulli-
van —light and richly illustrated by songs.

Captain William Bendall, one of the old
time salt-junk sailors, slipped his cable a
Wellington (M.L.) last week, after 80 yeai
of the storms and calms of life. Just on hal
a century ago he arrived in M.L. waters i
his own ship, the Three Sisters, with ;
cargo of miners rushing to the Otago golc
fields. In 1878 he was appointed pilot a
Wellington to the old Panama Steam Navi
gation Co., and, later, marine surveyor, to
the Wellington Underwriters’ Association.
His best-known exploit was the salvage of
£25,000 worth of gold, which went down
with the Koranui in the French Pass. Wit!
his divers he secured the lot in 24 hours
When he signed on with Charon he was
Lloyds’ surveyor.

AT POVERTY POINT.
“0.K.”: Melbourne the other week had a good
sample submitted to it of the sort of theatrical fare
which may be expected from the new combine if the
combine is merely going to “give the public what
the public wants.” “Silver King” was revived for
the 49th time or thereabouts, and the public stood
two deep at the back of the d.-c., the seating ac-
commodation of the whole house being unequal to
the demand. This happened eyery night for a week,
so there could be no doubt about the public’s want-
ing that revival of “Silver King.” Yet it was the
absolutely weakest performance of the old drama,
judged on the bulk, that J.C.W. has ever staged in
Melbourne. This suggests that any old piece with a
iirst-rate reputation can be produced in Second-rate
style, with every nrobability of drawing as, much
patronage as though it were an excellent production.
“The public wants it,” Where, then, is the need for
an excellent show —from an all-powerful combine
point of view? When there is earnest competition
between two strong rival firms, neither Of them can
afford to lose caste with the critical; but a reputa-
tion for sustained endeavor, as regards brief popular
revivals, is not necessary to a combine that has no
dangerous competitor in the trade. The house can
be filled for a week or two with unsophisticated
public; and the absence of the well-informed makes
no financial difference then or thereafter.
“X. 0.”: It is said that a dreadful doubt entered
one Sydney newspaper office by the “Business” door
the other morning—a dreadful doubt as to the
wisdom, after all, of this theatrical combine and
other such “amalgamations.” “I’ve just been think-
ing,” said the haggard boss—“supposing they cut
down their advertisements!”
The log lately submitted to the managers by the
Federated Stage Employees’ Association asks a
minimum of £5 a week of eight performances for
operators at permanent picture shows. Ushers and
spruikers for all shows ask for not less than ss. a
time. The actual work of an usher or spruiker is not
very arduous, though it entails four hours of hang-
ing around. The log price for cloak-room attend-
ants (4s. a performance) is a reminder that men’s
cloakrooms these times have no regular custodian.
Where the old order of things continues —in the
women’s -room—the attendant is vaguely _ understood
to get he? only recompense in tips ; and it is rather
less vagilely understood that she guards the room
through :many a weary night without getting a soli-
tary “tanner” or “tray.” The log prescribes 70s.
for head cleaners, 50s. for other male cleaners, and
:10s. for female cleaners. The cleaning of some
theatres is left largely to the audience, but no price
is fixed for this service. Here and there the log
may be a bit stiff ; but when everything possible has
been said in criticism of the employees’ valuations of
things, the fact remains that theatrical managers are,
on the whole, most systematic cheeseparers. Every
interruption to business means a stoppage of salaries
and wages, and a temporary cessation of profits sets
the management thinking of how to cut down exes.
Which would be more pardonable if the employees
were given a bonus every time a rotten show gets
five times the patronage it deserves.
“Theorbo”: Another opera co., Quinlan’s, is com-
ing. It reckons on getting Melba’s orchestra and
chorus after she has done with them, besides other
props. The Quinlan Syndicate is highly financial,
and already has an Australian agent. The artists
will be young and ambitious, though comparatively
unknown. There will be the Melba repertoire to
work upon, and a large selection beside, with prices
cut down by about one-half. Melba and Williamson
are perfect; but look at all that can be added:
“Barber of Seville,” “Don Giovanni,” “Figaro,”
“Valkyrie,” “Flying Dutchman,” “Tannhauser,”
“Aida,” “Trovatore,” “Lucrezia Borgia,” “Martha,”
“Maritaha,” “Bohemian Girl,” “Huguenots,” “Semi-
ramide,” “Daughter of the Regiment,” “Norma,”
■“Sonnambula,” “Freischiitz,” and so many other
glories. The Quinlans will just fling masses of
music at us, and trust to luck for the rest. But
for the present it is up to us to rise to the occasion
and give the plucky “Firm” all the support our
pockets can stand.

What an education besto ed on the generation be-
fore last! Did it not enrich them? And is not
the public of to-day too exigent in demanding from
Melba that the operas shall be done exactly as in

ought to go for the music, rather than the mighty
stars and fixings. Anyway, give me a sackful of
Cape diamonds rather than a Kohinoor.
“0.K.”: In the controversy re the art value of
slavish imitation in mummery, “Snell” scores a
point by his reference to the mimic m ‘Our Miss
Gibbs,” who looked and sounded exactly like an-
other mimic then performing in another part of the
Commonwealth. ■ I was among the pressmen who
supposed, before looking at the programme, that
Leslie Holland, remindful of Grossmith, the London
original, was identical with Langford Kirby, a pre-
vious imitator of the same comedian. The new per-
former was devoid of new effect, because he was
copying the other man’s model. There is no gleam
of personality in the performance of either comedian
if their respective imitations of a third comedian s
are indistinguishable. And how about that third
comedian’s natural rights? He may want to
come out here some day with his legitimate stock-
in-trade. If he came he would be in a worse posi-
tion than was Edward Terry when he brought his
own original creation of Dick Phenyl in Sweet
Lavender.” Frank Thornton had forestalled him
with a close imitation of his voice and mannerisms
in the same character. The public took I erry to be
t lie mimic, and rated him cheaply on that account.
The same sort of misfortune befalls some of the
music-hall singers imported by Rickards. I heir
song, make-up, voice and style have been scaled
by some pirate mimic who came before. Surely the
men who do something—however small—off their
own bat should be emulated rather than imitated.
The influence of mimicry in mummery tends to
discourage natural talent and. thoughtful work.
Slavish imitation should be for amateurs, not pio-
fessionals, in any line of art.
“Screen” : The Melbourne Cyclorama is up for sale.
It was built for the “Waterloo” panorama, which
had a good run. “Jerusalem” and Gettysbuig
followed ; but the fever was passing, and the results
from these were not so happy. The first _ boom in-
spired the building of the “Siege of Pans Cyclo-
rama in Little Collins-street. It was a terrific frost,
and has disappeared. At the opening, banlcei Turner
said, “What a splendid forgerv!” and Premier and
land-seller Patterson said: “Hundreds of acres in
the heart of Melbourne!” Yet, splendid illusion and
all, the cyclorama picture has for years been dead
as a kippered herring, and the place has had to live
on picture shows and stoushing matches, with a cold
frost mostly on Tuesday, Wednesday, Thursday and
Friday evening's. It looks a white elephant, y t
is nothing like so hopeless as the Alexandra was
when Williamson got hold of that barn and turned
it into Her Majesty’s.
Carden Wilson, whose “imitations of people I
have seen and heard” have given joy to many Aus-
tralians, died last week, after a tough struggle, in
which he was nursed devotedly by his wife, who is
Pearl Hellmrich in the profession. The plucky lady,
while she nursed a dving husband, kept her own
turn going at the Tivoli, and if there are any benefits
going to waste she deserves one of the fattest. Wil-
son was a son of the well-known old scenic artist.
Stephen Ewart, leading man in the Ethel Irving
Co., stands 6ft. lfin. in his ordinary walking boots.
On the stage the tall fellow abbreviates himself with
diminished heels, and a bend or inclination which is
a fine little study in calisthenics.
“Gnark”: An artistic feature of Lamberti’s per-
formance is the foreign accent, which reminds one
of another tall, gentlemanly ornament of the
Rickards show, named Rudinoff. Australia has
not forgotten the handsome stranger who made
“smoke pictures” and apologised fot his Ingleesh,
as being “rude enoff.” He was understood to be a
Cockney, a la Russe, who laid aside his fancy accent
with liis fancy ' dress. Lamberti’s accent smacks of
Franco Ge-man-Italiano parentage, but he is under-
stood to be an Englishman, who learned his business
in America. Or perhans he- is a Welshman. He was
born Lambert, and his brother of that name is an
influential citizen of Cardiff.

The figures representing Comedy and Tragedy,
decorating the proscenium of the re-arranged
Maiesty’s (Melb.), are the work of George Dancey,
the black and white artist, whose illustrations have
so often adorned The Lone Hand. Dancey is artis-
tically various. When not elaborating _ political car-
toons for Paunch, he is employed designing stained
glass windows for churches and other holy edifices.
He vearns towards mural decoration, and the work
at Her Maj.’s he considers his first Opportunity, with
a large O.

“The Oldest Inhabitant”: Apropos the “Margaret
Catchpole” drama at the Duke of York’s Theatre
(London) recently. “The Dictionary of Natural
Biography” states that the lady was born in 1773,
and died much respected in 1841. If this be cor-
rect, we have now three ‘‘Margaret Catchpoles
the one said to have come in the Royal Admiral in
the 1790 s, the one who came in the Nile
in 1801, and the one who died in Sydney in 1841.
The lady by the Royal Admiral, an educated woman,
died at Newtown in 1855 ; the woman by the Nile,
an uneducated person, died (according to Parson
Fulton) in 1819, and was buried in St. Matthew s,
Windsor.

“ Impersonator ” and Character Actor.
“Melb”: “The Silver King” again! Original
Australian cast: Nellie Denver, Annie Mayor; Olive
Skinner, Blanche Stammers; Wilfred, Titheradge;
Jaikes, Day; Ooombe, Redwood; Corker (.Duke of
New York), Phillips; Cripps, Leake; The Spider,
Carner; Baxter, Whyte; Ware, Cates; the Three
Cronies, E. Marshall, Sefton, Daniels; Pottle, Hos-
kihs. That cast was never equalled in London,
though, possibly, it may have been in America.
Silver King actors known to Australia have been
Barrett, Titheradge, Knight, Hedley, Bentley, Plim-
mer, Inglis, W. Holloway; and of Jaikeses we have
had Day, Hoskins, C. Holloway, Elton, Dodges and
Atholwood. The latest Melbourne cast figures out
better than might have been expected. Desmond
breaks away from some of our traditions. Tither-
adge was the best. Barrett had petrified by the time
he acted Denver in Australia. I remember having a
lively argument behind the scenes with Mr. and
Mrs. Rignold, both of whom championed “The Lights
o’ London” against “The Silver King.” Barrett, of
course, had starred in both plays in London, and
in both he had shown a good Saxon quality. In that
respect he compared well with the otherwise im-
measurably superior Irving, who was always of the
French school.
Richard Lee, a London dramatic critic, just dead,
at 80, made at least one sensation in his day. It was
over a long-forgotten play of Charles Reade’s, “Shilly
Shally,” which contained a line of possible double
meaning. Toole, who had to say it, did so in. italics,
so to speak, and the house chucklingly accepted it
as dreadfully improper. Lee fell foul of the author
over this; whereupon Reade —who always loved a
row —brought a libel action, and Lee’s paper, the
Morning Advertiser, was unexpectedly shot for
£5OO and expenses.
“W.F.”: “Propstick” must have dreamt he
had that copy of Bell's Life he talked about in a
recent Bulletin. The facts are still fresh in my
mind. About 50 years ago, or a little over, one
Saturday night I went to the Theatre Royal, Ballarat,
to see G. V. Brooke play “Louis XI,”, After it was
finished, Hoskins came on and announced that, on
Monday, Henry Neil Warner would appear as Oth-
ello, G. V. Brooke as lago, Rosa Dunn as Desdemona,
and Mrs. Charles Poole as Emilia. I went on Mon-
day, and, of course, the curtain rose to a crowded
house. Brooke and J. W. Bruton (Roderigo) came
on. Bruton spoke his opening lines; Brooke made
no response. Bruton whispered to Brooke, who
seemed half asleep, and stuttered out: “I am here in
the capacity of a man.” The audience tittered, and
Brooke said something about recovering himself;
then he went to sleep again, and the curtain was
dropped. Hoskins came on and said Brooke was ill.
The audience: “Drunk!” Hoskins said it was not
for him to say, and would they accept himself as
1 agoV Of course they cried “Yes!” Off he went,
and in a few minutes'the curtain rose, and the play
went off without a hitch, much to Hoskins’s credit,
his wonderful knowledge of Shakspeare pulling him
through.
From London: The inevitable burst-up iW the
picture-palace boom seems to have arrived. Pal-
aces” are putting up the shutters in all directions
in the suburbs, although in the streets around gay
and giddy Leicester Square and Piccadilly they are
still doing good business —in the evenings. In these
latter quarters, the “palaces” are freely patronised
by nymphs, who were quick to discover that a dark-
ened auditorium presents exceptional, facilities for
casual conversations and temporary acquaintance-
ships. It is well that scores of the “palaces are
closing up before some appalling disaster occurred.
Most of them should never have been licensed at all
—empty shops and warehouses quickly transformed
into places of public amusement, . without adequate
ventilation or means of escape in an emergency.
The slump did not set in a moment too soon.
Another triumph for Australia! There has been
a contest in caechination at the White City, London,
between a laughing jackass and a hyena. A bet of
£lOO a side depended on the issue. The kookaburra
belonged to the Hippodrome, and the snarling beast
to Bostock, of the famous menagerie. London Star
gives nearly a column to the affair, a member of its
staff being one of the three judges. Marks were
awarded to the row under three heads—loudness,
length, and timbre. Australia led off and did well;
nevertheless the first round went to the. hyena. But
that unprepossessing beast made the mistake of not
conserving his great natural resources. All t.ie
efforts of' his trainer could not persuade him to enter
the field again. The jackass, on the other hand, was
inspired to further and prolonged laughter by the
sight of a cage of monkeys, and thus “won easily. ’

The White Man’s burden in Maoriland :
In a Chinese vegetable garden in the suburbs of
Auckland, for the last couple of weeks a white man
could be seen, who is employed to walk up and
down between the rows of peas, and act as a living
scarecrow. — Wairarapa Age.
Which is a local illustration of Chowseph
Ward’s scheme of providing a Dreadnought
from M.Ci. to protect Hongkong.
Westralia’s best-known mining warden and
police magistrate, Finnerty, is on the point
of retiring. In the early days of Coolgardie
he was the Law, and the whole Law, and
when Finnerty spoke the Full Court was
silent —anyhow, nobody ever had the teme-
rity to ask it to speak. The veteran is to
get just the sort of send-off that the big
goldfield knows how to give.
The other day the Age broke into this
shout of joy:—
It will not be long now before the Commonwealth
and State Agents are housed under one roof, an
arrangement certain to lead shortly to the unifica-
tion of the offices and the abolition of the porten-
tous positions of States’ Agents-General, as there is
now an emphatic and widespread opinion that the
existing confusion is wasting our possibilities.
But why sail under false colors? Why be
hypocrites and present ourselves as one
people when we tolerate State Frights, and
can’t even deal with a little matter of com-
mon commercial roguery without submitting
six Bills to 12 Houses of Parliament, and
taking our chance of getting them through
at great cost? It is absurd, of course, to
have all this confusing swarm of Agents in
London, hut it isn’t half so disastrous as
having this confusing swarm of Legislatures
at home.
During the last few days Sydney has
been learning more about its one and only
Cardinal than ever Dill Macky knew about
him. For one thing, it is assured that, after
all, a Cardinal need not be even a priest.
He is only, it seems, a Papal prince, and
not necessarily a great ecclesiastical digni-
tary at all; and it is recalled that Cardinal
Newman finished his life as a simple priest
subject to Bishop Ullathorne. It follows
that the prelate on the top-rung of the lad-
der in Australasia need not be a Cardinal;
so there is no question at all of appointing
a Cardinal to “succeed” the one who has
just died.
George Vernon, who was tried last year
for the murder of an old man named Neil
at Doondi (Q.) and acquitted, is alleged to
have confessed to a London paper, and then
committed suicide. Vernon’s last seven
years had been full of wild adventure. A
son of G. F. Vernon (of Bligh s Eng-
lish team of 20 years back, and subsequently
a captain of an M.C.C. side), his people
were the toniest: but young Vernon s ‘ in-
discretions” led to his family helping him
off to Canada, where he joined the Border
Police. Then he drifted to the Cape, and
finally reached Australia, and went jack-
erooing at Doondi. When standing his trial
oceans of cash were spent on him, and Feez,
K.C., and Woolcock defended him. The con-
scientious Kingsbury (Crown Prosecutor), as
well as his own counsel, was impressed with
his innocence, and probably, for that reason,
the Crown didn’t push forward all its evi-
dence, which, anyway, was purely circum-
stantial. Vernon was 25, but looked only
17, and his fresh, youthful face pfocured
him a demonstration from the crowd on his
acquittal.
“J.F.H.” (from the other side) :
It may be news to Sydneyites that the heroic
statue of Captain Cook in Hyde Park is a humbug,
a fraud, and an imposture. Cook did not care two
straws about Australia. He actually apologised to
the Admiralty for not having discovered something
valuable. His scientific companion, Sir Joseph
Banks, is the man who ought to be statued. The
life of Banks, by Edward Smith, just published in
London, makes this perfectly clear. Banks became
an enthusiast for Australia after returning to Eng-
land and settling down in Soho Square, London.
For 40 years he was the unsalaried, unofficial Agent-
General for Australia in England, and very likely
the Sydney settlement would have been abandoned
if it had not been for his continued efforts and
powerful influence at headquarters. The TikES,
in reviewing the book, says: “No less force than
Banks persistently emploved for nearly 40 years
against official ignorance and stupidity and despair
could have induced the much-worried Ministers of
George 111. to establish and maintain a costly settle-
ment at the other end of the world while their _ own
end was continually disturbed with the turmoil of
American and French revolutions and the menace
of the Napoleonic wars.” In a word, Banks was the
real “founder” of Australia.
THE DEADLY WEAPON.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The hero holds the flaming boards,
Whence all but he had fled,
And on the yelling Boerish hordes
Come pressing o’er the dead.
“Come, cowards,” once again he cries,
“And see a Briton die!”
And but the booming shot replies,
And shells go howling by.
A smoking bomb falls at his feet.
He hurls, it from the fort,
And shakes the flag that none can beat,
And laughs as if in sport.
Like giant hail the cannon balls
Against the wall are slammed ;
In noble scorn the hero calls:
“Fire on, there, and be damned!”
But mark what terror seams his brow,
See how he quakes and nods,
As through the air comes spinning now
The turnip of the gods!
VICTORIA.
SNELL.
SUBSTITUTE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
King George gave orders for a stand to be erected
in the yard of the Guild Hall, to* enable the aged
poor and children in the workhouses to witness the
procession. —News item.
They asked for bread and he gave them a grand-
stand. —Revised version.
The weary Royal mime,
With golden coach and six,
Rolls through the weary time
That stupid customs fix—
With sceptre, orb and sword,
With church-blest “holy” oil,
An earl and noble lord
To keep off those who toil ;
Build a mighty stand
Get a brazen band
For the pore and the bybies of the workus.
Lord Loafer holds a wand,
Earl Waster bends a knee,
Dull Viscount in the Strand,
Drum-major by Chelsea ;
Trained choirs cry “Save the King,”
But none the people save ;
Yet let their voices ring
Though haste they to the grave —
Shout from mighty stand,
Blare with brassy band,
For pore blighters an’ the bybies of the workus.
This is your guerdon proud
And this your splendid lot,
To drink and cheer aloud
Though death be in the pot;
The pauper and his wife,
The children underfed,
Applaud a wasted life
With praises from the dead.
Build a mighty stand,
Get a brassy band
For pore blighters and the kinchins of the workus.
VICTORIA.

Society
HONI SOIT, ETC.
During the inevitable pause, Miss Simple makes
a “ slip of the tongue .”
In recently granting a decree nisi to a
husband who sued because of his wife’s mis-
conduct, but who had himself also strayed
from the path of virtue, Justice a’Beckett
(Vic.) not only capsized several ancient
legal precedents, but condescended—an ex-
.ample to learned brethren—to give sound
reasons for doing so. lie “proposed to de-
.part from the policy that held a wrongful
act a bar to divorce, because the husband
.could not be expected to live with his wife
again. The husband was of an age at which
lie might hope to marry and have children,
and by denying him the right to do so, the
Court would probably conduce to irregular
relations injurious to society.” So over-
board goes the doctrine of “clean hands” —
the stupid idea that because each party had
done something to qualify for a divorce,
therefore neither party should get it. It
was about as ridiculous as refusing to save
a man’s life by amputating his crushed
left leg because the right one was also
mangled.

The cheerful person whom a Melbourne

Thus London World :
Why white divers in future for the Australian
pearl-fisheries? The work is extremely hazardous.
It is folly to legislate against such obvious economy
as the employment of colored labor in work that is
none too remunerative, and attended by risks out of
all proportion to the reward.
Good, kind, Christian England ! Diving is
shockingly badly-paid work. Therefore, give
it to our beloved brother, the black man. It
is dreadfully risky. Therefore, let us recruit
ottr beloved brother for the service. And
then dear, good, kind, Christian England
wonders why “tbe savage” waits for Eng-
land’s white-chokered representative with a
club.
* * * *
The Vic. Railway Commissioners receive
so much conscience money, and are so dis-
turbed in their book-keeping by -it, that they
are thinking of distributing printed forms
which the conscience-stricken person may fill
in, giving details that will enable the clerks
to “place” the original peculation. The
printed docket will, of course, be known as
the penitent form.
Victoria’s Railway Department, having
had no bad smashes for quite a month, lias
struck anew vein of fatal casualness. The
other day it left a water truck containing
two full 400-gallon tanks in an up-country
station yard, and two swaggies, coming
along after a dry and dusty tramp, sampled
the contents. The contents appeared to be
clear water, and there was nothing on the
tanks to indicate otherwise; but each tank
happened to contain arsenic, for weed-
killing purposes. One of the travellers
is now humping bluey in Elysium, while
the other hasn’t decided, at latest, whether
to follow him or stay here a while. Mean-
time, the Coroner is preparing some
weighty remarks to heave at a Department
that does fool things like that.
A member of the Melbourne Employers’
Federation complained touchingly the other
day
Many people think there is no heaven for business
men.
Absurd, of course. Yet people have been
saying it, or something like it, for quite a
long while. In fact, there is a report that
1900 years ago, one, called Jesus, said it was
easier for a camel to pass through a needle’s
eye than for a rich trader to crawl into
Paradise.

The S. Aus. House of Assembly keeps on
asking questions about delays in the Su-
preme Court. The latest return furnished
shows that there is nothing at all hung up
—with one horrible exception, Sexton v.
Opie, which was “reserved for judgment” by
Chief Justice Way, in September, 1909!
“The stress of work ever since has prevented
the decision of this case,” it is explained.
The C.J., who wants to have a finger
in every pie, is a member of more
Boards and Councils than a veteran
can fairly be expected to find time
for ; and the Government lately gave him a
strong hint by dropping him altogether off
the Public Library, Art Gallery, and Mu-
seum Board, of which for years he was
president.

The cruellest spear that has been thrown
at brother Binghi for years past left G.
Reid’s hand at the Colonial Institute in
London just before the last mail left. “You
must make allowances for the Australian
black,” said Binghi’s apologist. “He had
no dense forests to arouse his latent ener-
gies, and there were no wild beasts to dis-
turb his gluttonous repose!” So now J.
Bull knows Why Binghi habitually had a
menu running to a thick and a clear soup,
two kinds of fish, a couple of entrees, a
brace of roasts, two sorts of ripe cheese and
wines en suite, and thus gradually accom-
plished a girth of 82 inches. Had there
been wild beasts to nose in at the feast, and
tread on the victuals, Binghi would have
been compelled to run his palace on the
quick-lunch and counter-s'nack principle,
and he might then have been as
lean and haggard as Houston Greid.
But it was his misfortune that the ichthyo-
saurus was dead and the dinosaur remained
only as a fossil, so the poor wretch became
a glutton, who never had his repose dis-
turbed, and in the end a dreadful High Com-
missioner, who never had a decent meal in
his life, and couldn’t be reposeful if he
wanted to, looked back and threw clods at
him.

The vastly intelligent system, whereby the
flood of N. S. Wales products is directed
towards the bottie : neck of Sydney, became
unusually distracted recently, as the result
of a miscalculation on the part of the city
commission agents. Gambling on a dry
Spring, they had purposely held supplies at
country sidings with the object of giving
prices a leg-up; but when it became appar-
ent that the Spring wasn’t going to be a
party to the proceedings, a spurt was made
to clear the sidings and rush everything to
Darling Harbor. Although the season was
slack—with the fruit, grain and wool traffic
absent —the extra quantity of pumpkins and
the unusual amount of spud were unable to
crowd down the bottle simultaneously, and
hurried messages were sent north, south and
west to stop loading at country stations for
more than three days a week. In the middle
of the storm of abuse, protest and gibbering
rage from merchants, agents, cockies and
others, came the triumphant word that the
new line of railway between Maitland and
Dungog was open for traffic and ready to
empty a contribution into the congestion.
At latest information, decentralisation was
galloping to the front like a wooden horse.

The strenuous “Jimmie Pannikin,” who
is a N.S.W. school-inspector when he has
his office clothes on, has pushed another
iron in the fire. Many of the school grounds
are now being planted with trees, and he
wants to know now why they
some of them, anyway—be fruit trees, His
idea is that, while the fruit wouldn't do the
youngsters any harm (presuming it was left
long enough on the vegetables), the school
plantation might incite the neighborhood to
dig a few holes and put in a few trees'" round
its own humpies, to the advantage, of its
pocket and the good of its health.
SONG OF LOST SUPPERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Launceston, Monday.-—The ketch L. 11. Burden,
which was • stranded at Flinders Island about six
days ago, is likely to become a total wreck. The
vessel was loaded with crayfish.— News item.
The bright red fish with the armored back that stirs
on the marble slab,
Or crawls about in search of sea on the roof of the
homing cab ;
The diffident, well-boiled, crimson food which waggles
protesting palms,
And acts (though very reluctantly) as the hoozer’s
coat-of-arms:
All these are prisoners doomed to die, poor prisoners
of the town—
The Dago’s clear cash-register bell
Has rung in the night their careless knell!
But there’s a different tale to tell
When the fisherman’s ketch goes down.
Now, how many drunks will weep to-night and moan
on the shadowed quay /■
For armored food that crawls about on the floor of
the sounding sea?
The hold was a tangled mass of claws as she sailed
the wintry sea;
And the captain feared —-as a captain might—a cray-
fish mutiny ;
But all went well till the vessel struck; then the
seamen fled the wreck;
The cargo stirred in an awful way —The suppers
crawled up on deck!
Oh, yes, that cargo seethed and boiled till the wreck
was a heaving dome!
The drunks may weep on the shadowed quay,
But every fish got off scot free;
Down in the depths of the winter sea
Are the prisoners crawling home.
The silent, salt, Imperial cray for the soak hath
many charms;
But not this time will these fresh fish act as the
dipso’s coat-of-arms l

“Vagabond”: I don’t see why “Chasuble”
(B. fi/3/’ll) and the other fellows
make a fuss about a parson inventing a
little thing like a hat-rack under church
seats. It isn’t as though he’d invented an
adjustable cushion or pillow, so that a man
cbuld lean back and think comfortably and
not too loudly during the sermon! And
“invented” a hat-rack —great Hamlet, aren’t
these inventions in scores of theatres all over
the world? And there are theatres and music-
halls where they have another invention—
a neat little shelf, designed to hold a bottle
and glass, fixed on the back of the chair in
front of you. If “Chasuble’s” parson would
get something of the sort installed, and ar-
range a proper supply of the necessary
liquid, and supply a neat little rubber tube
attachment that could be fixed to the bottle,
he would fill a long-felt want. Also, inci-
dentally, he would fill his church.

Of all the Coronation slobber in the
London papers—and there was, on the
whole, less there than in the Australian
papers—the most slobberiy was a column
in the Chronicle by C. Brunsden Flet-
cher, associate editor of the Sydney Morn-
ing Herald, which might have been passed
if it hadn’t been headed “An Australian
View.” C. Brunsden Fletcher dipped his
pen in tears, and began this way:—
“God Save the King!” I have seen the crown
actually placed upon the King’s head, and have
heard the acclaim ot his people. I have also been a
witness to the anointing and crowning of the Queen,
and have cried with the multitude for her safety.
Then he asks himself how it is that the
spectacle has moved him so —him from the
Commonwealth, which “is supposed to lack
reverence, and to think little of robes and
rubrics.” And he explains why
I had seen the King and shaken hands with him
before reaching the Abbey. A great deal of my
emotion at the Coronation came because in Aus-
tralia I had held the hand of the King in mine.
That settled it! Brunsden Fletcher had
touched the King’s hand, and, after that,
when the King .was crowned he “felt that
it was all necessary, fitting and right.” In-
cidentally, the Man Who Touched the
King’s Hand mentions that “we were cooped
in a dark, dirty gallery, and the only
glimpses which visitors like myself could
get were obtained by craning forward at the
risk of life and limb. 1 had to hang on to
iron bars,” he says, “and hug the dust of
grimy pillars until I was foul.” Still, on
reconsideration, Brunsden Fletcher, refers
to it all as “a solemn ceremony in so appro-
priate a setting” Well, Mr. Fletcher no
doubt knows what his surroundings should
have been, in order to make everything ap-
propriate ; and as everything was appro-
priate, he was evidently in his right place.
Still, wouldn’t something in the way of a
nice, damp cellar have been better than a
dusty, grimy gallery V
* * * *
The N. S. Wales Marine Court, having
pondered over the circumstances of a colli-
sion between a Manly ferry boat and a col-
lier, which had been anchored in Sydney
Harbor’s fairway during a fog, has decided
that, as both navigators were slavishly fol-
lowing the regulations for preventing colli-
sions, the act of bumping can be blamed to
nobody excepting Providence. And as Provi-
dence can’t have its certificate cancelled, nor
be fined five bob and costs, the matter ends
there. If the judgment is any good at all,
it means that a crowded ferry can anchor
in the fairway and be walked over by an
ocean giant feeling its way up or down the
harbor through a peasoup fog. In that case,
nobody would be held to blame, and the
corpses would have to pay for their own
cofiins. What is wanted is the suspension
of these British-framed collision-preventing
regulations, which did not anticipate crowded
water thoroughfares like Sydney Harbor, and
the substitution of special regulations to
meet local requirements. The Harbor
Trust has power to do this, and it is the
common-sense thing to do. There are rude
people who will say that that is probably
why it has not been done.
A few days asm, a Chow named Wong
Low called at one of Victoria’s marriage
shops with a girl of 17. The parson said
to the girl: “Were you 21 years old last
birthday?” and of course the girl said “Yes.”
So the Chow was handed a young white
girl, and the parson got his fee; and no
doubt both were satisfied. The irony of the
business is that one of the three principals
is being tried for participating in the horri-
ble business. A sane person would natur-
ally suppose that it would be either the
Chow or the parson. Nbt at all. It is the
17-year-old girl who is being tried for “mak-
ing a false declaration.” This parson con-
fessed to marrying about 400 couples during
the year, so it looks as though it’s his living;
and the sooner the job is turned over to a
State-salaried registrar whose bread-and-
butter does not depend upon hitching young
white girls to yellow abominations the bet-
ter. That sort of official would have shooed
Wong Low off the premises if that horror
had dared to come to him. Most likely the
Chow wouldn’t have dared.
Has the Britisher lost all his national
pride? The last notable to visit the Com-
monwealth —Sir John Kirk, of the London
Ragged School Onion —boasts that his insti-
tution has 130 depots for relieving starving
families in the slums of London. This may
mean anything from 25,000 to 50,000 people
dependent on one institution in London for
the bare necessaries of life. The absence of
crowds from the Coronation show was pro-
bably due to the fact that about half London
was too naked and ashamed, or two weak
from hunger, to turn out and help to add to
the glorious pageant.
* * * *
The S. M. Herald says that Cardinal
Moran “identified himself with the social
and industrial progress of the land.” Which
no doubt means that he favored the Labor
policy. It is a. fairly correct statement of
the case. The organ of the Cardinal took
upon itself, at a critical time, to stampede
the R.O. electors out of the Labor ranks,
prompted thereto by a disgruntled Laborite.
When Pope Leo XIII. issued his famous en-
cyclical letter on Socialism, a Consecrated
Press writer proclaimed that it bound the
Ronjan Catholics in Australia to leave the
Labor party or be attainted outcasts. The
Cardinal’s organ foolishly supported this at-
titude. The rival Catholic journal in Syd-
ney, however, pointed out that the Papal
announcement was ex cathedra, and not
binding on the consciences of those who took
their views from Rome. Moreover, it quoted
another encyclical letter of the same Pope
in support of Australian Labor principles.
This declared that “any free, self-governing
community could make whatever laws it
pleased for the welfare of its people, pro-
vided always it did no injustice to any
man.” The following Sunday the Cardinal
spoke out plainly. He said the organ that
had attacked Labor had done excellent ser-
vice for the Church, but in this case it had
made a serious error. The Pope’s pronounce-
ment did not apply to the peaceful, lawful
reforms of the Australian Labor party.
Thereafter the editorials of the consecrated
organ were supervised.

the fiends who blister the landscape wth re-
marks concerning corsets, pills and other
confectionery. The referee, who is a magis-

whether an ordinance prohibiting the adver-
tisement eye-sore on a “wall” had been
broken when it appeared on the side of a

charged the bill-poster £2 for the informa-
tion. Round two will be fought in the
Sunerior Stadium.
Touching a historic estate : —•
Announced that A’ale Head, Molong (N.S.AAL), an
estate of 2000 acres, has been sold by Mr. H. C.
Betts to Mr. Robert Bruce, of Cumnock. The
transaction revives some old memories. Vale Head
was one of the first Crown grants. It is a fine pro-
perty, on the right bank of the Molong rivulet (a
stream having its source at the ba.se of the Canoblas
Range, near Orange), and was originally vested in
the Rev. Samuel Marsden, parson and magistrate, of
Parramatta. Vale Head itself is an eminence over-
looking Molong and a charming valley, with willows
cooling the waters of the rivulet, while on the oppo-
site elevations are remarkable outcrops of limestone.
(“Molong” is native for “stone”.) What was once the
main western road, that leading to the old-time
convict settlement of Wellington, trailed by Vale
Head. Here the Rev. Samuel depastured sheep, and
he built a big octagonal house on the height. That
quaint caboose was rented by Thomas Cobden, a
brother of Richard Cobden, the Corn Law repealer
and contemporary of John Bright. Thomas Cobden
secured a liquor license —the first issued beyond
Bathurst —and the octagonal dwelling was the resort,
of travellers, teamsters, shepherds and others. Oh
the estate is a graveyard where sleep many pioneers
of a vast territory. There rest the bones of the
Cobden’s bonifacial brother. Thomas Cobden left a
large family, and it would be interesting to know
the number and whereabouts of his descendants.
The sons were “thorough, colonials” —that is, they
were daring horsemen, expert bushmen and adven-
turous spirits. Among the first managers of the
pastoral property was John Smith (afterwards
M.L.C., and owner of many station properties in the
vicinity of Molong). Mr. Smith occupied a cottage,
embedded in a garden and orchard, near the bank of
the rivulet, and in this cottage was born his eldest
son, the first native of Molong—Fergus Jago Smith,
M.L.C. To-day there are but few traces of the
cottage. The garden and fruit-trees have long dis-
appeared. On the opposite bank is the snug town
of Molong of to-day. Vale Head eventually passed
to Henry Samuel Marsden Betts, a descendant of the
grantee. Mr. Betts married a daughter of Captain
Finch, and reared a large family. On his death
Vale Head passed to his son, Mr. Henry C. Betts:
and now, after the lapse of nearly a century, the
estate leaves the Marsden descendants. The pur-
chaser, Mr. Robert Bruce, springs from another
pioneer family. The late George Bruce, of Loombahi,
won world-first prizes for champion wool.

THE SPITTING BRIGADE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They set up a sign in the street,
They set up a sign in the train,
And on it was writ:
“PLEASE DO NOT SPIT!”
But the Notice was printed in vain!
From spitting they would not refrain,
For to spit is to prove you are “free” :
Dick, Harry and Tom
Simply spit with aplomb
From the station to Circular Quay!
Spit! Spit! Spit!
In ferry, in carriage, in tram—
They spit with a will,
And they’re spattering still,
For they don’t give a tuppenny darn.
All night it had steadily rained,
The city was clean as a pin;
In the clear, morning light
All the pavements shone bright,
As the people came hurrying in
The work of the day to begin.
It was really a pleasure to sit
And look at them, bustling,
And cheerily hustling—
Till somebody started to spit!
Spit! Spit! Spit!
In spite of the sign, often seen,
Which they held in derision,
And spat with precision
AVherever the pavement was clean !
All day was the city defiled.
Each tram was a moving spittoon,
While the pavement was made
By the Spitting Brigade
To look like a viscid lagoon.
They spat upon dresses and shoon,
On passage, and stairway, and floor;
And they gathered at lunch
In a “fag”-smoldng bunch,
And started to salivate more.
Spit! Spit! Spit!
At factory, office, and shop,
They smoked and they chattered,
And copiously spattered,
And nobody told them to stop !
Arise, O Lord Mayor, in thy wrath !
Arise, stern Commissioner, too!
And turn mild requests
Into haughty behests
To awe the contemptuous crew,
Who spit if they talk, smoke or chew,
Who spit just to show they are “free” —
Tom, Harry and Dick,
AVho make everyone side
From Redfern to Circular Quay!
(With their) Spit! Spit! Spit!
Give heed, 0 ye Powers, to this rhyme!
Make a merciless raid
On the Spitting Brigade,
And fine them a fiver a time !
N.S.W.

ALITIES
HE WANTED THE FREEHOLD.

a bed, Mister?”
KIND OLD GENT (fingering his money): “How

“H.S.M. : Can anybody better this, and
speak the truth ? I have an old mare who has had
11 foals (five fillies and six gentlemen). The five
fillies have between them 20 foals and three grand-
children, making a gross total of 35. All but four
are alive and working. Nobody can cast doubts on
this in my presence and remain healthy.
“A.F.”: Re death of “Wild Wright” (B.
3/8/’11), just a little recollection of that notorious
man in his prime. He gloried then in the name
of Isaiah, and weighed 12 stone, mostly bone. He
made a meteoric appearance at Yanko station just
before shearing started in August, ’69. There were
about 50 men in the hut as he stalked in, and an-
nounced himself: “Hogan’s ghost 1 Is there any
flash man here?” No response. “I’m Isaiah, the
prophet, and I’ll ride, reap, or rastle him for a
quid!” Modest silence. “I’ll fight him for a belly-
ful.” There were no takers; and when he tweaked
Big Mick by the nose, Mick responded with a weakly
smile, as if having his beak tweaked in public was
one of the most humorous things. However, Isaiah’s
prowess did not go untested. There was a black
“rig” in the paddock that no one had tackled suc-
cessfully, and next morning he was yarded by
Scotty Turnbull, the head groom. Not a seat was
vacant on the stockyard rails as Wild Wright
mounted, in moleskins and heavy-nailed boots. Most
expected to see him half killed, and Scotty smiled
appreciatively, but withheld judgment. For a few
seconds there was a wild whirl; the “rig” plunged,
reared, bucked, and bit all he knew; and then, solid
as a rock, Wild Wright started a series of yells,
bashed the animal over the head with his hat, kicked
him in 50 places, and finally handed him back to
the admiring Scotty, an exhausted, broken and
humble animal. There was no more fight in him
than there had been in the 50 shearers the day be-
fore. That night Bob Wilson arrived. Now, Robert
was a flash man, if you like —cabbage-tree hat, Bed-
ford cord pants fitting like a glove, and red silk
sash; and, besides having a considerable reputation
as a rider, “Flash Bob” had appeared in the ring a
few times. He would take that bumptious young
man down a peg! “Pooh! He may be strong,” he
said; “but he’s got no science.” So, at Sunday
breakfast, as Wright reached out for the brownie,
Wilson snatched it from his hand across the table.
“After your betters,” he said, insultingly. There
was no explanation on either side. Rising slowly
and calmly, Wright said “Come on,” and on the
level plain, where you could see the horizon half-way
round, with the creek gums for a background, they
faced one another. Flash Bob stripped to the buff,
a well-made, muscular man, in the pink of con-
dition; Wright, in shirt, moleskins and bluchers, as
he had ridden the “rig” the day before, made no
preparation, but stood with one arm awkwardly in
front of him like a semaphore. There were only two
blows struck, worth , mentioning; and Bob got both
of them. It took water from the creek to bring him
to after the second, and he was not comforted for
weeks. Shearers and rouseabouts went back to the
hut and played euchre till dinner-time, satisfied that
an edifying Sunday morning had been spent. Wild
Wright did not shear at Yanko that year (nor, in-
deed, any other year), as the super thought him a
bit too strenuous to have about the place.
* * * *
“Stingaree” : Here’s another “Yorky” yarn
that isn’t in.. Banfield’s, “Confessions. of a Beach-
comber.” Yorky ' had his beche-de-mer camp on
Hook Island, one of the Whitsunday Group. In his
employ was an aboriginal named Tommy, very skil-
ful in finding the favorite haunts of the sea-slug.
One afternoon Tommy left to look for bSche-de-
mer. When he did not return by night, Yorky be-
came anxious, and went in search. After walking
along the beach for several hours he gave up and re-
turned to camp. Next morning he was up at day-
light, and, the tide being out, he made for the reef.
After. walking over the coral boulders and splashing
through weed-infested pools, he came upon the dead
body of the blackfellow, with one leg imprisoned
within a big clam shell. Tommy had evidently
stepped into the open clam the preceding afternoon,
and when it closed on his leg he was unable to
escape, and was drowned by the rising tide. When
he told me the story, Yorky said the clam shell
fish was about the most dangerous of all the creatures
to be found on coral reefs. They lie wide open in
pools, and are often completely hidden by seaweed.
On one occasion the one-armed marvel had the sole
of his boot torn off by the springing of one of the
animal rat-traps that he happened to tread into on
a half-submerged reef.'

“Denys” : Tried the effect of music on a
gohanna. When I assailed him, he . had in his pos-
session a discarded' 31b. piece of beef. I got my
flute, and cautiously sat down within about 4ft.
He seemed appreciative from the first note/ dropped
his bundle and posed like a duck looking down the
neck of a bottle. His long, sinuous tongue shot in
and out like a magic piston; no doubt he was
greedily licking in the melodious strains of “The
Minstrel Boy.” His eyes scintillated and flashed
like miniature search-lights, and he showed real
affection for “Annie Laurie” and other sweet things.
The fright stayed the time out. Then he attached
the meat to his head, raised it on high, brought his
ultima-thule to the “Present! ” and the pageant
moved. Haven’t seen him since.

“Wyn de Wiff”: I herewith lodge an
appeal against the decision of “Mr. Justice Ocky”
that venomous snakes do not climb, on the
ground that it is in direct opposition to
the evidence accepted by the learned Judge
in the case of “Landlord v. Adam and Eve,”
an application for permission to cancel free-
hold selection for non-fulfilment of conditions. In
that case, which your Honor will find reported in
Genesis Law Reports, page 9, the defendant .Eve, in
her affidavit referring to the apple, deposed that “the
serpent beguiled me, and I did eat.” As your Honor
is well aware that there were no fruit-shops at the
time, the said serpent, who was joined as a party
to the proceedings, must have obtained the means
of beguilement by climbing. If that is clear to your
Honor, I will not pursue the point, except to ask
for costs on the highest scale.

“Ivory”: I agree with “Kuru” that bad
water has a lot to answer for when beri-beri is about,
but that rice is a factor has yet to be proved. I
have never been able to find evidence that too much
vegetable fobd of any kind is responsible. In the
camp of nearly every drover who brings stock from
the Territory into Queensland there is a case or two
of the disease. I know, because I have worked across
the Territory behind cattle, and had it. Its preval-
ence there certainly isn’t due to too much vegetable.
At nearly every camp, however, the water varies con-
siderably in quality, and the lime and other reprehen-
sible minerals in the springs probably do the mis-
chief. In mild cases, scurvy-grass, steeped in a billy
of boiling water, is good medicine; also it is useful
as a preventive. Potatoes and vinegar are a sure
cure, if the thing hasn’t gone too far. Cut the
spuds up raw, and make a salad of them with the
vinegar, or else boil them in the vinegar, and I’ll
guarantee they’ll shift the beri. Plenty of milk is
also helpful, and so are citrous fruits.
NECESSITY KNOWS NO LAW.
BAPTIST PARSON : “ Surely ALL the Baptists in the district haven't turned Methodists?"

“Quidam”: As an old hand among the
vegetable porcupine, I don’t think much of
“C.R.W.’s” suggestion (8.3/8/’ll) that ostrich farm-
ing might have a beneficial effect on the prickly-
pear country of Queensland, as the birds could be
largely fed on chopped pear-plant. There would have
to be about 40 million ostriches to destroy the present
supply of pear; and by the time Bananaland had
accumulated that many of the poultry, the pear
would be in a position to demand more; and by the
time the pear was exterminated, the market for
ostrich feathers would be busted. Apropos of vari-
ous creatures’ appetites for prickly pear, in drought
time goats and dairy cattle develop a partiality for
it, so logic might suggest another big drought as an
exterminator. As a matter of fact, every dairyman
who carts pear to his cattle generally plants as
much as he helps to destroy, per medium of
leaves, etc., dropped en route, and every bird that
eats the fruit assists in distributing the pest by
scattering the seeds. There is, in truth, no easy,
ostrichy way of wiping out the pear. It is a matter
requiring hard thinking and harder work and pro-
fanity. The profanity isn’t absolutely essential, but
it is absolutely inevitable.
“Berkeley” : The “vegetable caterpillar”
mentioned by “Jack Jay” (B. 27/7/’Il) is fre-
quently found in N. S. Wales and Vic. (and probably
elsewhere in Australia), generally in moist, thickly-
timbered country. Am inclined to question “J. J.’s”
statement that the spores of a fungus settle on the
grub. A more likely theory is that the caterpillar,
while feeding, swallows the spores of the fungus.
These germinate inside the grub, and the fungus
pushes forth a stem at the back of the head. The
unfortunate caterpillar is generally found upright in
the ground.

“Condamine”: “Smell” (B. 20/7/’ll) ex-
aggerates his steeplechasing adder’s performance
when he credits it with a seven yards jump—unless
it was down a shaft. The adder can certainly spring
in the manner described, but my own observations,
backed by the evidence of those who have had a
life’s acquaintance with the reptile, show that four
feet would be a long jump. Also, an adder is so
lively with his business-end, which isn’t the tail-end,
that one could easily bite “Smell’s” dog without
“Smell” seeing the performance.
SIGNS OF SPRING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
For years I watched The Bulletin
About the time that Springs begin,
In hope of rushing in an ode
Ere other bards blocked up the road.
In vain! I always found, instead,
A rival bard had got ahead.
So this year I sat down and plotted
How to get in ahead. I jotted
Several delightful, airy things,
Compound of all I know of Springs,
Down upon paper. “For,” thought I,
If I write Spring up in July,
I’ll give the others the go-by.”
Foresight most rare I showed in this ;
I wrote the Ode — and here it is I
How do we know it is Spring?
Spring In the city so bleak?
Hark to the band playing “GOr’save the King!”
Sales are commencing this week !
Pubs are being painted bright red,
Quite in a literal sense;
Even the Dagoes say Winter is dead—
Crayfish are up eighteenpence 1
How do we know it is Spring?
Isn’t the wind from the West,
Bearing sweet odors of Botany ling—
Odors we sniff at with zest?
Look at the shows in the shops!
Feel the keen edge of the air!
Watch the almighty and dignified cops
Chasing the Chow from his lair!
How do we know it is Spring?
Doesn’t it buzz in the blood?
Look at the sun with his eye in a sling
Trying to dry up the mud !
Mark the glad grocer at work,
Tying up salts with some string.
Getting up early continues to irk—
That is a sure sign of Spring!
Spring ? I should say it was Spring!
Look at the girl in the skirt;
Hobble they call it —and how does it cling!
Wouldn’t you think it would hurt?
What an astonishing hat!
Delicate primrose and pink;
What is it trimmed with on top, where it’s flat ?
Pickled nasturtiums, I think.
How do we know it is Spring ?
These are infallible signs!
Into The Bulletin basket they fling
Thousands and thousands of lines—
Tender, unfledged little odes,
Trifles that verge upon tears.
Monsters! But usage, like acid, corrodes —
They’ve been rejecting for yearsl
How do we know it is Spring,
Spring in the country so fair?
Bull-ants have started to sting—
Stingees have started to swear!
Cows have relented at last,
Letting down milk by the pail;
Look at the grass growing fast!
Look at our old cock’s new tail!
Fanners are rising at four —
Last month they roused out at five—-
Cow-slaves may slumber no more:
“Now, then, yous blanks! Look alive!”
Staggering out in the dark,
Bucket and dipper they bring;
Gracious ! What language—-just hark !
Everything says it is Spring!
S.B.W.
O. C. CABOT.
PARADISE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I think I want to go to Paradise,
Not for the sake of harping all day long,
Or lifting up my voice in happy song,
But chiefly to sit down where Peter plies
His job, and hear the old hands swapping lies.
Yes, I could loaf for aeons by the door
Or in the little lodge-room where St. Pete
Is hobbling in and out with gouty feet,
Admitting some to tread the golden floor,
But sending others downwards one stage more.
The energetic push can make a din
With concerts and processions and the rest;
But as for me, I think it will be best
A comfy nook in Peter’s house to win,
And sit for ever watching trains come in;
And meet old pals, and shake them by the hand,
Or give a “hoy” as they go farther down,
Hearing small gossip of my own old town—
Who keeps the pub? Who’s Mayor? You under-
stand !
No harping, but such loafing would be grand.
Around the gate where Peter holds hi§ court
The idle chaps of Paradise will flock,
Waiting to hear some well-known cobber knock,
And all eternity won’t spoil that sport
So long as the tobacco don’t run short.
VICTORIA.

Many friends in England, to view the Coronation
procession, arose at 3 a.m. In the dark, some lost
their way ; others arrived too late ; all were exposed
to weather inclemencies. We in Australia, however,
may choose our own time, an enjoyable seat and view
the procession at several vantage points. Cinemato-
graphy is to be thanked for this and entrepeneurs
will be thankful for improvements affecting cheap
non-inflammable films, or better projection. Phillips
Ormonde & Co., Patent Agents, 266 Flinders St.,
Melbourne.*

“Phil Harum” : “C.0.D.” (B. 3/8/’ll)
tells only half the story of the virtues of the ger-
anium as a snakebane. He forgot to mention that
it is as effective against imaginary serpents as it
is against the wriggling realities. If “C.0.D.” is in-
clined to doubt this, let him try a decoction of the
plant next time he has the jim-jams. He will be
able to furnish some interesting reading afterwards,
The stalks, crushed and applied in the form of a
poultice, also form a sovereign remedy for bursting
head. If all its virtues were as widely known as
they should be, there wouldn’t be any geranium left
to scare away the real snakes.

PLANS AND SPECIFICATIONS.
“Puketapu”: It was at the hour when
Dusk spreads her soft rug for the coming of Night
that I blundered across an apparently long-lost
whare on the East Coast of Maoriland. It was a
lonely place, with that distinctive feeling of death
about it which some deserted places have. It didn’t
take me long to see that here had been a pa. at one
time, and the remains of the huge old Maori ovens
set me wise to the death-feeling. Perhaps it was be-
cause I had lost myself that I was nervous; perhaps
it was the cold, hungry swish of the sea along
the darkened beach that wore on my nerves. Doesn’t
matter; I managed to get to sleep. It must have
been a couple of hours later, when an almost naked
Maori warrior walked into the hut and called me to
follow him. Massive and grim he strode towards the
ovens near the beach, with me at his heels; and as
we topped a sand-rise I saw a sight that stayed the
flow of blood in my veins. A war party had returned
with a canoe-load of prisoners, and these doomed
wretches, were being butchered and gutted like
sheep—brained where they stood helpless by breath-
hissin'g, blood-splashed demons, and put into the ovens.
Already some had begun the feast, squatting round
the smoking banquet of corpse, hacking at it and
tearing it with their dogs’ teeth. One who bit at
an underdone foot had the blood running down his
jaw. And all the time they laughed and jested, and
threw bones or scraps of unchewable sinew at each
other, like so many devils kicked out of Sheol as
incurable. Then an awful sickness swept over me, and
I dropped. It was bright day when I came round, and
everything was the same as on the previous evening,
except that, instead of waking inside the hut where I
had gone to sleep, I was lying on my face near an
oven, and the fresh marks of a canoe that had been
hauled upon the beach were where I expected to find
them.
“Little Plain”: “Ah” (B. 27/7/T1) asks
why any old tin used instead of the orthodox billy
is called a “Whiteley King” ? During the big
shearers’ strike the “free” laborers, sent by Whiteley
and Co., to take the place of the shearers on strike,
almost invariably sported an old treacle pot, or some
similar makeshift thing, presumably from motives of
economy. Also, any particularly despicable sort of
a swag, composed of pieces of bag, discolored calico,
etc., is widely honored by that same illustrious name,
and for • the same reason.
* * * *
“Billy Bluegum” : It is only in the trap-
per-infested localities that the fox is decried as the
pastoralist’s enemy. In many of the western
(N.S.W.) districts the bonus on fox scalps has been
discontinued, as there is ample proof that Reynard
is Bunny’s natural enemy, not on account of the
number he eats, but because his tactics lend them-
selves tb the cannibalistic proclivities of the buck
rabbit. Instinctively alive to this weakness in Pa,
the doe excavates a shallow dug-out,- some distance
from home, in which she conceals her young, keep-
ing the entrance carefully closed during her absence,
till the kittens are able to shift for themselves. Here
they easily fall a prey to the keen-scented fox. The
doe has thus been driven to make the parental roof-
tree her lying-in hospital; but she is no better off, as
few, if any, of the babies escape the buck. The fox
will not trouble sheep to any appreciable extent
while rabbits are plentiful; but when they become
scarce —well, he is more easily got rid of than the
rabbit. By the way, he does not live up to his Old
World reputation for cunning, as he is more easily
poisoned than any of our native animals. His tail
is built into the wrong end of him, for it’s his
brains that are most in need of a brush.
* * * *
“The Pariali”: Re “Wang’s” bet (B.
27/7/’ll) that there is no harder Australian wood
than the black wattle to cut in a dry state. Has
“Wang” ever met the fiddle-stick blackwood ? When I
first went to the survey camps, the assistant told me
to cut two pegs out of a stump he pointed out. At
the first collision about half the axe-head flew into the
next State, and for 10 minutes I couldn’t be pt?r-
suaded that I hadn’t got into an electric battery.
Beach Forest (Vic.) has a yarn that Alcoclc’s tried
some of its fiddleback for a billiard table. It says
something for the grit of Australian workmen that
the table was made; but every tool introduced to the
wood agate was chipped out of recognition. Still, I
must say this for it; if there is any Victorian wood
that takes a higher polish, or presents a finer surface,
I would like to make its acquaintance.
“Altair”: Re “Jack Jay” and the vege-
table caterpillar. This member of the Fusion party
exists in Australia and a lot of other places. I
have found stray specimens here in Central Queens-
land, but they were not so large as the Maoriland
samples. The mature form of the grub is a wi-nged
insect, and it is widely distributed, as Darwin dis-
covered most flying creatures to be. Presumably the
moth carries the fungus spores, or at least the fun-
goid diathesis, with it, and thus propagates and
spreads the vegetable half of the Fusion.

“Picton”: Takes a lot to disturb William
Wagtail. Last year a couple built their nest on a
wire stay attached to the chimney of an open school
shed, and the lady with the oscillating rudder dis-
charged her maternal duties in public, without ex-
hibiting a blush, while her partner filled in his time
making periodic assaults upon a magpie that had
thoughtlessly elected to build in the vicinity. One
night, just about the time when the lady was “ex-
pecting,” a cyclone wandered into the shed, lost its
bearings, and distributed half the roof throughout
the district. Next morning I was surprised to
find the nest, and more surprised to discover no
traces of excitement in the parent birds. The
gentleman apparently attributed the whole business
to a frolic on the part of the magpie, and was busy
organising himself into a punitive expedition, while
bis missus merely expressed a few regrets that Social-
ism was spreading. The subsequent repairs to the
roofing iron raised a din like the National Debt
falling off Kosciusko, but the parties with the vi-
bratory steering gear never ceased in their atten-
tions to the offspring, which were reared and fed in
the daily presence of a constant stream of inquisitive
children.

“F.C.”: So “Wang” (B. 27/7/T1) thinks
black wattle the hardest Australian wood to cut, dry.
Well, I’ve got a few black wattles in my paddock,
and I mostly get light exercise Sunday mornings by
cutting up a tree or two for the week’s firewood. I
won’t bet “Wang” drinks, but if he happens along,
I’ll lay him where I lay other suburban swaggies : —•
on to a slab of yellow box, about the size of a boy.
This grew near here (Melbourne suburbs), and sup-
ported a neighbor’s tank for two decades. The
splinter has broken two axes and the hearts of
several travellers who thoughtlessly tackled it; and
I would dearly love to get “Wang” on to it.
“Nell Cook:” “Wee MacGregor” (B.
27/7/’ll) wants to know why dogs delight to roll
in anything particularly stinksome. Pride! Dirty,
snobbish pride! The animal has no joy in fine
clothes, to call forth the envy of his fellows; gold
is no use to him; meat is his only wealth. So he
rolls in the stale article to convince the other dogs
that he has so much tucker that it is valueless in
his eyes, and he can afford to roll in it. From the
circumstance of dogs doing this, arose the old
expression, “stinking pride.” Anyone may notice
the supercilious airs of the dog thus anointed, and
the crushed expression of others that have not been
lucky enough to meet with the odoriferous patch.
» * * *
“FeTicer” : Can arrive at only one opinion,
after reading the statement of “Salt Beef and
Spuds” that bloodwood and stringybark are not
long-lasting—-he is raw. Practically every fence on
the Tweed (N.S.W.) is either bloodwood or red
stringy; and some of them are older than the
Oldest Inhabitant. Moreover, the former wood does
not burn freely, and fire will not touch the latter
with a long flame. Finally, white ants rarely do
business with either of them. As for the advice to
use teak for fencing—well, with that timber at 17s.
per 100 superficial feet, what do you think? “Salt
Beef and Spuds” ought to get back to his restaurant.
“Eddyson” : This is a story of Pat Macka-
boy’s. Pat had descended to manual labor in a weak
moment, and was scratching mullock in the Old Hoss
for 7s. 6d. a shift. Going home one morning, after
night-shift, he fell upon a marauder mauling his
belongings in the hut. The intruder had a blanket
spread on the floor, and in it was an .assortment of
Pat’s valuables. The hut-breaker was a lean, fair-
to-middling, 12st. blackguard, with a broken nose
and a vivacious eye. When Mackaboy fell in upon
him, he made no demonstration, offered no apology.
“This lets me out,” he said, and sat down on the
bunk. “No,” said Patrick, “it lets you in. Seven
months is the minimum.” “Sorry to put you to
so much trouble,” said the man. Mackaboy recol-
lected that a trial would mean great aggravation.
“See here,” he said, “you can have 3'our choice: I
get to you, or whistle up a cop.” The man on the
bunk was prompt. “Get to me,” he said. He took
it like a lamb ; right and left for the jaw, a rip, and
a hearty kick by way of stern admonition. Then
Pat went cold on it. He found it dull work pasting
an impassive resister. “Don’t stint yourself,” sa'd
the cheerful burglar. “Have another turn.” “It isn’t in
me,” Mackaboy replied. “Scoot! Get over the hill."
The party with the googly nose thanked Pat politely
for a square deal, and took the direct route for else-
where. Two months later Patrick Mackaboy went
up to Launceston to see the State champion smothered
in three rounds by Six-finger Rigg. That battle
impressed Patrick profoundly. . The winner was a
man-eater, and P. Mackaboy recognised him at once
as the broken-nosed hero of the hut.

“Australia! - ”: Caterpillars have been
making things lively on the Northern railway tracks
in S. Aus., especially, on the Broken Hill line, be-
tween Petersburg and Cockburn, where trains have
been held up daily. Even the express has been
delayed for hours by the crawlers; and, in a des-
perate struggle to keep the rails clear, engine-drivers
have been fixing stable brooms, bottle envelopes, and
other queer devices on to the cow-catchers.

“A.G.E.” : I can give both “Quidam” (B-
-8/G/’IX) and “Hecagus” (B. 6/7/’ll) a better recip°
for scaring cockatoos from a corn patch, or galah-
from a wheat field. Dissolve some strychnine in
pure vinegar or acetic acid, which will take about
10 minutes; then pour boiling water on.it. Ds-
tribute loz. to a bushel. This saves trouble for
everybody but the visitors.

“Mosgiel” : Saw a strange case of foster-
parentage some time ago which may interest other:?
as much as it did me. Was stopping at, a hou>e
where one of the inmates was a small fox-terrier
pup, taken from his mother weeks too early ; another
inmate was a Manx cat. The cat. whose name va?
Bill, gave birth to five kittens, two of which were
promptly drowned. Bill thereupon accidentally, or
by design, lay on the other three and smother-?
them. After waiting long enough to be sure of their
deadness, she adopted the pup. For five weeks she was
all to that pup that a mother should be. Then an-
other pup, visiting ours, explained that cats were
possessed of devils, and illustrated the manner o
casting them out. Between them they nearly kilk
Bill; and after that the pup could get nothin?
better than water with his lunch.
PURELY A MATTER OF TASTE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
mission station by the sea
Sent out the Reverend Jabez Jills
o find a tribe well known to be
ound up in blind idolatry—
Far inland where the Wondee spills
Its waters down the azure hills.
His Jills was plump and not too old.
lie took his hymn-book and a guide,
And started out with spirit bold
And resolute to find his fold —•
To scatter idols far and wide,
Arid humble all the heathen’s pride.
The guide rode back at length, full spent.
He bore a box of curious plan ;
With it a message had been sent:
“We have no use for Reverend Gent.
Returned herewith; for, to a man,
This tribe is vegetarian!”
