Apropos the branch business which Henry
Parkes founded in 1850 at Geelong- (Vic.)
for the sale of “billiard and bagatelle balls,
humming tops, children’s rattles, etc.” (B.
17/8/T1). Parkes’s manager was Angus
Mackay, who afterwards became proprietor
of the Bendigo Advertiser and Minister of
Mines. It was the same Angus who
founded the Sydney Daily Telegraph, hop-
ing to make a strong Protectionist paper of
it. But he burned his fingers, and flew
back to his comfortable Bendigo property.
A recent visitor to these parts is John
Cox, for over 20 years a science professor at
the great McGill University in Canada. He
has retired on a pension, and, aided by the
Carnegie millions, is employing his leisure
in travel among the world’s universities. He
finds those of Australia so surprisingly up
to date that he probably wonders why they
go to third-rate European universities for
their teaching staffs. Talking of radium,
the retired professor admits that, so far, its
discovery has not resulted in anything very
tangible, except as a possible cure in some
cases of superficial cancer, though not very
much is known about even that.
Victoria’s Acting-Premier Willie Watt,
the man who is coming, made a sort of pil-
grimage the other day to his birthplace—
Barfold, 10 miles from Kyneton. The cot-
tage in which he was born has gone, and
only a solitary poplar marks the little
estate. Willie was the youngest of 11 chil-
dren.
William Jeans, who was an Argus re-
porter from 1865 to 1569, but then went
back to the English Parliamentary Gallery,
has just retired after being for some
years the Father of the Gallery. The
Argus twice obtained conspicuous men
from London as reporters. The first time it
got Aspinall and C. A. Smyth, barristers,
who soon took to the Bar. Jeans belonged
to a second little batch.
John Foster Fraser is the Parliamentary
sketch-writer for the London Standard.
and during the recess he always goes to some
outlandish place to collect material for a
book and a lecture. Australia was the dark
and inaccessible spot he favored last year.
This year he went with a camel caravan to
the Sahara regions behind Morocco, Algiers,
and Tunis. The inevitable book is to be
called “The Land of the Veiled Women.”
John Cash, one-time sergeant in the old
43rd Regiment of the line, answered the
roll-call at Pambula (N.S.W.) a few days
days back. lie saw service during long-
ago troubles in suffering Erin ; pursued Ram
Dham during the Mutiny; met that first-
class fighting man, the Zulu, in his most
aggressive mood; and exchanged discourte-
sies with the Maori before settling down to
cultivate peace and other products in N. S.
Wales.
G. R. Nichols, of St. Mary’s (N.S.W.),
who has been appointed hon. librarian to
the Pioneers’ Club, is a grandson of Isaac
Nichols, first gazetted postmaster of Syd-
ney. Isaac was a clerk to “Campbell of the
Wharf” when he was given the job by
Lieut.-Goveruor Foveaux in April, 1809.
Governor Macquarie confirmed him in the
appointment in June, 1810. The post office
was at Mr. Nichols’s own residence in Lower
George-street, just about where the fire-sta-
tion now stands. He was paid at per
letter.
“The Undersigned”: Premier Verran’s re-
cent mention of Judas Iscariot and some
comments thereupon remind me of an
episode that occurred in the Melb.
City Court 20 years or so ago. when
a Syrian who swore that Judas Is-
cariot was his name was charged with
hawking without a license. Call,
P.M., fined him 30s. (the usual fine
in those days was much less). Judas’s
solicitor produced two
coins, and sent someone
out for change. On the
return of the messenger,
the lawyer walked over
to the clerk’s desk and
slapped dbwn 30 single
shillings, which closed
the incident. I looked
next day for some in-
dignant comment upon
the profane comedy, but
none was. forthcoming,
and the pious Melbourne
Daily Telegraph was
alive in those times, too.
On Partridge Island
—a lone, bleak speck
down the Channel in
Southern Tasmania— :
there live a couple who
follow life’s procession
with indifference. They
are the only humans on
the island, and civilisa-
tion means a long sail
for them. Sometimes the
husband varies the mon-
otony by taking on a bit
of timber-lumping at one
of the Huon ports, and
then the missus looks
after the billows of the
Antarctic by herself. If
she goes short of any
victuals, she hoists sail
and beats her way over
to some small port, picks
up her packet of pins
„ , , and turnips, and is then
off home to her lonely castle.
A pressman’s farewell:—
.Patrick Francis Moran, who hung his Cardinal’s
ha.t on the Peg of Eternity last week, was a good
friend to pressmen in more ways than one. He was
a never-ending source of good “copy,” and about
the only public man (for he was a public man)
outside leading Federal or State Ministers that a
chief-of-staff would never take a risk with. A body-
guard of pressmen followed him wherever he went,
and if they didn’t get something to flavor the
public’s breakfast coffee next morning, things could
be counted slack. Nothing pleased him better than
to be interviewed on a “hot” subject, but it was
not easy to pass the barriers that hedged him
‘Your Eminence,” said a scribbler to him on one
occasion, “there are times when I must see you or
suffer m office reputation, so if I bring a shot-gun
with me next time, and leave a trail of the slaugh-
tered behind me till I reach this room, I hope you’ll
understand that the exigencies of dfity are the cause
and that no ill-will is intended.” The great Church-
man laughed softly in that happy manner lie had of
eipoying a joke with his whole body, and that press-
man s name was thenceforward put “on the doors,”
so to speak. The old gentleman’s one grievance was
against the nressman who misreported him ; but the
remarkable thing is that he was not more frequently
“taken” inaccurately. During the past few vears, it
was often almost impossible to hear him distinctly,
unless quite close to him, and time and again daily-
paper men were saved trouble by the notes of the
reporters for the Catholic weeklies, whose ears by
constant practice, were tuned to the modulations of
his voice. He will be missed bv the ink-slingers
and last Saturday, when I passed through the crowds
before the Cathedral, where his body lay, all undis-
turbed by the sobbing of the muffled bell above him,
I saluted a lost friend of the craft.
Australian swimmer Harold Hardwick was
at latest teaching the Prince of IVales the
crawl stroke. What, is “Sailor George”
going to hold court at sea?
THE WALKING-MANS POINT
OF VIEW.
THE TRAMP: “ Well with all their inventions,
they'll never beat the old-fashioned dust
storms! ”
Buried with military honors in Adelaide,
Pierce Purcell, who went through the In-
dian Mutiny, settled in Melbourne, volun-
teered for the Maori wars and had three
years of them, and then joined the Maori-
land constabulary. His favorite recollec-
tion was the chase of the “flying column’’
after Te Kooti —a procession which lasted
for months.
The only unkind thing so far said about
Lady Denman by Society writers appeared
in the dear old Australasian. Lady Den-
man went to see “Lady Frederick,” and the
writer who gushed over her Ex.’s clothes,
etc., remarked that her hair was “beauti-
fully-colored,” which was a cruel thing to
say of an Excellency whose thatch is above
suspicion.
Tom Gill, S. Australia’s Under-Treasurer,
is the least spectacular and about the most
useful of its public officials. He runs a big-
banking business —for on the security of
Treasury bonds there are millions invested
with the Government—and he also keeps
every Treasurer on the straight and
narrow path. As a side line, he looks after
the finances of the Municipal Tramway
Trust for the State, which advanced over a
million to buy out the horse-trams and instal
electricity. Likewise he is the authority on
the early history of S.A., and has written
several books on the subject, and he is now
producing a biography of Colonel Light, the
founder of Adelaide. His bibliography of
S.A. —now, unfortunately, 25 years out of
date —is the only thing of its kind attempted
in the State.
After pondering the matter, and consider-
ing whether Sol Bloom should be stopped
from becoming a member of the Victorian
Lawyers’ Union because lie had honestly
earned something towards his keep while he
was qualifying, the Full Court decided to let
Bloom blossom into a wig. A successful
N. S. Wales practitioner had a similar nar-
row escape. He had earned his board bill
by fluting or something in a paid orchestra,
and one learned Judge, who had got a hint
of it, asked whether the applicant hadn’t
been guilty of earning money. “Yes. that
is so, your Honor,” said his ready godfather ;
he went as a private to the Soudan, and
earned great distinction as well as a little
pay.” After that exhibition of the appli-
cant’s “patriotism”—well, how could a
Judge pursue the matter any further?
His Australian experiences changed many
of Cardinal Moran’s views. Thus, when he
was Bishop of Ossory, he was regarded in
Ireland as an opponent of Home Rule —he
had been reared in the reactionary political
cult of his uncle, Cardinal Cullen, of Dublin.
It was not until the Cardinal had
thoroughly experienced the blessing of self-
government in Australia that he adopted
the same principle for Ireland. A reference
to this -was once made by Acting-Premier
Holman at a Patrick’s Day celebration in
Sydney. Commenting on the great advance
ot the Home Rule cause, Holman made the
sensational statement that even the chair-
man (the Cardinal) had been converted to
Home Rule. The Cardinal demurred, but
Mr. Holman quoted an article written by
the Cardinal in the Nineteenth Century,
Thereafter, it was arranged to do away with
impromptu speeches at the festival, and an
invitation is now sent to one public man to
deliver a prepared address.
“Tamaso” on an ancient dispute:
“Oldest Inhabitant” (B. 10/8/’ll) evidently has
the impression that Margaret Catchpole and' Mrs.
Reiby were one and the same person. Such was not
the case. The former came out in the ship Nile and
the latter in the British Admiral •
Mrs Reiby’s name then was Marv
Haydock. Margaret Catchpole was
never married, nor had she any
children. She died in 1819 on the
banks of the Hawkesbury, where she
kept a little shop. She was buried
in Richmond churchyard, as can
be verified by the register of
St. Peter’s Church, where it is
distinctly stated she was a single
woman. Cobbold, in his book, shows
that he had the same impression with
regard to Margaret and Mrs. Reibv
being identical; he thought she hail
married her old sweetheart Barrv,
and had adopted a thin disguise by
ehanging the name to Reiby. Mar-
garet wrote to the Cobbold familv
regularly up to the time of her death.
She also sent a case of Australian
bird-skins to the Rev. Mr. Cobbold ;
he had them stuffed, and presented
them to the Norwich Museum. When
the Cobbolds heard from Margaret
no more, they made some sort of en-
quiries, from which they were led to
believe she was Mrs. Reiby, and a
wealthy woman. As a matter of fact,
she was then dead ; hence her silence.
On the assumption that Margaret had
become Mrs. Reiby, and did not wish
to further communicate with him.
Mr. Cobbold was pretty rough on her
in his book. There is little doubt
this book was the original cause of
the two women being so generally
regarded as one. Years ago the mat-
ter was debated in the papers, and
those who maintained that Mrs.
Reiby and Margaret .Catchpole were
identical wished to make evidence
out of the fact that Mrs. Reiby had
many descendants in Sydney, some of
them in the highest positions, and
not one of thorn had contradicted
the assertion. They had a very valid
reason for not doing so, which I will
not mention here. Finally, Margaret
Catchpole was old enough to be Mrs.
Reiby’s mother.
Councillor W. R. Cooper, the municipal
G.O.M. of Victoria, is retiring from the
oversight of drains and dead-dogs at the end
of his present term. The old gentleman,
who sits for a Fitzroy ward, is within a few
weeks of 80, and his experience of roads and
bridges and parish pumps dates back to 1856,
when he represented the McArthur ward in
the municipality of Melbourne. Later he
went into the country and did some muni-
cipal pioneering, but a boom in building
brought him back to Melbourne, where lie
tirade a tidv pile. The municipal fever runs
in the veins of the Coopers. This one’s
father was a member of the Lambeth Coun
cil in the foggy township, and there are
other relatives who have been mayors and
thinvs in the Cold Country.
THE LATE CARDINAL MORAN.
Photo, by New Falk Studios , Sydney.
The widow of the late P. N. Russell,
whose will has been proved at £176,000,
leaves a third of her estate to Dr. Barnado’s
homes, another third to the Church Army,
and the balance to a sister. Her late hus-
band, Peter Nicol Russell, handsomely en-
dowed the Engineering school of Sydney Uni-
versity ; and there was a lingering hope, now
shattered, that his widow would recognise
his interest in the University when dispos-
ing of the fortune he had left her.
THE NEW DEVIL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
1 dreamed to-night the Devil came
And sat beside my bed —
Not like the thing the churches shame,
Nor garbed in flaming red,
But clad in black,
And, set well back,
A topper on his head.
No lank and hollow cheek had he,
No cloven hoof nor horn,
And on his linen, I could see,
No trace of soot was borne.
A great big paunch,
A mighty haunch,
His body did adorn.
He fixed me with a piggish eye,
And talked an hour or so —
Was glad, he said, that rents were high,
And wages still were low;
He yarned of meat,
And wool and wheat,
And wheezed, and would not go.
Then clearly I remembered him,
And cursed him where he sat;
I knew him by each portly limb,
His bright and shining hat.
“Great Scot, ’tis true,
If giv’n his due
The Devil then is Fat?”
He chuckled with convulsive glee,
And chortled: “That is so.
The whole of Hell belongs to me;
Nick borrowed on the show,
And was deposed
When I foreclosed
A hundred years ago ! ”

When the Melbourne land boom was on the point of
bursting, this co. had £250,000 of paid capital, and
it owed £994 764 on debentures. Practically all
of this had been advanced against landed properties
at boom valuations. When the bubble broke the
securities were thrown on to the co.’s hands whole-
sale. There was no hope of selling them at their
book values; in other words, there was no hope of
getting 20s. in the £, though the co. was expected
to hand its creditors their full pound of flesh. In
the circumstances the shareholders had to face a
dreary prospect of calls. The co. at the time was
in 250,000 shares of £5, paid to £1 ; and share-
holders thus carried a liability of £1,000,000.
First, two calls of £1 each were made, under
arrangement with the debenture-holders, and then
another of 10s., also in consideration of the deben-
ture-holders keeping the bailiff out of the premises.
Unfortunately, many shareholders couldn’t play the
game of back up or forfeit, so, of the original
250,000 shares, only 145,770 are now alive—-the other
104,230 have been forfeited. On the whole lot
£2 10s. has been written off, and that and the re-
purchase of debentures at a discount has allowed a
deal of writing-down to be done; and at the written-
down values, so many sales have been made that to-
day all the and other debts have been
paid off. Moreover, things have improved so much
since the last writing-down that in the aggregate
the subsequent sales have shown a surplus. While
there were any debentures outstanding this surplus
had to be held as a contingency or guarantee fund.
Now, however, that the shareholders are quite free,
it has been decided to cut up the accumulated profit.
There is 6s. per share of this money available ; and
with it goes back to the shareholders 2s. per share
of capital. From this it looks as though the co.
has given up all idea of having a second time on
earth as a new-business concern.
The following comparison shows the position now
and as it was when the last reconstruction was
agreed to: —
Liabilities.
1907. 1911/
Capital £113,567 £142,688
Debentures .. .. 318,703 Nil
Other debts .. .. 1,663 9
Assets.
Advances .. .. 298,629 88,538
Properties .. .. 124.579 40,774
Cash, etc 38,798 62,705
For the most part the advances are money outstand-
ing on properties sold on terms—and mostly, it
seems, not on very long terms. The first table shows
what surplus has been realised on the sales. Further,
some provision apart from the contingency fund be-
fore mentioned has been made for doubtful debts;
the extent of this provision is, however, not known.-
There may be a trifle to come to the shareholders
on this account. On the other hand, there is no
certainty that the £40,774 of property in hand will
fetch £40,774. If it does, and if the other assets
(advances, etc.) are worth just their book value,
then the capital is, of course, intact; that is to say,
the £1 paid share is worth £l, plus its proportion
of the £49,320 of reserves, or, together, a penny or
so under £1 7s. per share. (This is before the dis-
tribution to be made this month.) But, obviously,
after the distribution, £1 worth of shares (book
value) won’t be worth £1 present value, since no
dividend is being paid. Still, the discount shouldn’t
be large, because some profit is being earned. I-n
appraising the true value of the shares, the un-
known quantities are-—(1) the money that the sedi-
ment of unsold assets will bring in; (2) the time
that shareholders will be out of their money pending
the realisation of the last asset. But no doubt money
will be cut up as it becomes available —that is, as-
suming that liquidation has been fully determined
upon.

This co. has been doino- exceedingly well during the
past 18 months. Of course the general prosperity of
the city has had a great deal to do with it. But
improved management and organisation has been an
important factor; for, after all, General Prosperity
isn’t a Santa Claus warrior who goes round dropping
money into your pockets—you at least have to hold
out your hand to grab it. Also, the co. dashed into
the catering business at a time when that line be-
came unprecedentedly profitable in Sydney. And
there isn’t a business in which management counts
for more than in this of food-supply; the profits
the good manager makes are the waste that the bad
manager permits. Further, the meat-export trade
has been large, on account of thq good seasons and
the big surplus of stock ; and that means good profits
for this co.’s refrigerating branch. Finally, this co.
is a big ice-maker, and the ice-makers arrived at
an honorable understanding some time ago, where-
upon the usual thing happened with regard to price.
These various things explain the much bigger profit.
And they also suggest that drought and bad times
would shrivel up the profits considerably. As the
table shows, the co. had a revaluation of assets
about seven years ago, and, in order to make assets
and liabilities balance, it had to write off £118,293
of capital and reserves. Things in Australia weren’t
bright when that valuation was made, and no doubt
there was a subsequent recovery in many of the
assets. That gave the co. a good start; it meant
perhaps that the eo. hasn’t written off revenue to
cover depreciation as vigorously as it would other-
wise have had to do. Still, there can be no ques-
tion about the progress having been very solid. This
shows it better:—
June, ’O4 Dec., ’lO June, ’ll
Liquid assets (inc. stock) £42,267 £64,880 £74,027
Liabilities .. . . 31,188 16,410 23,432
Surplus . . £11,079 £48,470 £50,595
That means that in the seven years since ' ie clean-
ing-up, the co. has (1) paid its steadily- creasing
dividends, (2) paid for all repairs, rent Us and
additions to plant, and (3) put by £39,516 1 liquid
assets,. which are more than half b.ank dep its and
Government stock. Whether the general -precia-
tion of plant has been fully counterbalance! by the
amount spent on plant renewals and additions there
is no way of telling; but, in view of the business
extensions of the past few years, it would lie sur-
prising if more has not been spent than would cover
the decline in value. The figures therefore look
exceedingly good; they show that profits could
slump very much, and still very comfortably pai"
the average dividend of the past seven years. In
fact, the amount added to the surplus of liquid assets
is equal to an average 6 p.c. per annum on the
capital, making, with the average dividend paid, a
total of over 134 p.c. p.a.

Boom, boom, boomj The Bananaland Government
is borrowing hard, and most of the money it is bor-
rowing it is putting into railways and railway plant;
and that’s where this co. comes in. Moreover, when
the Government borrows and lashes out, most pri-
vate people are borrowing (if they can) and also
lashing out; and this co. comes in there too. And it
isn’t all borrowing, of course. The State has had a
succession of wonderful pastoral seasons; and
the sugar business has been flourishing. Also,
though Queensland hasn’t been doing anything
extraordinary in a mining way, it has been
spending a lot of money on plant—at Mount
Morgan, at Cloncurry, on the Etheridge and
elsewhere. All this, too, has played right into the
hands of this old-established engineering co.—this and
the more reasonable duty imposed nowadays on such
goods as _ the co. produces. It has, however, been
too long in the business to expect to go on showing
such figures. In 12 years under the Queensland
State tariff it paid a total of 41 p.c., equal to only
£3 Bs. 4d. p.c. p.a., and in 10 years under the
Commonwealth tariffs it has paid in all 24 p.c.,
equal to a miserable £2 Bs. p.c. p.a.; and, though
it may expect to do better now, it has a lot of lee-
way to make up before its shareholders reach a fair
average. Moreover, though the tariff may be more
reasonable now, prosperity is very liable to come bv
fits and starts—it always is that way with construc-
tional activity. So the wise man won’t build much
on the boom profit of the past year. The co. has
throughout been very prudently handled, by the look
of things; when it was doing poorly it dropped its
dividend rather than not provide for depreciation.
That is to say, it didn’t scatter its doubtful profits
and then go to the bank for money to pay for plant
repairs and renewals. Consequently its financial
position is very sound; before paying the current
dividend it had £75,476 in liquid assets, including
over £45,000 in cash and “invested funds,” and its
total liabilities were £15,267. It is thus in an ex-
cellent position to meet the demands which are
sure to arise for more plant; it can finance its jobs
without paying dearly for the accommodation ; and it
can meet any ordinary contingency with as much
cheerfulness as almost anj r manufacturing concern in
Australia.

There is no law in Maoriland to stop a co. from
combining the trustee and investment business; and
investment in such a case nearly always means specu-
lation. This co., for instance, has liabilities (mostly
deposits) amounting to £182,773, and practically
the \yhole of that amount is out on mortgage• the
capital and reserves are represented by freeholds and
leaseholds and Corporation and Harbor Board deben-
tures. Mortgages are shrinkable securities; but, no
matter how much they shrink, this alleged trustee
co. is expected to repay the depositors 20s. in the
£. Thus capital which ought to be available for
the protection of the beneficiaries under trusts may
be wholly absorbed in meeting losses made in the
loan and mortgage business. It need not be sug-
gested that a co. with trust funds in hand would
ever apply them improperly, supposing it found
itself pinched by a sudden slump in the property
market, followed by a sudden demand on the part
of depositors for their money. But a co. never
ought to be able to get into a position where such
a thing was even possible. This co. should either
drop the trustee business altogether, or pass the
branch on to some other concern ; and simultane-
ously the law should make it illegal for any co. doing
trustee work to raise money by deposits or other-
wise for the purpose of loaning it out again. As
previously remarked, the co. has liabilities amount-
ing to £182,773, including £165,465 of deposits.
The liquid assets (bank balance and cash in hand)
total £1354 ; probably the Corporation and Harbor
Board debentures (£22,939) should be included,
though these things are only more or less liquid.
The cash position is therefore wretchedlv weak;
evidently the co. leans its full weight upon some
bank, and the favor of a bank is a doubtful stick
to put one’s full weight on.

The activity in the Sydney property market is
i making this old-established co.’s figures look good;
and, though the dividend has been again put up a
point, the big deficit is being whittled down fam-
ously. Originally, when the business founded by
the two ex-bank managers was sold to this co., the
latter paid £57,500 for the goodwill; that is to
say, of the £60,000 of capital, all but £2500 was
represented by nothing that you could touch, or
smell, or taste, or see, except with the eye of Faith.
That was just upon 23 years ago. The management
prudently set to work to replace this paper asset by
something more substantial; and, though the ac-
cumulation has been slow, it is growing like a snow-
ball, not only because of the better business, but be-
cause there is now over £50,000 in liquid assets
earning interest —which means (say) £IBOO a
year to go towards increasing the profit and wiping
out the deficit. At the present rate of progress, the
deficit will have entirely disappeared in three or four
years; and then the capital will be all represented
by liquid or tangible assets. They will presumably
earn from 34 to 4 p.c. ; so, even though the profit
from ordinary business should shrink —as it is bound
to do when the present property boom eases —a
comfortable dividend should be assured, assuming, of
course, that the funds are safely invested.

This co. is only just holding its own on a 4 p.c. basis.
As the table shows, the total amount added to re-
serves in seven years is a trifling £2298; and it is
even then doubtful whether enough has been written-
off to provide against current wear and tear and
decay. That is a point, however, about which no-
thing definite can be said. One thing that has to be
remembered is that the co., whose own capital is
£50,000, owes £36,865 on debentures; and though
the property represented by the debenture money is
no doubt depreciating just as fast as the rest, the
debt remains as good as new. In five years the total
liabilities have been reduced only from £39,356 to
£38,007, and the liquid assets haven’t been in-
creased worth mentioning. The co.’s trouble is
cheap electrical competition, municipally-owned, and
that is likely to increase and become keener rather
than otherwise. It is very sad for the shareholders,
no doubt; but the vastly greater number of the
public look at the matter from quite another point
of view. The fact is that the publicly-owned body
is, doing as well as it wants to do when it makes 4
p.c. net; therefore it reduces the selling price of its
goods, or whatever it has to supply, when more than
4 p.c. net is shown consistently. But the private
concern thinks it is only just beginning to make a
living when it makes 4 p.c. And the investor who
doesn’t remember that fact when he cuts into some-
thing that is liable to public competition, has less
regard for his widow than he mostly thinks he has
for his wife.

Two and a-half years ago this co. went on strike against
an award of the Commonwealth Arbitration Court,
and shut up its mine for two years. There may,
however, have been method in its madness. It had
found what looks like the bottom of the mine in
the north end, and, though there was still a con-
siderable quantity of ore left in the south end, ad-
joining Block 10, the co. couldn’t afford to use it
up rapidly. _ For this reason: it had just decided to
go into business in a large way as a purchaser and
smelter of other cos.’ ore; and it is very dangerous
for a co. to attempt that business unless it has a
good reserve of ore of its own on which it can draw
if outside supplies run short. If it must buy or
shut up, the chances are that those who have stuff
to sell will be able to extract a price that may leave
very little profit for the smelter. Moreover, metal
prices were poor; so, taking one thing with another,
the directors decided to close the mine, and they
kept it closed for two years. It was only in Feb-
ruary of this year that they re-opened it, and then
only on day shift. When a mine works only one
shift, the administration and other standing charges
are, of course, heavy in proportion to ore extracted;
on the other hand, men simply cannot do good work
in a stope where toil goes on all round the clock —
the air never gets a chance to become even fairly
pure. _ The increased gross profit for the past half-
year is no doubt the result of this partial resump-
tion of mining. But naturally the co. doesn’t show
what part of the profit came from its own ore, and
what from the purchased ore. The aggregate out-
turn of lead and silver was as follows:—
Lead —-Tons. Silver —Oz.
Nov., ’lO .. . . 44,554 .. 2,688,800
May, ’ll .. . . 39,509 . . 2,307,014
Thus the increased profit was achieved in the face
of a shrinkage in the output; and that seems to
show that the co. has made a very good profit by
resuming the mining of its own ore.
The co. really carries on three businesses. (1)
It mines its own ore. (2) It is a purchaser of silver-
lead concentrates for smelting. (3) It produces
large quantities of zinc concentrates and has latterly
gone into the business of zinc-smelting, which (ex-
cept for a little done by the Sulphide Corporation)
is anew and most important industry for Australia.
From the co.’s own ore a first crop of silver-lead
concentrates is extracted. The tailings are then
either put through the silver-lead mill again (most
of the old tailings are treated that way) or sent to
the zinc concentrating mill. At this concentrating
mill in the past half-year 178,125 tons of the resi-
dues produced 44,110 tons of zinc concentrates,
assaying 44.88 p.c. zinc, 6.95 p.c. lead, and 12.860 z.
silver. This product is still mostly exported to
foreign_ zinc-smelters; but the co.’s own plant at
Port Pirie (S.A.) is being gradually extended. Last
half-year, 665 tons of spelter and 100 tons of blue
powder (zinc dust) were produced. General-manager
Delprat says that, though the initial difficulties have
been many, “they have now been overcome, and the
results obtained in costs and recovery compare with
the best results obtained in Europe”—which, by
the way, isn’t very definite.
The spelter plant has been very expensive ; though
something has been written off it, it still stands in
the books at £86,000, and that is apart from the
roasting and acid-making plant. On the zinc-
smelter plant alone just about £IOO,OOO has been
spent. This amount was provided out of the Reserve
Fund ( £225,000), and it is now proposed to gradu-
ally repay the fund out of cash profits. This may
mean that the dividends won’t be quite as large as
the growth of the profits might lead shareholders to
expect. The financial position is strong, notwith-
standing the big demands made for new plant. A
comparison with the position five years ago follows:

Besides the reserves shown in the table, there is
£15,000 to the credit of a contingency fund. This
amount was put by in 1910, and is shown as written-
on , and that partly accounts for the larger sums
thus disposed of in those two half-years. The
amount might, however, be perhaps quite as fairly
reckoned in with the reserves; for it is understood
to be a fund to protect the co. in case it should
overdraw against consignments. These cos. mostly
get advances against their output, subject to a final
adjustment (say) three months after delivery; and
if there should be a bad slump in metal prices with-
in the three months, they may have a good deal of
refunding to do. As to the financial position, these
changes have occurred
June,’o6. Dec.,’lo. June. ’ll.

In the past six months the co. has paid its £52,500
in divs., spent £8439 in plant, and added £1496
to its liquid surplus. As the plant stands in the books
at £113,100, and as wear and tear is rapid, it is
certain that the surplus in excess of the dividends
was not large enough to provide for fair deprecia-
tion. In other words, the very big dividend paid
was not fully earned.
The gross profit for the past half-year was derived
mainly from the current output of silver-lead con-
centrates and zinc tailings, but a certain amount
also apparently came from a deposit paid on the
sale of the old slime dump. Probably the latter
amount was about eoual to the value of the slimes
produced in the half-year. If so, the £62,201 of
gross profit may be taken as the gross profit from
the half-year’s output. The output was 128,272
tons ; so the gross profit was equal to 9s. Bd. per ton,
out of which, of course, depreciation—renewal of
plant, etc. —has to be provided. The ore reserves
above the 1100 ft. level are estimated at 1,500,000
tons; and the gross profit on that lot at 9s. Bd. per
ton would be £725,000. But the plant will swallow
a good deal of that in repairs and renewals, and
there are a hundred other holes in a mine for gross
profit to fall into. As against that, however, has to
be put the fact that the present surplus of liquid
assets over liabilities is £115,782, and there is also
a big dump of old slimes—-90,000 tons—lately sold
at 7s. 6d. per ton, on which only a deposit has so
far been paid. And there is also the ore below
1100 ft. The lode has been pierced in what is called
the 1250 ft. crosscut; but it certainly is not safe to
rely upon any great quantity of ore below that point.
It is more likely not to be there than to be there.
There are 175,000 shares in the co. ; and the market
values them at about £5 10s. =: £962,500—a good
deal more than the £725,000, which is the amount
of the gross profit on the ore above 1100 ft., assum-
ing that last half-year’s profit is maintained through-
out. But it is all a matter of speculation. A pound
more or less per ton for lead on the whole lead con-
tents would mean about £200,000 difference in the
profits ; and in the past 10 years lead has averaged
as much as £2O Bs. loa. in one half-year, and as
little as £ll 4s. 6d. in another.
The Broken Hill North mill is doing extraordinarily
fine work; it is very doubtful whether any other
lead mill in the world beats it, though the B. H.
South isn’t far behind. Its concentrates contain
76.8 p.c. of the lead and 54.8 p.c. of the silver;
and there is only 8 p.c. of the zinc left in the con-
centrate. The product is divided into four sections
—concentrates, coarse tailings, zincy tailings, and
slimes. The coarse tailings—practically the tailings
—represent SO p.c. of the total tonnage, and assay
only 2.6 p.c. lead, 2oz. silver and 7.8 p.c. zinc. In
the zinc product no less than 68 p.c. of the zinc is
collected. Certainly, the Sulphide Corporation re-
covers as much as 93 p.c. of its lead; but that is
only done by de-leading the zinc concentrates. Still.
93 p.c. is a great performance, and shows the long
strides that mill work has made in Broken Hill during
the past few years. The strides are really much longer
than a comparison of the figures would indicate,
because the officials’ calculations are made on a much
fairer basis than was mostly adopted until recent
days.

The capital of Hydro-Electric and Metals,
Ltd. —the Anglo-Tasmanian co. which is to utilise
the waters of the Great Lake—being assured, work
is to go ahead promptly. J. 11. Butters, for a
couple of years past engineer to Siemens Bros.’ Aus-
tralasian branch, has been appointed chief engineer
and manager of the electric works, and he is going to
Tasmania at once to lay out the work, prepare speci-
fications, etc. Butters has had a fine training, first
at London University College in Engineering, and
then at the famous Thornycroft works.
American zinc smelters are having a good
time, and new works are going up at a merry pace,
particularly in Oklahoma. That district is still
blessed with a supply of natural gas, whereas the
supply has run short in Kansas, resulting in a shut-
ting down of many furnaces there. Zinc ore (69
p.c.) fetches up to £8 per ton, and even the lower-
grade sulphide sells for a much better price than it
does in Australia. In the latter case, freight to the
other side of the globe is a handicap. The tem-
porary shortage of American furnaces and spelter is
proving a big help to those European metal firms
which are “supporting” the spelter market.

5000 acres, and about one-fourth of it is planten
with cocoanuts, cotton and rubber. There is no
question at all about the fertility of the Solomons.

MAMARA PLANTATIONS, LIMITED.
See PROSPECTUS on Page 40 of this issue.
ihree or four years ago they took in hand a sub-
sidiary planting co., it was to the Solomons they
turned for their country. The co. now on the stocks
—Mamara Plantations, Ltd.- —will have the advantage
of starting with a large area already under culti-
vation, from which in seven years’ time a net profit
of £15,000 a year is expected; and by putting
£SOOO a year into development, the promoters reckon
in 10 years to be earning at least 25 p.c. net.
These figures cannot, of course, be checked; but,
given a supply of labor at the price figured on, there
seems to be no fly in the ointment. The labor supply
is the unknown quantity. There is certainly no
question of a White Solomons, and there isn’t likely
to be one for many years to come. For the property
the vendors get 40,000 paid £1 shares and £60,000
in cash; but £45,000 of the purchase money is not
payable until 1921, though meantime it carries 5 p.c.
The present issue consists of 40,000 shares to the
vendors and 40,000 to the public ; another 40,000 are
held in reserve. The shares are 10s. on application
and allotment; the other 10s. will be got in by calls
as the money is wanted for development. The first
£20,000, available on allotment, will pay the first
instalment of purchase money, and still leave a trifle
in hand. Half the shares are underwritten.
■» * * *
The success of the Victorian Araluen Cos.
(N.S.W.) has caused P. W. Tewksbury to open negoti-
ations with owners of other dredging areas at Ara-
luen. It is intended to form a co. with 32,000
shares, and place two large dredges on the ground.
Dredging promoters favor N.S.W., because the Vic-
torian regulations with regard to resoiling are irk-
some.
Great Cobar (N.S.W.) is aiming at a re-
cord output, and the men have been asked, as a
favor, to work four consecutive Sundays in order to
achieve it. It is said that the management wants
£50,000 to erect a concentrating and flotation
plant, and if Bellinger can get out a record tonnage
it will be some encouragement for the shareholders
to dip for the money. Funds haven’t been too plen-
tiful lately, wherefore no new works of any im-
portance are at present in hand; and, in general,
expenses are being cut down to the minimum. In
fact, rather below the safety level. Probably in a
couple of months time the excuse of a shortage of
labor will be heard again to account for a decreased
output. Murphv, the new Yankee smelter boss, has
shaken up the furnaces a bit, and a larger quantity
of Cobar Gold Mines and Chesney ore is going to
the smelters. This has made it still more evident
that underground work on the co.’s three main pro-
perties (Great Cobar, Chesney, and Cobar Gold
Mines) is very much behind. Fairly good ore has
been struck in the bottom level of the Chesney, but
not much can be pulled from there at present. The
sinking of the shaft is to be continued as soon as
possible, to see what’s below, and prospects are
good. But “as soon as possible” may not be very
soon. it has taken the co. the best part of a year
to sink the Cobar Gold Mines’ main shaft 100 ft.
(from 400 ft. to 500 ft.), and cut a plat, and there
it remains. Apparently, they don’t care about
driving the long distance into the lode. The sink-
ing was a blunder; anew shaft should have been
put down. On the Chesney a few men are em-
ployed cleaning-up and putting together the remains
of three Hancock jigs, and it looks as though a
little concentration is intended, probably to take
some of the slate out of the ore before it goes to the
smelters. But everything at present is being done
so slowly that a shortage of cash sticks out a yard.
■* * * *
Occidental gold mine, Gobar (N.S.W.),
is still battling along slowly with its bottom-levels
dirt. The co. has six concentrating tables at work.
The intention appears to be to put in tube-mills and
slime all the ore. The gold extraction is about 15
p.c. better from the slimes than from the sand, and
if that extra 15 p.c. can be saved by sliming the
ore, the tube-mills should soon pay for themselves.
But the drawback is the hard and tough nature of
the ore, which keeps the tonnage down. The co. is
at present sending an old heap of mixed slime and
sand to the treatment works, but that won’t last
many more weeks, and the output will then shrink.
Expenses, have been fairly heavy this last half-year.
A good deal of opening-out work has been done, and
anew change house and fitting shop have been
erected; but the number of hands has lately been
reduced, and a dividend may possibly come before
long. Yet it looks as though there won’t be many
of them, and, indeed, the policy most likely to
benefit the mine is to increase the reserve fund.
Crowl Greek (N.S.W.) is said to have
been purchased by plucky W. Longworth (former
part-owner of Great Cobar), and the old battlers
who have hung around that property since it closed
down some years ago feel better already. Anew
shaft will be required for a start; but a small
matter like that is not going to cause the Long-
worths many restless nights. Everyone wishes them
luck.
* * * *
Queen Bee (N.S.W.) is to have a half-
yearly meeting soon, and no doubt something will
be mentioned about smelting operations. The de-
velopments have been fairly good, though small,
and, as the available cash must be getting down a
little, it’s about time smelting was thought of. The
reports appearing in the press about rich strikes
and virgin ground, supplemented by glowing reports
from the mine manager, are beginning to look as
though a bait is being prepared to dangle in front
of shareholders and the public.
Cobar Gladstone (N.S.W.) report and
balance-sheet are out, and disclose rather better
prospects. It is the intention to sink the shaft
again, but that is a matter that will probably cause
someone a little worry, with the present appliances
on the mine.
Westralia’s gold dividends for July-
amounted to £77,019, making a total for the ex-
pired seven months of this year of £521,212. The
August distribution so far amounts to £78,297.
* * * *
Great Boulder (W.A.) “finds it impossi-
ble to allow the Golden Horseshoe to use Edwards
shaft for the purpose of opening up the boundary
lode at 2800 ft.,” and, as a result, the market has
tumbled. If the Horseshoe can’t use that shaft it
will be two or three years before the co. can get at
the rich ore, which is supposed to exist at the depth
mentioned. In that case the chance of the Horse-
shoe officials redeeming their promises with regard
to profits in the near future is hopeless.
* ♦ * *
Some explanation of the recent mysteri-
ous rise in Hainaults (W.A.) is found in the follow-
ing excerpt from a letter which the manager wrote
to the London directors on June 5: “Generally the
mine is showing an improved appearance all round,
and already our exploration work is beginning to
show results which point to good bodies of ore
being developed on the eastern side of the lease.”
On this guarded opinion of the mine’s prospects,
shares rose a couple of shillings or more before buyers
began to consider the speculative aspect of the
question. # *
Somewhere about 1000 tons of Greenfinch
ore is being bagged for treatment at lvalgoorlie,
which means that the market is to be considered
first, and the mine either second or not at all. To
think about spending money on a battery when there
is so much development to be done is bad enough,
but to waste cash in cartage and railage charges is
a great deal worse. There is only one straightfor-
ward thing to do in the case of a show like this,
and that is to go on proving it until a sufficient ton-
nage is blocked out to justify a mill. This could
easily be done with the cash the co. has in hand, and
what it has within calling power; but as insiders
are credited with having most of the contributing:
scrip, and would consequently have to find a lot of
the extra money if calls were made, it may be that
they are responsible for egging the Board on to
adopt what is a most unwarrantable policy, and one
calculated to make the concern a mere gambling
counter.
* * * *
From Meekatharra (W.A.) : The Mar-
mont, now owned exclusively by Tom Ryan, pro-
duced over £12,000 worth of gold last quarter. A
12 months’ option has been taken over the Ivyarra
on behalf of the Bullrush Cos. Hitherto the right to
work below water-level in this property has been re-
fused by the Government, because it is within the
boundaries of the local water reserve. An assurance
has, however, been given that if a mine is proved to
exist in depth the embargo will be removed. Mean-
time, a search is being made for water elsewhere.
Lupton’s lease at Barambie, which, up to date, has
starred a bit on surface crushings, has been floated
locally, with assistance obtained from the eastern
States. So far, about 700 tons have been treated for
a return of something over llOOoz. bullion. The
last parcel of 51 tons is said to have averaged 21oz. ;
but, maybe, this was what is generally known as a
flotation crushing. Barambie hasn’t been regarded as
the best place in the West to find permanent mines,
though everybody wishes Lupton’s show better luck.
Ingliston Consols pays a smaller dividend than usual
this month, while its near relative, the Ingliston
Extended, shows a larger profit. Latter has recently
substituted producer-gas for steam, and will be the
next Meekatharra mine to pay dividends. The Com-
modore ran across 9ft. of ore in the No. 3 level the
other day, 3ft. of which assayed 85s. per ton,
though these values have not been maintained in
driving. The development is interesting, as the
stone is in the present bottom workings, and ap-
parently east of what has hitherto been regarded as
the main body. Up to date the Fenian has paid its
four shareholders an aggregate of £102,000 in divi-
dends, and is now reputed to have 40,000 tons of
15dwt. ore in sight. The No. 6 level, is, however,
giving the management some concern.
* * * *
From the West: Emerald Option Cos. has
been filling up its Melbourne shareholders with glit-
tering accounts of its Westralian holding. Ore,, giv-
ing pan prospects up to 20z., is declared to be visible
for a good length at a few feet below water-level,
while (as usual) both faces are in rich stuff, and
underfoot the shoot looks better still. Something is
also said about it being difficult to understand how
a mine like the Emerald, which turned out such a
lot of gold in the ’9o’s, was never tested below
water-level. It was tested, and pretty extensively
too. Then it was abandoned. Maybe the present
crowd has got on to something new, but whether it
has struck the continuation of the old rich pipe, or
an isolated patch, has yet to
Golden Pole (W.A.) temporarily emerged
from obscurity the other day on reports of a good
development in the tributors’ workings. It has, how-
ever, since returned to the dusty corner whence it
came, which is a pretty good indication that the find
was nothing to blow about.
* * * *
One by one the Yilgarn (W.A.) “bon-
anzas” are being bowled out. The latest to be ex-
posed is the 4oz. ore which was alleged some months
ago to be visible on the Sunset lease, and on which
the show was floated. Even the State inspector of
mines indulged in the following whoop: “Just in-
spected Cortis’s new find (Sunset lease), Golden
Valley. Assays said to range from 3oz. to 84oz. ;
reckoned to average 4oz. per ton 10ft. across. No
walls yet. Consider the most promising find since
Bullfinch.” Alas! A trial parcel of 20 tons put
through a battery the other day gave lldwt., and as
the crushing had to be carted 30 miles, the co. was
probably out of pocket over the transaction. . The
co. reckons that the parcel treated was not a picked
one. If so, all that can be said is that the samples
from which the 4oz. assays were obtained must have
been very carefully picked. Unfortunately, that has
been one of the greatest curses of the Bullfinch
boom. Unwarranted estimates of the value of ore
were made all over the place, and later on the
public was hugely deceived. The “sampling has,
in fact, been ridiculous. #
For 12 months to March 31, Chillagoe
(Q.) smelted 61,094 tons of copper, silver, lead and
gold ore, which gave 3272 tons copper, 2376 tons
lead, 512,6250 z. silver and 11,6460 z. gold, repre-
senting a money value of between £5 and £6 per
ton. The co.’s gross receipts for the year amounted
to £623,606, or more than double the value of the
metals extracted at the smelting works. The dif-
ference represents the earnings of the long line of
railway from Mareeba to the Etheridge goldfield.
The negotiations with the Queensland Government
to take over the line are well advanced, and if the
deal comes off Chillagoe finances will be on a much
sounder footing.
* * *' *
Mount Bischoff’s profit of £43,376 for the
six months is the best for years past, though the
output (550 tons of concentrates) was exactly tfie
same as for the previous term. A profit at the rate
of £7B per ton of concentrates is something to
boast of, though, of course, most of the honor and
glory belong really to the recent boom in the tin
market. The ore milled (101,310 tons) was slightly
less than for the previous half-year, but the extrac-
tion improved from 10.631 b. to 10.861 b. of oxide.
The profit of over Bs. per ton of crude stuff was very
high in proportion to value; but no aoubt this big
average from low-grade dirt was _ considerably helped
bv the profit made in the smelting department, it
is satisfactory to hear from the chairman that
Mount Bischoff can smelt as cheaply as the work can
be done in England. A recent slight slump m the
metal market will reduce prospective profits; still
things are running smoothly, and mechanical im-
provements will help the dividends.
* * * *
The Oaks Rush (N.Q.) mill has obtained
a better water supply, and has started crushing.
The Acorn mine, next door, has cleaned Up at the
Welcome mill 299 tons for 107 oz. 13dwt. With
concentrates and sands it is estimated that the aver-
age will pan out lOdwt. The clean-up doesn t leave
much profit, so the market is slack. Oaks Rush is
also out of favor because the starting of the mill
was delayed so long, and because the Acorn return
isn’t up to the mark.
* * * *
During last quarter the Silverspur, Stan-
thorpe (Q.), had an output of 1749 tons, giving,
silver, 33,5440 z. ; gold, 5040 z. ; and copper, 19 tons.
The total value was about £4585. The owners have
been carrying on development work side by side with
the ore-gouging; and, as their main stopes have
reached the ore-body immediately under the 300 ft.
level west, better things are expected. The Warroo,
the only other decent return-giver in the district,
handled 464 tons for 5040 z. silver, 1660 z. gold and
12-i tons copper; total value, £I4OO. The bright
price of tin has set more scratchers on the trail,
which all helps the old tin town to get over its hor-
rible disappointment when it discovered that its
hopes of drawing millions from radium-manufacture
were built on a bubble.
Mungana (Q.) is preparing for a stoping
campaign now that the powerful new pump has
mastered the water. It is intended to attack the
lead sulphides in the Girofla mine, which is now pro-
vided with a mullock pass to facilitate the refilling
of the depleted ground at the 610 ft. level. Work is
also going on at the 300 ft. level in the Lady Jane.
Last year the Westralian Government
loaned £B3IO to lease and battery owners under the
Mining Development Act, but deductions, represent-
ing amounts charged to 1909, reduced the borrowed
total to £7900. Refunds aggregated £3201, so
that the actual out-of-pocket expenditure for the year
was £4699. Nearly £13,500 has been written off
as bad debts during the six years the Act has been
in force, but the bulk of this sum should have really
been shoved under the heading of “Waste.”
Some Southern (N.S.W.) colliery officials,
who have evidently already forgotten the Mount Ivem-
bla horror, got a very necessary though over-gentle
rap across the knuckles the other day. A deputy
or pannikin boss —had gone through the mine looking
for gas with a so-called safety lamp; but when the
lamp was looked at later on it was found to be
without the safety gauze. A rule requires officials
who learn of defaults of any kind to instantly report
them to the management; but three or four other
officials who knew of this shocking business turned
“oyster,” and the management only learnt of it
after some days had elapsed. It cost the principal
offender his job, and the others about £3 apiece.
* * * *
Callide coal country, at the back of Glad-
stone (Q.), is to be given another chance. One J.
H. Kessell has secured 2560 acres as a lease, and a
syndicate intends to “test the field, with a view to
future coal mining operations.” Mine Minister
Appel, in reply to Gladstone’s member the other
day, stated that £7950 had been spent by the Gov-
ernment in testing the central district, chiefly on the
Dawson River; but, as only £IOO had gone into the
Callide, that country would be given a chance “dur-
ing the current year.”
* * * *
Altogether 616 mining accidents were re-
corded in Westralia during 1910, of which 29 proved
fatal. Explosions, falls of earth, and shaft and mis-
cellaneous underground casualties were responsible
for 404 serious and 23 fatal accidents, while surface
and machinery accounted for six fatalities and 183
cases of serious injury. Considerably more than one-
half of the accidents occurred at Kalgoorlie, where
11 mine workers met their death and 363 sustained
serious injury. Several hundred minor mishaps to
body and limb, including the usual percentage of
“narrow squeaks,” were also recorded; yet the idea
still prevails in some quarters that mining is not
the hazardous occupation it is cracked up to be.
* * * *
“Quidam”: Re remarks of “Windlass”
(B. 3/8/’ll) anent the charges of Etheridge Rail-
way and Mines Cos. for crushing and cyanidmg. 15s.
a top for the former is certainly a good price, but
nothing out of the way for North Queensland. In
Central Queensland I never got a small lot crushed
for less than £1 per ton, and things are generally
cheaper there than in the North. As to the cyanid-
ing charges, if “Windlass” means that first one-
third of the tonnage is deducted from the sands,
and then one-third of the assay value of the sand
(together equalling two-thirds), and after that the
cost of treatment, it certainly is hot. If, however,
he means that one-third of the fire assay value of
the tailings is deducted, and payment made for
the two-thirds on the whole tonnage, less treatment
costs, it seems reasonable enough, as m North
Queensland ores there is generally a big difference be-
tween fire-assay values and cyanide returns. Ail or
more than one-third might easily be left m the tail-
ings. A lot of old miners think that cyanide ex-
tracts aU the gold in an ore, and all that most
cyaniders wish is that it would do so. Very many
heaps of residues, even of fairly clean stuff, have
more left in than the cyanide gets out. ■ When the
process that can extract practically all the gold in
tailings arrives, a lot of cyaniders are going to
quit cyaniding very suddenly.
* * * *
From Maoriland: Talisman, Karanga-
hake, got £18,740 from 4500 tons for its last four
weeks. The co. pays its 27th div., a useful eighteen-
pence. Prospects are quite as good as hitherto. Ihe
div. returns a shade under an annual 12 p.c. on
market value ; and there is also an occasional bonus. A
new reef carrying rich stone has been opened up near
Reefton (South Island), and the ground all round
has been pegged out. Consolidated Goldfields (Reef
ton) continue steady returns— Progress, 4028 tons
for £4324 (cyaniding and sulphurets £1986) ,
Wealth of Nations, 2169 tons for £3883 (cyanidmg
and sulphurets £1370) ; Blackwater 4210 tons for
£7672 (cyaniding and sulphurets £1455). lairua
Mines, over the hills from Thames, makes a nice
start with £433 from 350 tons from plates only,
cyaniding of residuals to come. Thames Deep
Levels’ crosscut has got through its hard bar and is
again in good sandstone country. An inflow ot
water has set the pumps a quicker pace, and may mean
either another reef handy or the draining of old work-
ings 400 ft. overhead. May Queen is getting some
picked stone from the reef first cut by this crosscut,
and Saxon is getting nice dabs of gold m the reef
in its ground. The Waihi-Paeroa Gold Extraction
Cos., which is getting on well with its £ 70,000-plant
for dredging and treating the tailings that lie m the
Ohinemuri River, expects to be banking returns early
next vear. Near Waihi the co. treated some 38,000
tons for an encouraging £14,000, but was washed
out by the big flood. It was then decided to launch
out in the present big plant at Paeroa, and so catch
not only the Waihi but the Itarangahake tailings.
The co. expects to start on 500 tons a day, increasing
to 750 tons. #

The undisputed benefit of asbestos covering for
steam-pipes and boilers has been exemplified by a re-
cent scientific treatise of the principles of steam
conveyance. Tests made have established the fact
that 100 square feet of pipe covered with this
material, at a pressure of 2001b. per square inch, and
coal at 16s. per ton, will save £7 per year, and
prevent loss of pressure equal to 13.84 B. Th. U. per
square foot per minute. Bell’s Asbestos Non-con-
ducting Composition, supplied in cwt. bags, or

Bell’s Asbestos, Ltd., 315 Kent Street, Sydney.*
The Bigelow Patent Rock Breaker commends
itself to mine managers who welcome an effective
machine which is cheaper to maintain and repair
than Gyratory and other crushers. This machine is
of the well-known Blake type, but has some ad-
ditional improvements which have justified in prac-
tice all the claims advanced by the patentee. The
work of crushing is done on the down-stroke of the
pitman instead of the up-stroke; there is no strain
on the pitman cap; two large side rods resist the
thrust of the toggles, and enable the jaws to be
regulated within a large range; the back toggle is
so made as to resist all legitimate strains of crush-
ing, but will shear under exceptional conditions and
save the machine. The shaft and eccentric bearings
are self-lubricating; the jaws are convex and con-
cave, and with the cheek plates are made of steel
and are reversible. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have installed a great
number of these machines, and hold the sole manu-
facturing rights for the Commonwealth.* [3

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, August 21, 1011.
-My Dear Arini, —
The passing of Cardinal Moran has
thrown a blaze of light on the departed
f hurchman and scholar, and made most
brands of religion shake hands beside the
Mighty Dead. With tolling bell and raven
drapery, chanted prayers and lighted tapers,
St. Mary’s did homage to its great chief,
lor days the tram-service nosed its way with
ant-like patience through the crowds that
hung round the Cathedral which enshrined
the emblamed remains of the late Cardinal.
There was a light-hearted bubble in his
bookish temperament which made this
learned prelate’s rare appearances at Society
Parades a joyful item to those who circled
round his stately robes. At one of the last
ot these gatherings—the Government House
private view” of Longstaff’s portrait of
J.ady Dudley —the Cardinal, in sitting down,
somehow missed the chair and went to the
floor. With an almost boyish gaiety that
astounded elderly male persons in the early
twenties, the Cardinal was on his feet in a
moment, before the adjacent group had time
to stretch out a helping hand. Death seemed
tar away then from this indomitable spirit.
Archbishop Kelly, who succeeds the dead
Cardinal, is himself, hurrying towards the
JMiadow; he knows it, and is unafraid. A
man to whom a mistake in this matter
should be almost impossible, assures me
that some months ago one of Sydney’s great
surgeons broke the news to him. “My
dear Doctor,” said the Archbishop cheer-
tu‘ly> please do not worry, for I
assure you I don’t. I am like a traveller
with his bags packed waiting for the jour-
ney to begin.” It is a big asset, the faith
which can make a man regard the horrors
ot inoperable cancer in that spirit.
Melba stepped out of the Victorian ex-
press, looking 30 or thereabouts, in a plum-
colored tailor-made, dark furs, and a high
plumed hat. A night of train jolting and
the cold light of a drab morning couldn’t
bowl out our skylark on the dull wicket of
mere years. Her physical health must be
superb; she walks with the spring of twenty,
and the poise of an Indian water-carrier.
John Lemmone’s smile was first out of the
car Melba came next, and was rushed at
with flowers. Henry Bracy (also growing
younger) gave a morsel of blue frock and
naxen hair the right impetus, and she
dropped a huge basket of blooms at Nellie's
toes. When little Valerie Mason—the
youngest atom of the new Opera Co.—backed
away from the Presence, H. N. Southwell
very spruce in new millinery, did light por-
terage for floral tributes, and went off with
the air of a prima donna in luck. Con-
ductor Joseuh Bradley hurried into the
melee and Claude Mackay, of the J.C.W
staff, also spent the prayerful end of the
Sabbath at the Central Station.
The notion that Coombe Cottage, in Vic-
toria, is intended as an Australian
home for the masterful songstress—a
rural Australian resting-place for her
tired ambition seems to be wide of
the truth. It will be the store-house
ot ner art-treasures—the interesting col-
lection of things she intends for family
heirlooms and sundry bequests. These were
all in her London house at Great Cumberland
1 lace, which she sold a while ago to Lady
Randolph Churchill for many thousands of
pounds ihe treasures had to be stored
somewhere; and, as they were destined for
Australia, the charming cottage at Lilydale
will serye as their museum.
Tenor John McCormack’s wife, her sister
(Miss holey), and two chubby children
with engaging dimples, have arrived. John
follows, and will step ashore next Monday.
Mrs. John is a bright, black-haired Irish
woman, with sparkling eyes and domestic
habits. The McCormack babies vigorously
attacked Coogee’s sandheaps with spades
and other implements the day after their
arrival.
the tube skirt. There’s comfort in the
Melba tailor-made, which showed a wider
area of stuff about the knees, when the wearer
nim bly off the Melbourne express.
As Melba makes two pilgrimages a year to
1 ans for her plumage, with incidental treks
to other shrines, her garments must be the
last word m Fashion. The female stick is
apparently getting out of date, and the pre-
sent Chinese trot of the Tube Lady will
soon become a memory.
A returned Coronatrix pours in the item
that George and Mary’s only girl is an
elder sister in the hardworked intervals of
being a Princess on show. “When the Royal
carriage-load of children passed our stand on
" un e s h e says, “little George, in a glee-
ful frenzy, was stampeding about it as if
the horses and crowds had gone to his head
bister Mary gave him a gentle smack, shook
him in the manner that prevails during
kmckerbocker riots in humble homes, and
sat him firmly in one corner of the carriage.
]n tl L e nursery argument, Mary’s coronet
tell off and was picked up by the Prince of
\V ales, who hurriedly popped it on again.
In an instant tne girl became the Person-
age, and bowed affably to the crowd, the
eclipse of George, junior, being so rapid
that very few saw it.”
The Westralian Jioys, now climbing green
seas in search of Vancouver, passed through
Sydney last week. J. ,T. Simons isn’t quite
tall enough to stand still as a lighthouse, so
he moves across the ocean to pilot this pil-
grimage, which will sample American foot-
ball and British beef before it is finished.
The boys are Australian from cover to cover,
and their national sentiment boiled over in
The Bulletin office the other day, when
light refreshments and brief speeches were
poured out. Mr. Simons answered back,
from his altitude amongst the office beams,
that the Union Jack was all right—they
wore it with a home-grown trimming of
Southern Cross. The biggest youth amongst
YV estralia’s 47 boys is large enough to take
on any stranger who throws dead-cats at
this continent, and the smallest dresses in a
ferocious trumpet, a green tassel, a gilt map
of Australia, and other drapery of local
manufacture.
The Patriotic Club, Ltd., opened its resi-
dential apartments for women with an offi-
cial broadside of cream puffs and other
weapons last week. Miss Margaret Harris
has hurled waggon-loads of good-looking fur-
niture into the abode. Mrs. Cecil Youl, who
concealed a residential club for women in
her secret thoughts for quite two years,
showed a meek front to a roomful of polite
remarks. Blessings, unappropriated or other-
wise, with nice luggage and personal refer-
ences, can now put up at this Patriotic dove-
cot for any period from one night to a
month without unpacking their politics.
Once a year the N.S.W. Kindergarten Union
rubs noses in _a fraternal council meeting,
and gathers its accounts together over
a teapot, at its headquarters in Roslyn
Cardens. Acting-Gov. Cullen’s gentle dame
presided at last week’s meditations. Dr
Philip Sydney Jones inserted a slab of criti-
cism into the shortage of trained Kinder-
garteners. A higher standard of theoreti-
cal training tears the student from her prac-
tical work, and the wave of prosperity which
tosses a golden spray over the State has
caused a shrinkage of applicants for the
Kindergarten course. Mrs. Francis Anderson
and principal Elizabeth Jenkins weighed out
the good results of Free Kindergarten
schools; general sec. Ellen Desailly—-as re-
cording angel, in a pepper and salt tailor-
made and alluring millinery—stabbed a
tountain-pen into reports and other heavy-
weight matter. Mr. Boorman, who never
lias time, to get into mischief, because he
spends his otherwise-spare moments toiling
for the Golden Fleece Kindergarten, hurled
t i n,r rief remarks at the gathering. Mr.
John Mackay, another honorary worker and
Mrs. Goldschmidt were on the official trail.
The meeting- was packed to the doorkey
which resisted the attempts of late-comers to
squeeze in.
The Professional Women Workers’ Asso-
ciation has caught the enlargement microbe
and reconstructed itself on a bigger scale,
it slams the door, as of old, in the face of
dabblers, and only admits the paid worker
t 9 its camp. Dr. Grace Boelke, Miss Har-
liott, 8.A., and Mrs. C. Day are in charge
or its morals, which are militant in the cause
of women and children.
A B.A. of Sydney Uni. recently believed
herself predestined to inject verbs, Latin
roots, and other necessary evils into upcoun-
try youngsters. She got away to the Never-
Never as a governess, somewhere between
the saltbush and the sunset, and now wields
a broom and duster with much vigor
cleans a large, growing family of boys and
girls in homoeopathic doses of water and
wonders where her ’Varsity training comes
m Ihe domestic problem here is first and
last, she writes. “Any girl who comes
governessing Out West, and won’t use her
muscle, must take the next coach for home
I loneermg is a useful habit, and a little
Latin goes a long way when there’s a basket-
ful ot mending.

down to the social sloping of their acting-
Governorship with a plain resolve to clear
every obstacle The imported Gov. has
lately developed a taste for travel, and the
Cullens success incites the old question—
V\ hy use the imported article when the
home-made brand is so satisfactory?”

Nutting, of the 17th Lancers, is the best-
advertmed ornament of the new Gov.-Gen.’s
start. Cold Land papers pursued him with
polite references to his parent’s wealth till
he got well away from the Thames village
His father is the first Baronet. John Gard-
ner Nutting, who was once High Sheriff for
Co. Dublin. A.D.C. Harold Nutting is
heir to the family money-bags, which are
said to weigh many tons.

day was mentioned the other day, and a
large area of bunting hung out of various
Consulate windows.
liish envoy Hazleton, of the beautiful
fl OC u’rn. WaS i, ch , osen by tlle Irish party to
fight Tim IJealy at the last British general
elections for a curious reason—he had no
past There is nothing of the Bayard
about Healy when he is on the political war-

Papua loses its head-nurse next month—
not Lieut.-Gov. Murray, who has chief
charge of the infant but Nurse Gertrude
Tait, of larkes (N.S.W.), who was chosen
last year to go up and pat the pillows in
the big Possession’s bush hospitals. There
isn t another Australian woman who
knows so much about the Tropical
as this Nightingale does. For
five years she was nurse and matron
at the Thursday Island Hospital, and
not many people have the chance that
a hospital nurse at Thursday Island has to
get a real knowledge of the Jap and the
fuzzy-headed tribes of Ham and Japlieth. She
had scarcely shaken down into her new
Papuan charge when the Lakekamu gold-
rush happened, followed by the worst out-
break of disease that Papuan gold-rushes
have known. So Papua’s chief nurse was
packed off up among the swamps, on the
edge of German New Guinea, and there she
has been battling with fevers and other
troubles for five months, the one white
woman on the field. She rarely goes visit-
ing ; her nearest white-woman neighbor she
could reach by going down the river, through
interminable sago-palm swamps, a three-
days’ journey; and that would be merely
the start of the expedition along the coast.
She handles a good pen, and if she doesn’t
use it when she gets back to sidewalks and
afternoon-teas, it will be a shocking waste
of wonderfully-rare material.
Attorney-General Findlay (M.L.), who
returns with baronet Joe Ward, brings with
him, as excess luggage, one of the gauds
that somehow' missed. Adelaide’s Mayor
Cohen. Findlay bears a general facial like-
ness to Gov. Islington, whose arduous efforts
to label his missus “Her Excellency” may
yet cause a riot in M.L. I was sniffing sul-
phur in Rotorua when Gov. Plunket got
ashore, and began to struggle for the same
tag for his wife —a piece of court-plaster to
which only the Australian G.-G.’s dame, the
Indian Viceroy’s missus, and the Canadian
G.-G.’s best girl have any shred of right.

black in the face over the fight of the
smaller Gov. House fry to crown its lady
“Her Ex.”
_ Genial David Mills steps into the shoes
kicked off by F. W. Jackson, as Sydney
manager of the Union Steamship Co.’s busi-
ness. David, who has an old reputation for
affable habits, is the brother of knight
James. Mr. Jackson’s footwear will hence-
forth be the comfortable slippers of the re-
tired veteran.
High Church parson Albery, who for-
merly rounded up souls with much zeal in
Christ Church paddocks, and whose marriage
was a nine-days’ wonder to his flock, mi-
grated with his Australian wife to the Chan-
nel Isles. Jersey is now their home, and a
new daughter their most exciting possession.
Colonel R. Wallace, R.A.G.A., and Chief
of the Scrap-Iron locker, recently put into
this port from Melbourne, to sit on our Har-
bor searchlights. The mercantile marine is
much worried in its eyesight when a long
oin of white light reaches out from South
Head, and hits its steering gear on a black
night. General Gordon, with Sapper Wil-
kinson (who trains mines at Chowder), com-
pletes the khaki commission of Wise Men.
.Mrs. Wallace passed back to Melbourne the
other day, after a hurried look-in at Syd-
ney’s blue-water front.
Laurence Godfrey Smith—commercially
and otherwise one of this town’s most suc-
cessful pianists—is packing up his kit-bag
tor Europe. Vienna will be his chief resting
place.
Fiddler Lorna Ussher, just back from
Leipsic, where she used her bow under the
eye of Prof. Hans Becker, chats cheerily
°f, hfe en pension. Lorna avoided flats
where the bachelor girl generally neglects
her food, and buys chocolates instead of
beef. “A small room in a good—that is to
say, well-kept—pension is much better than
a more ' pretentious room in a cheaper
house.” Lorna is sure that girl-students in
foreign flats develop ribs and sharp corners
instead of flesh and grey matter, the cup of
tea being a handy method of genteel starva-
tion. “Mere food is a necessity,” muses the
girl with the fiddle.
Eileen O’More (Bessie Doyle) is said to
be now officially connected with the Leland-
Stanford Uni. (U.S.). where she teaches
the Young Idea to fiddle.
Representatives of two very old Australian
families had a wedding recently at St.
Augustine’s, Neutral Bay. The bride was
the fourth daughter of the late Marcus Dul-
hunty, of Mosman, and the groom William
Marsden, eldest son of the late H. S. M.
Betts, of Yale Head, Molong. The groom
is descended from Parson Marsden, of the
very early days; the Vale Head farm was
one of the vigorous old parson’s grants. The
bride is a grand-daughter of Colonel Gibbes,
a former Collector of Customs, the grand-
father on her father’s side being a Dr. Dul-
hunty, who gave up medicine for sheep. The
founder of the Betts family in Australia was
John Betts, of Parramatta, who married a
daughter of the Rev. Samuel Marsden.
The Stewdrt-Newmarch wedding in St
James’s Church attracted battalions of doc-
tors to the orange-blossoming of Dr. New-
march’s only girl. Dr. Colin Stewart Was
the man, and Miss Remington, Miss Bray
and a little Miss M. Gilfillan were assistant
high-priestesses. Later on, when the guests
fluttered their wedding finery across King-
street and scurried up the steps of St. James’s
Hall, the sprint of this city’s best-known
doctors for the wedding feast suggested the
outbreak of a sudden epidemic— probably
champagne and confetti.
Mr. Geddis, who owns a newspaper
or two in Maoriland, with artist W.
Blomfield—whose signature “Bio’ ” is often
in Maoriland’s eye—has just passed through
Sydney after a scamper across Europe. Rlr.
Geddis hasn’t seen any place he likes better
than Geyserland, or any girl he admires as
much as his own Best Girl. Mrs. Geddis
met him on the Australian shore, and they
got away for the North Island this week.
Asked if he saw the Coronation, Mr.
Geddis admitted that he glued both eyes on
to the procession, and also noted that the
masses were in no humor to explode with
joy at the feet of the peerage. “George is
popular, but this labor trouble was brewing
in June, and the note of sullen apathy in
the crowds was so ominous that we hurried
away from England—there was no telling
how the strike would extend, and we didn’t
want to get stuck on the other side ”
A note frsm a confirmed traveller:—
Struck a troupe of entertainers on the Sydney ex-
press the other night, but hadn’t the heart to strike
em hard. Should like to introduce the “turn” to
the Railway Commissioners. Though the “troupe”
consisted of two only, the “duo” managed to get in
some very effective work. As a matter of fact, thev
woke me up from a sound sleep at about 2 a.m. E
lay and listened awhile to the tuneful strains of a
mouth-organ accompaniment to a male soprano’s
singing of “Break the News to Mother” ; then, curi-
osity prompting me, I jumped out of my bunk and
proceeded to investigate the weird phenomenon.
Making my way to the rear of the car, I came upon
the minstrels. They proved to be two conductors of
the train, and seemed quite surprised when I in-
quired what the row was about! They explained
that, by a recent rule of the Department, they wen-
forbidden now-a-nights to so much as nod on the
trip, so they. had to keep awake somehow! 1
quite saw their point, but by this time the entire
sleeping-car was awake and hurling curses at my
head for “talking at that time o’ night,” so, feeling
a bit foolish, I crawled back to bed.
A stray dogs’ carnival is going to happen
at the North Sydney Coliseum skating rink
on August 31. The Admiralty lady will
hand over the prizes to those who struggle
best in a tug-oGwar, elopement race, and
other classic devices for snaring funds for
the Neutral Bay Dogs’ Home. Dames
errant, on skates, are going to make a blind-
fold dash for the blackboard picture of a
tailless dog. They will (d.v.) append a
chalk fixture to the spot nearest to the
beast’s appendix. She who tilts at the tail
with happiest results will receive her reward
on earth five minutes later. Miss Gullett,
who presides over the Dogs’ Home cogita-
tions; hon. sec., Miss Compton Legge ; and
Mrs. Fritz Hart, an incurable optimist, are
scraping in stony ground for subscriptions
of any - size.
Maorilanders in this city are going to
give a Dominion Day hop on September 2U
at Sargent’s.
The Blascheck-Wrighton combination still
incites even the timid to explore the city
for the marble steps that lead up to the
Y.M.C.A. Hall and Mildred’s explanatory
music. Mr. Joe Blascheck cheerfully emerges
from a “Spring Cleaning” and other domes-
tic arguments, while Miss Wrighton eggs
him on with harmonic chords at the piano,
It’s the best one-man-one-girl show we’ve
had for years, and the girl’s good looks
supply a long-felt want in Y.M.C.A. circles.
Swords have recently clanked through this
town in a great military exodus due north.
The returning Defence-Minister Pearce has
been the objective; the tourists will pick
him up at Brisbane or further north. Mean-
time Paymaster Laing and the office cat are
alleged to be the sole defenders of the
Ancient Armory department’s headquarters.
As this closes, the Health Society’s
ladies are assimilating pearls of wis-
dom in the Town Hall, on the subject
of slums and similar crimes against
a clean city. Dr. W. G. Armstrong is the
lecturer, and he tramples on “Insanitary
Dwellings” with both feet.
At the same moment., in another selection
of the Town Hall, various citizens are con-
spiring together to give Carrie Lanceley’s
coming concert in aid of five local hospitals
a push forward. Carrie can’t keep away
from harmonious noises for more than five
minutes, and she collected all available musi-
cal talent in her home-nest on two evenings
last week. Surrounded by palms, wild
flowers, and other stimulants to sing, th<'
company did its best. Then the Gore Hill
girl, heavily laden with her presentation
tourmalines —large chunks of green sparkle,
edged with milk-white Australian pearls—
joyfully sang the “Jewel Song.”
The suicide of American novelist Myrtle
Reed recalls the cynical remark of a well-
known pen-woman—l fancy it was John
Oliver Hobbes—that hairpins, husbands and
babies were generally mixed up with the
literary woman’s writing materials. Myrtle
Reed dragged her husband’s name into the
tragedy of her death, perhaps unjustly.
Who knows? “If he had been kind and
considerate,” sounds like an ordinary fit of
married blues when the male creature wipes
his muddy boots on the new stair-carpet.
Yours, with a broad nib.
