Chunder Loo,
Of Akim Foo,
Had his Coron-
Ation too.
No one could
His claim demolish
To the title,
“ King of Polish.”
Chunder, when the
Primate crowned him,
Whispered to the
People round him:
‘All the best
Archbishops use
“COBRA” polish
On their shoes.”

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, August 21, 1911.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Denmans are keeping up a running
fire of small dinners to which the Very Best
People are bidden in turn. The social sheep
that baa away out on the edge of the flock
will doubtless get their share of drink and
entertainment in the race-time garden
parties.
Lord Dick Nevill, who returns to Aus-
tralia to act as G.-G. Denman’s chamberlain
in the absence of sick-man Victor Hood, will
be here in good time to devotedly shadow
Melba.
The Fuller pair continue their scheme for
the division of their toil. Accordingly Lady
Fuller went to Buffalo with Miss Phipps
and a party, while his Ex. attended a few
country shows. The country show’, which
is painfully plentiful at this time of year,
is a great test of vice-regal endurance; yet
Carmichael got so much practice that before
he left Australia he could “do” them as easily
as an art show. Lady Fuller enjoyed the
rugged rocks and the snows, and was able
to enjoy some ski-ing. Bright, the stopping
place on the descent, organised a carnival
for her, and then returned her safely to
town.
The Barttelot pair have taken Dr. Norris’s
South Yarra house for a term. Married
gentlemen-helps have been to Australia be-
fore this, and have been provided with quar-
ters under the official roof. They were all,
however, “without encumbrance.” Sir
Walter and his lady, on the other hand, own
two fine boys, and feel that they need a
place of their own. The youngsters trav-
elled out with the two Denman babies on the
Themistocles, which arrived last week.
Ethel Irving, in “Lady Frederick,” is fin-
ishing the present Melbourne season with
crammed houses. Before Wednesday’s mat-
inde there was quite a stampede for seats;
afterwards there was a suppressed riot of
disappointment because “Dolly’s Little
Bills” had to be cut out of the programme.
Dolly, who “never had the money sense,”
and rouses the wrath of her bridegroom with
her chiffon accounts, fascinates the feminine
mind, and provides thrills of sympathetic
horror. Most women have passed through
the financial ordeal at one time or another
with papa or hubby. Leading-man Ewart,
with his locks becomingly silvered, bears a
strong resemblance to Sam McCullagh, the
handsome widower of Woodlands.
Ethel Irving recently turned her trans-
lucent orbs to the gallery girl, and gave her
a general invitation to come along and meet
her at Menzies’ Hotel. It was a senti-
mental, heart-to-heart roast, with straw-
berry and coffee for stuffing. A corner was
occupied by a piano, and one of the girls
produced a sister who could sing. The host-
ess volunteered to play the accompaniment,
and the vocalist did so nicely that she is to
have a chorus place in the next show.
The gallery lady is making a pose of her
adoration of Ethel. Nowadays she takes a
folding sketching-stool, some fancy work and
her lunch, and squanders the day in the
queue, buoyed up with the hope that a
deeply-touched leading lady will discover her
and _ send her out tea and buns. This
species of mild lunatic became common dur-
ing the stay of tne Asches. Scientists and
other dustmen have found them abroad as
early as eght in the morning.
Ambrose Patterson has painted a portrait
of “dad” for Melba, and it is just about
ready for her inspection. The artist is a
protege of the singer’s; she gave him
two years in Paris, and looked him up when-
ever she was passing that way. The
painter’s brother, Tom, is one of Melba’s
brothers-in-marriage-law, and Mrs. Tom
goes to Sydney for the opening of the opera
season as her sister’s special guest. “You
look peaked,” remarked Melba to the rather
pale-faced matron, and straightway arranged
to take her away north, where sunshine
is supposed to be more plentiful.
The star had a whirling time motoring
about Victoria the week after her arrival.
She was to have crossed the Murray within
three days ; but, instead, she sent an avail-
able brother-in-law to attend to pressing busi-
ness, and thereby gained several extra days
wherein to oversee the finishing of Coombe
Cottage. A family party motored there on
Friday last and had a scratch meal. The
ukase is that no other guests are to see it
until everything is complete.
There is to be a marriage in the Melba-
Mitchell family very soon. The eldest of
David Mitchell’s sons and the diva’s brothers
has won a fluttering “Yes” from the bright
little widow who nursed Dad at Doonside.
Premier Murray’s daughter, who accom-
panied her father to the hungry city by the
Thames, was to have been presented at the
Coronation Drawing-room; but, when the
other women were doing their agonised best
not to fall over their trains, the Australian
girl was being waited on by nurses in a pri-
vate hospital. When the mail left the disap-
pointed one was making a slow recovery.
The Australian relatives of the Countess
of Darnley are glowing over her d6but as a
song-music writer. She has got the inmates
of most London drawing-rooms by the ear
with a ditty called “Content,” for the words
of which May Aldington is responsible. The
brunette countess, as some people have for-
gotten, was Miss Morphy, a Victorian girl.
Mrs. Daniels is a sister, and the Titian-
haired lady who married P. and O. Roberts
is a niece. Artist Fox painted this pretty
girl when she was Beatrice Daniels.
The Glassy Harem’s slight social frost is
being rapidly thawed by the sunny influ-
ence of vice-regal interest. Under the Den-
man party’s skates the ice almost melts
with gratitude, for the returned Coronators,
seeing the G.-G. group on the floor, have
made a rush for their sliding implements.
The management rubs its hands and
chuckles. A grand fancy-dress carnival is
to happen this (Thursday) evening.
The golf tournament now smiting its way
round the links is minus one devoted player.
Whilst motoring last week, Dr. Jack Gordon
bumped into a train, at a dangerous Bruns-
wick crossing, and he, the shover, and the
car, got the worst of the argument. The
shover was the sorest sufferer, losing a hand
and acquiring skull fracture. The doctor
escaped injury, but received a severe shock.
A curious effect was that he lost the sight
of one eye for several hours after the smash.
Mrs. Gordon, who is also a golf enthusiast,
deserted the tournament in order to watch
over her invalid medicine-man.
The other day Acting-Premier Watt mo-
tored to Kyneton, the district in which he
was born, and the countryside tendered him
a picnic welcome. Willie talked in a remin-
iscent strain to the children in the school
where he acquired knowledge, and then de-
clared 'a holiday. A flag was stuck in the
ground where the house that once sheltered
the man of the hour used to stand, and alto-
gether Kyneton had a Great Day—compara-
tively speaking.
Madge Fink, daughter of Theodore, is to
be married very soon to V/'alter Timmins.
The two go to America for their honeymoon,
and, later on, are to settL in Melbourne in
a new house they are bui.ding on a part of
the cut-up Chatswood es'.ate. A wilderness
garden amongst the ti-t/ee is to be one of
the features of the hr me. Miss Fink, by
way of following in father’s educational
footsteps, _ has been .studying housekeeping
and cooking on the most modern plans.
For a girl who is well off, she has remark-
ably few delusions about servants. The kit-
chen is to be electrified, and made as far as
possible independent of the coy and uncertain
Marianne, so that the bride may cook at her
ease. Bridegroom-elect Wally Timmins is
one of several well-known brothers.
Assistant-Paymaster Ramsay, of the Com-
monwealth Naval Forces, and Miss Gertrude
Leslie, daughter of the T. G. Leslies, have
intimated to their friends that they intend
to incur the usual waste of rice and confetti
at some future date.
In satin, lace, orchids and pearls, Hilda
Grantham ceded the centre bit of her pater-
nal name, and became Mrs. Captain Ber-
tram R. Graham, at St. John’s, Toorak, on
August 10. Hubby comes from some ’pore
or other in India, where he captains the
Queen’s Own Corps of Guides. He will take
his bride to India, home and duty after a
honeymooning in the Blue Mountains. The
best man was a brother officer, Captain I. E.
Blois Johnson, and the groomsman was Tom
Grantham. DorothvM’Caughey, of the Wood-
lands clan, and Wanda Pinnock were the
two pale-pink bridesmaids. Mrs. Grantham
got away from the speeches and flurry at
her mother’s place in a navy-blue travelling
serge amid a sprinkling of rose leaves.
Art students hereabout darkly suspect
a serious and silent canvas-critic of matri-
mony. The suspect has hitherto ranked as
a woman hater, and has diplomatically kept
female worshippers at a respectful distance.

The ill-fated expedition of French avia-
tors to Melbourne last year is leading to
some result, anyhow. One of the French
gentleman, with a strikingly romantic name,
is now under matrimonial contract to a local
girl.
Leonard Borwick and the Marshall-Hall
Orchestra gave joint and several aid to Mel-
bourne’s musical culture, at the Town Hall,
last Saturday afternoon. The hit of the
concert was Beethoven’s Emperor Concerto,
with which Carreno was introduced to
Melbourne some years ago. With gentle
Leonard at the piano, the effect of the Em-
peror was less imperial, but decidedly more
harmonious, than when Carreno bossed the
show. Madame made the forces at the back
of her sound comparatively ragged and un-
certain; but the “poet of the piano,” as the
Taits call him, shed a softening, sympathetic
influence, and the slow “madonna” move-
ment of the second part was charmingly
done. The usual Marshall-Hall incident
gave a touch of humor to the concerto. A
lady in the left-hand balcony had left her
seat to close a draughty door, and, with
glaring eye and upraised baton, the great
mokanna of music waited for her to creep
back into her place ere his soul would allow
him to start. The offender crept back; but
in her agitation at being singled out by the
terrible Eye, she dropped her ’brella, and the
waiting audience whispered “Serve him
right!” Had the poor woman dropped a
brick she might have earned loud applause.
A recital of songs written by May Brahe
is to happen at Allan’s this (Thursday)
afternoon. The piano starts at 8.30. and
half a dozen artists—Maggie Stirling, Lilian
Reid, Rebottaro, etc. —have undertaken to
give the Brahe songs a good sounding.
The allegedly-old Xaverians hung scarlet
geraniums on the chandelier of the St. Kilda
Town Hall last Tuesday, and danced joy-
ously in the lurid light of scarlet-shaded
electrics. The dancing Old Boys were mostly
very young indeed; but a few mature old-
sters benevolently watched the whirl in com-
pany with some visiting old collegians. Dr.
Edward Ryan, president of the association,
extended a welcome to representatives from
“Geelong” and “Scotch,” and told a St.
Peter’s (Adelaide) man that they were all
glad to see him. After the first lancers,
these grave and reverend ones left the danc-
ing to the frivolous, and retired to talk poli-
tics—or something. The musicians tried the
new “slow” waltz on the nimble-footed sec-
tion, but it didn’t take. It is a dreamy, lan-
guorous glide, which merely irritates ener-
getic dancers who are out for a healthy
frolic.
The elusive Angel of Success flapped her
gold-tipped wings over a juvenile fancy ball
held in the Prahran Town Hall on a recent
night, for a children’s ward at the Talbot
epileptic colony. Over 1000 kiddies of all
ages and sizes took the floor, and their dress-
ing was astonishingly good. Dames a’Beckett
Best, Donald Mackinnon and Guy Sherwood
were the judges, and awarded the first prize
after deep consideration, to a set of miniature
black and yellow jesters. A march past, ar-
ranged by Miss Hyams, was a bewilderingly
pretty sight. When the joyous pageant of
youth had passed, the judges looked like
people who had been presented with a prob-
lem tougher than the most expensive motor-
tyre. However, they solved it satisfactorily.
At wrapping-up time, the word flew round
that all hands had assisted at a financial suc-
cess.
On Friday last the Writers’ Club, Spring-
blossomed for the occasion, supplied Ethel
Irving with one scone (earned by affabilitv)
and one cup of tea (bonus). The guest
was a gracious vision, in hat, coat and shoes
of thrush-egg blue —a summer-sky impres-
sion translated into exquisite clothes. Miss
Nelson, of the co., accompanied her in an
18-carat frock, and a hat decked with bird
of paradise tails. I think there must be a
surplusage of those birds on the other side of
the Styx; otherwise I can’t imagine how the
white-winged rangers could allow them to be
captured in such quantities by the milliners.

browse on Australian pastures, encouraged
thereto by the sale of several of the high-

portrait-painting commissions are flowing in ;
so Rupert and his charming French wife
have taken the widow Watson’s house in
South Yarra. Mrs. Sib Currie and her
small daughter are the artist’s present
“sitters.”

From “Rosna,” in London-
The motor smash, in which George Reid and his
daughter were the chief sufferers, caused much ex-
citement in Empiah circles, and telegrams of con-
cern rushed in from George Rex and a quantity of
the Beerage. Daily bulletins were posted outside
the Bank of Adelaide, in Leadenhall-street, telling
the world how much sleep and nourishment the in-
valids were taking. The Reid damsel, by the way,
has nerve—a fact that was illustrated at the Pageant
of Empire. She was impersonating the Queen of
Australia in the Masque Imperial, when the steeds
belonging to her chariot became facetious, and
began waving their feet about the atmosphere. The
gallant outriders —not Australians, I beg to state —-
jumped down and fled, leaving the damsel in sole
charge of the acrobatic beasts. She was equal to
the occasion, however, and extricated herself from
the chariot in a cool and blithesome manner.
Sydney and Melbourne each had a Wattle
Day last year; but rain was pouring on Mel-
bourne when the appointed time arrived, and
the celebration was swamped. The Gaud
Mayor, who was in charge, said “Better luck
next time,” or words to that effect, and in-
timated that another attempt would be made
in 1911. September 1 is the day chosen by
the Wattle Day League for the glorification
of the gold blossom. G.-G. Denman is patron
and Sub.-Ex. Fuller vice-patron of the floral
band, of which ordinary mortals may become
life members at a guinea a time. The unco’
canny, who are afraid their lives may not
stretch to 21 years, may be Leaguers for a
lone shilling every twelvemonth. The League
will do all in its power to protect the wattle
from the picnic vandal, and to encourage the
spread of the numerous beautiful species. A
wattle show will happen on September 5, at
Melbourne Town Hall, in conjunction with
the Royal Horticultural Society’s daffodil
spree. A public, celebration is to follow in
the evening, with nice flowery speeches.
Patriots are implored to wear a sprig of
blossom on the Golden First, and Senator
Findley has promised to try to get his col-
leagues to give the country a lead in that
respect.
The approach of “The Chocolate Soldier”
has caused town to be invaded by Sydney
chorus girls, who, so far as their dressing
goes, are early-edition heralds of Spring.
Ihe fur-clad Melbournite peeps sideways
from her winter armor at the visitors and
•admires their courage. At the same time,
knowing her climate, and not desiring to be
a late-edition pneumonia patient, she de-
cides that not yet will the moth get a chance
to dodge between the camphor lumps and
banquet on the muff and the stole.
The first flight of the oversea woolbuyers
arrived the other day by the tall ship Oster-
ley. These birds of passage usually enliven
the suburb they pitch their furnished villa
To ° war .y to wake the sleepy respecta-
bility of fashionable taverns, they form fours
or fives, rent a smart house, hire a motor,
and straightway turn their attention to busi-
ness—and pleasure. When the respectable
householders return to their Queen Anne, the
neighbors tell lurid tales of the revelry that
was. The Highly Respectables look properly
shocked; but they generally make a secret
note to let the shack again if possible, for
the wild and woollies pay handsomely.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—-
A travelling ’Murican has “discovered” a new
view for us. It is to be seen from the hills at the
back of Mitcham, where a sheer drop to the plains
reveals the whole of the Adelaide plain, from far
south beyond Brighton to the shadowy lines of the
northern hills. A good point about the find is that
it is the merest stroll from the Mitcham tram.
Mrs. E. T. Smith’s estate has been sworn for pro-
bate at £52,500. The bulk of it goes to relatives.
Another estate to enrich the Treasury of late is
that of Charles Geddes, of Port Pirie. By farming
and the timber trade he collected some £B2 000
and, incidentally, did a good deal for the advance-
ment of his town.
The storm occasioned by the appointment of 6X*
Bishop Wilson, of Melanesia, to .the rectorship of
Walkerville has died down. The suburb turned out
en masse the other night to toast its new cleric
with tea, and there wasn’t a hostile critic in the
bailiwick.
Talking of Bishops, the ecclesiastical residence
here has been the subject of some wrangling lately.
White-ant or dry rot or something got into it,
and someone began talking of re-building. There
was a composite howl from those who’d no wish to
see a modern “villa” take the place of the present
respectably solid pile. Compromise has decided on
under-pinning and a general tidying, so peace is
restored.
We got our Admiral back from Brisbane on Wed-
nesday, after sparing him to our neighbors for 10
days. Lady and Miss Bosanauet broke the journey
in Sydney; but Captain Fletcher journeyed from
here to the Victorian capital, and “personally con-
ducted” his Ex. home.
Editor Sowden, Who, as well as controlling the
destinies of the Register, has a large say in the
management of our Public Library and Art Gallery,
has just returned from a flying visit to the eastern
States. At Rupert Bunny’s exhibition he noted
several works, any one of which he considers might
be advantageously purchased for the local gallery.
We’ve said “good-bye” regretfully to Mrs.
Brough. The Lyric Club entertained her informally
during her stay.

bling at Richmond Park, where the popular Ald-

The dilapidated condition of the West Terrace
cemetery is the theme of constant newspaper letters
and the last correspondent had a further grievance’
in that children pilfered flowers and odd gardening
tools from the graves of the “dearly departed.” Is
this a protest against doctors and undertakers’
fees, or what? Talking of graves, that cheerful
column of the dailies, devoted to “in loving
memory” notices, contained an original idea t’other
day. when a picture of the tomb appeared along
with the tearful poem.
Among houses to change hands of late is that
which belonged to the late “Tony” Fotheringham
of Menindie. The purchaser is A. A. Scarfe. ’
Opponents of the Outer Harbor scheme were given
fresh data for their case last week, when the Oster-
ley was two and a-half hours’ late in arriving, and
12 in leaving. Owing to a strong north-west wind
the passengers spent a more or less happy night
bumping beside the wharf.
Mayor Cohen has explained his attitude re the
Sheffield Choir. Said he: “The line must be drawn
somewhere, and a civic welcome should not be given
when it is sought in the main as a cheap advertise-
ment.” He also hinted that the reception had not
been any too courteously or tactfully asked for.
,Passed out at Kapunda Hospital iast week, Henry
Holroyd, aged 82 years, and a colonist since ’49.
He was a fine and courteous old citizen, who varied
gold-digging, surveying and pastoral work with
literature and writing. Amongst other things, he
was credited with knowing all about horses and
dogs.

Misery after Meals
♦
This Woman's Life Made
Miserable by Indigestion —
How She was Cured*
“Some time back I became subject to pains
in my chest and in my right side,” said Mrs.
E. McGuinness, 63 Alfred Street, Prahran,
Vic. “I found that my food was beginning
to disagree with me. Almost as soon as I’d
taken a meal it would lie cold and heavy on
my chest, as if it wouldn’t go any further,
and I’d have a tender, sore feeling right from
the chest to the back, and particularly under
the left shoulder blade. I began to diet my-
self. and was careful not to eat rich, heavy
things, but still the pain and inconvenience
came on. I’d wake up each morning with
such a thickly-coated tongue and a most un-
pleasant, sour, bitter taste, and my eyes so
heavy. I had no energy at all. And such
sick headaches would come on, lasting per-
haps a couple of days at a time. My temples
would throb violently, so that I had to put
on bandages soaked in vinegar and water. 1
felt tired and run down all the time, and my
skin became very sallow. If I stooped, I
would feel quite dizzy. At last, through,
reading of a cure that seemed very like mine,
I tried a course of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills,
taking three a day and one at night time,
and they picked me up completely. I began
soon to shake off the weakness, and my food
began to agree with me again. Before Id
finished the third box I noticed I was much
brighter and brisker, and I’d feel the head-
aches not so severe. I kept on with the
medicine, and after six boxes was quite well.
My appetite and color came back, and I have
kept well ever since.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are 2s. 9d. a box,
six boxes 15s. 3d., of all dealers, or from the
Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co. of Australasia
Ltd., Sydney.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Some time ago the Maoriland Government
took in hand a block of waste and, according
to that croaksome bird, Private Enterprise,
worthless land, and proceeded to cultivate
it on scientific lines for fruit culture. The
experiment was a complete success, and the
Government then decided to let Private En-
terprise sit down, as usual, in the unearned
comfort. Recently 10 sections, planted and
ready for next season’s market, were opened
for selection, and the ballot was rushed by
about 250 scrambling anti-Soshers, practi-
cally all of them ready to take an affidavit
that Capital is being driven out of the coun-
try because of the meddling interference, etc.
John Bull’s “cheap loaf” is dough.
Poor David Storey, the hatter, desper-
ately eager for office, laid himself out to
help Wade defeat Labor in Mudgee and
Liverpool Plains, and threw all his alleged
Independence overboard. Henceforth he is
David Wade’s Tory.
The other day, at Birmingham, Lloyd
George, J. Bull’s Treasurer, was speaking
on the lot of the British worker, and, in
the course of his address, declared: “The
wolves of hunger prowl constantly around
millions of doors in this favored land.”
Amid the faint cheering that followed that
poetic assertion, there was heard a small
voice exclaiming, “ ’Ear! ’Ear! Blessin’s
of Freetrade!”
The Stanford-Merthyr coal miners
(N.S.W.) have protested against compul-
sory training for Defence “because they have
nothing to fight for.” Apart from the fact
that a good many of them have wives and
daughters, they might as well go back a bit
and find out -why they have nothing to fight
for. Put in very plain language, it is be-
cause somebody else tvas trained to do the
fighting.
. China has declared for the compulsory train-
ing of students of primary and secondary
schools in gymnastics, military drill and shoot-
mg. Sydney Telegraph, in its worship of
effete superstitions, merely because they are
worshipped at ’ome, clings to the theory dis-
carded even by Mongolia. And this, too,
when it has been stated by the chief officer
of Britain s recruiting staff, that more than
,)() per cent, of the recruits for the regular
army are unemployed. In other words, the
Telegraph’s recruiting agent is, not patriot-
ism, but starvation.
Henry Willis. having accepted the
Speakersnip of the N.S.W. Assembly, it is
rumored that four of the undependable “In-
dependents ’ have more or less guaranteed
the Labor Government a majority till the
end ot the present Parliament. Though no
one (in Parliament) wants a dissolution,
there will he much stage thunder over this
arrangement; hut it will be secretly ap-
plauded, all the same, because Wade’s fol-
lowers have been cursing in their beards
over the mess he has got them into. They
saw the gulf of dissolution yawning before
them, with probably no return ticket for
many of them; and anything is better than
that.
M riting of the dead Cardinal, the Sydney
Telegraph (17/S/T1) said:—
To most men there comes a period of their lives
when the march of progress goes past them. And in
such cases the stronger the man the more promin-
ently anachronistic is the figure that lie presents.
From the Telegraph, which opposed Feder-
ation, citizen service, an Australian navy,
Arbitration and Conciliation, “Socialistic
legislation” and a “Yes” vote on the Refer-
enda questions, that remark concerning the
essentially progressive-minded cleric who
advocated every one of them, is a superb
example of “hide.”
THE TREE AND THE FRUIT
THEREOF.
DAD BULL: “ Ok, Sonny, I've had an awful
time—they attacked me like dogs! ”
SONNY : “ Yes, dad—but haven't they been
housed and treated like dogs?”
Even on the Westralian goldfields there is
the blazing absurdity of two mayors and
two sets of aldermen and two cupboards in
the “mayor’s parlor,” where one of each
would do better. Kalgoorlie and Boulder
are the rivals—such deadly rivals that the
two mayors‘used to snort when they met in
the streets. A foreign-owned tram co. is
allowed to charge fid. for carrying pas-
sengers a few yards across the dividing line;
but each crowd puts up with the imposition
because it makes the people buy their beer
in their own township’s pubs. When Kal-
goorlie spends Is. in entertaining the visit-
ing pugilist, Boulder pulls out a florin to
look better. Naturally the two municipalities
have become almost stone-broke; councillors
can t sleep o’ nights ; town clerks and staffs
are asked to work for honor and glory ; and
bailiffs have become a nightmare.
The Weary Willie Government has
drafted a Midwives Bill, enacting that the
ladies shall hold certificates, “one of the ob-
jects aimed at being the suppression of mal-
practice.” It isn’t the trained and quali-
fied nurses, though, who indulge in this
brand of murder; it is frowsy and incompe-
tent Betsies who could not make a living
by honest nursing. They won’t seek certi-
ficates or publicity any more than they do
now; but, all the same, they won’t retire
from business. The only thing that will
render them unemployed is a place where a
girl “in trouble” can get out of it in the
way nature intended, and can afterwards
leave the consequences there and depart;
and that means a Maternity and Foundling
Hospital. Doubtless this would shock the
Wowsers; but the Wowsers love being
shocked, so that wouldn’t matter.
They wifi be gibing next at the Wes-
tralian State Parliamentary party that it
“doesn’t represent the real workers.” Among
the candiuates selected to give the Wilson-
Gregory Government a bad time at the next
elections are two farmers, one school-teacher,
one solicitor, a town clerk, a chemist and
an agent or two. It is becoming pretty hard
work for Fat to get home his favorite gag
that the Labor party represents a class and
stands for class legislation.
Leonard M. Isitt, the parson who has
been elected by Christchurch North to fill
the gap, left by “Tommy” Taylor in the
Maoriland Reps., will do little more than
draw attention to the size of the gap. For
years he has been one of the Dominion’s
chief Cold Tea apostles, and without intend-
ing to do anything of the sort has been driv-
ing the general public from the closed pubs
to the keg in their own homes. Like Taylor
he will generally be found behind the Bor-
rownet in divisions.
During the Mudgee (N.S.W.) by-election
Colonel Onslow was smitten heavily over the
leasehold business. Goslow had been shout-
ing for “freeholds for farmers” at the top of
his voice, and picturing the uncertainties
and other miseries of the leasehold system,
when Acting-Treasurer Carmike broke in
with the cold remark that the Colonel and
his family own the -whole country-side about
Camden, and lease the lot to the highest
bidders. Then the Waverley warrior had
to set to work to show that what was good
Onslow policy couldn’t possibly be good
State policy.
A while ago Attorney-General Denny (S.A.)
introduced the Veto Bill, which provides that
a measure twice passed by the Assembly,
with a general election intervening, shall be-
come law whether the House of Landlords
assents or not. This is intended as a
means to end the long struggle for the broad-
ening of the Council franchise —a reform
which has been persistently blocked by the
Council itself. The Labor Government,
being a practical crowd, says that it is tired
of banging its head against a stone wall,
wherefore it announces that, if the House
of Fat Landlords defeats this attempt, it
will be obliged to go straight to the British
Government, and ask the power which
granted the original Constitution to help in
bringing the machine up to date.
Henry Willis, M.L.A., who, by agreeing
to accept the Speakership in the N. S.
Wales Assembly, upset for the severalth
time the clumsy tactics of Calamity Wade,
V.C., and David Storey, hatter, was born
in S. Aus., but started to address the multi-
tude as a lay preacher under Anglican rules
at Mudgee (N.S.W.). He sat in two
Federal Parliaments in the House of Reps.,
but only with sufficient distinction to be
shot on his third appearance at the ballot
box. However, at the last State elections
he was able to capture a “Liberal” seat in
the “Smalls” House, and in Macquarie-
street he has usually adopted the role of
schoolmaster. “In the higher Legislature
to which 1 have been accustomed,” he scolds,
in effect, “an intelligent person knew
exactly where he was ; but your Parliament,
and your standing-orders, and your way of
conducting business would give an intelli-
gent person the pip.” So, now, if there are
any intelligent persons in the Assembly,
they ought to come into their own.
It didn’t take long for the ARGUS-sown
seed of a huge road-loan policy to germinate
in the spongy brains of the Gippsland farm-
ers. Last week the municipal councillors
of that province met and decided to ask
Weary, Willie and Co. to appoint a “de-
velopment Trust,” lend it a round million
and tell it to bog in. These mostly land-
owning councillors reckoned 3f per cent, in-
terest and 1| per cent, sinking fund' a fair
thing, and as that means £52,500 per an-
num, they were willing to forego their share
of the municipal subsidy—about £20,000 —
the Government to find the balance! It is
a great scheme, and when the rest of the
State has gone on the same loan racket, it
will dawn on Gippsland that it has to pay
interest and sinking-fund itself. At least it
will, as long as it pays anything; after that,
the kid will shoulder the burden, and trudge
along a long-since worn-out road with the
interest on the I O U.
Labor Minister Millar intends to ask the
Maoriland Parliament during the current
session to amend the Conciliation and Arbi-
tration Act to provide that an award on a
dispute in one district may become a com-
mon rule throughout the islands. Hitherto,
much waste of time and general irritation
have resulted from the fact that awards
could not be made to extend beyond the
bounds of their special districts. Millar is
also proposing some changes in the Workers'
Compensation Act. He wants to make em-
ployers pay something towards the doctor's
bill, the whole of which is at the present
time loaded on to the injured worker. An-
other important provision suggested is that
when an accident is fatal, or results in total
disablement, compensation must be pad
even if the accident is due to misconduct on
the part of the victim—just as an insur-
ance office pays up even though the policy -
holder has tried to drop off a tram in violent
motion.
THE DEFEAT OF THE ALLIES.
As another step towards absolutely free
education in S. Aus., the Government is
about to provide State school children with
all requisite books at a cost of £26,000 a
year. There is quite a strong party in the
Assembly anxious to make the University
free, and that will probably be the next
important step—if good seasons continue.
The New Protection and its dumb oppon-
ents came under discussion last week at the
opening of a Melbourne paint and varnish
factory, where Minister Tudor gave the new
machinery a start. Charles Atkins, the good
and pushful Australian who runs this show,
was only an importer of machine oil in the
first instance, but is now spreading himself
on the local production of dry colors and
varnish. There is any quantity of Aus-
tralian ochre to be had, but such things
when ground and treated are insufficiently
protected against the imported stuff. When
Atkins raised his tariff question, Tudor
spoke feelingly about those other manufac-
turers from whom he could not get any figures
concerning profits and wages. His inquiring-
circular addressed to “775 manufacturers in
one industry” had not fetched a single reply.
The silent 775 live in hope, seemingly, of
some further Protection that will benefit
only themselves, and they will do or saj
nothing meanwhile that might advance th<
interests of wage-earners on New Protectioi
lines.
SOFT-SOAP FOR LADIES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. A. Robinson, referring to the proposed scheme
of amalgamation of the various Liberal leagues in
Victoria, said it was a matter that needed careful
consideration. To bring women into an organisation
which should consist of men only, might have the
effect of seriously impairing it. Women did the
best work when they were left alone. With a joint
committee there was not the same efficiency as with
separate committees. For the past seven years the
Women’s National League had held the field.—
Daily paper.
Dear ladies, 1 implore you, for your own sake [and
for ours],
Do not seek amalgamation with us men ;
In the past you have been peerless,
And your methods, strong and fearless,
Still compel our admiration, now as then.
Our courage is as strong as in the past [or nearly
so] ;
And, as yet, we do not fear to stand alone;
But you still may aid us greatly
By-er-canvassing, as lately,
And, pray, let us do the rest upon our own.
[Good Lord! We can’t allow these cats to interfere
with us !
They’re hard enough to manage where they are, with
all their fuss.
At rounding up the snobbish vote they’re quite a
useful lot;
But, admit ’em to our inner councils? AA’hew ! I
reckon not!]
For many years, dear ladies, we’ve done nothing but
admire
Your drawing-room assemblies and your teas.
You’ve a gift for organising,
And your canvassing’s surprising—
Mere men possess no qualities like these.
Your knowledge of political economy is great;
Your arguments, advanced as they can be.
Do not think we would resist you,
Nay, we’re anxious to assist you
At your meetings by-er-passing cakes and tea.
[AVhew! I hope that we can choke them off their
latest little scheme,
If they knew our inmost secrets they’d get on the
chairs and scream.
They’d simply kill the Party—-such a wild, erratic
mob.
AVhat would they think if they knew, say, of Smith-
son’s little job!]
I earnestly assure you, you can aid us men-folk best
By remaining in a body as you are,
In the interests of the nation;
For, I think association
With the men, on finer minds, is apt to jar.
There are certain little matters—oh, not secrets t
Dear me, no.
You’re acquainted with our e’vry aim and act—
But-er-certain little matters
That would wear your nerves to tatters
If you had to grapple with-er-sordid fact.
[Jee-rusalem! I hope I can persuade ’em to keep
out!
AVhat sort of Party meetings would we have with
them about?
Best prime ’em up with Busted Homes and Shat-
tered Marriage Ties.
They’re keen on propagating such old, crusted Tory
lies.]
Dear ladies, you have always been the Labor party’s
dread ;
It fears you as it fears no other thing.
All our wrongs will soon he righted,
If you stand firm and united,
And to the Sacred Truth, as ever, cling.
Remember, Socialism is a monster in disguise,
AVhich seeks to leg'slate for class alone.
But your association
Stands for all the blessed nation
And the people’s good—which means —cr —means
our own.
[l’d like to know who smarted this amalgamation
craze ;
They certainly are useful in a hundred little ways;
But a pack of foolish cacklers at our councils!
Goodness me!
AYhy, there wouldn’t be a Party if they knew os
much as we.]
VICTORIA.
SEN.
THE LAST CAPITALIST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The world is grown extremely old,
And furrowed, dry and grey;
About a sun fast turning cold
It winds its weary way ;
And where the ice comes creeping through
Upon a lonesome land
Where once the tropic orchids grew,
Two shrivelled mortals stand.
The last are they upon the earth,
And each with age is bowed.
There’s little left of any worth;
The snow a final shroud
Is weaving for all worldly things;
A shadow, dun and drear,
As from the all-embracing wings
Of death, is drawing near.
One speaks, ill-covered and ill-fed:
“I will no longer moil
Without my own fair share of bread,
The product of our toil.”
The other frowns. “Talk not,” says he,
“This socialistic tripe.
We can’t have crude equality,
The time is not yet ripe.”
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
An unexpected winner at Flemington last
Saturday was the 3-year-old St. Vano, who captured a
7-furlong handicap in good style. The youngster re-
ceived plenty of weight—2olb.—from Twa Brigs
(who ran second) and others; but his breeding on the
dam’s side suggests that staying rather than sprinting
should be his game. The dam is Vanity Fair, a
mean-looking animal that was “placed” one year in
the Melbourne and Caulfield Cups.
* * * *
Said that the sharps who rushed to back
Eyeglass for the Metropolitan Handicap are likely to
be left lamenting.
* * * *
The Bananaland Government proposed to
bar the raucous, non-productive layer of odds from
racecourses; but the House of Fat struck the idea
out by 12 to two, only the Ministers voting for the
proposal. The House of Fat thus put itself in
its proper class as the champion of those who do
nothing useful.
* * * *
Adelaide Racing Club pushed off the first
day of the Grand National meeting on Saturday, with
a glorious advt. for the totalisator. The boodle
passed into the machine reached £20,529 —a jump
of £5OOO over last year’s effort. Throughout the
day good “first” divs. were paid out; but, when
Eager returned a “second” div. of £22 55., and Mor-
ganite paid £7 10s. for a similar position, the joy
of punters was extremely great. Such results may
yet knock the scales from the eyes of punters and
racecourse proprietors in N.S.W. and Victoria, and
may even convince the Bananaland House of Fatty
Degeneration that Ikey Noses is a superfluity.
* * * *
“0.K.”: At Caulfield, the other Saturday,
I looked for evidence in support of “Pro-Book’s”
contention that punters derive much joy from hear-
ing their fancy gee-gees shorten in market price
after they (the early punters) have “got on.” I
found a heavy weight of evidence on the side of
Sorrow in its contest with Joy. Where the
books opened proceedings with 4 to 1 on
the field, and two horses were backed at
that figure, the odds presently expanded to 9 to 2
on the field, and one of the original selections fell
back to the 6 to 1 mark. Similar retrograde move-
ments were the rule in other minor events. Also, I
took notes of the book quotations for the Australian
Steeplechase, in order to compare them with reports
of starting prices in Monday’s newspapers. The odds
mostly offered against Field Trial were 6 to 1. Here
and there a cry of 7 to 1 could be heard amidst the
babel. Leah Kleshna (who ran second) was a 10 to
1 chance with every odds merchant I visited, save
one who said “8 to 1 to you, sir!” in the tone of a
bargain salesman. And he was in a big way of
business, too. The newspapers reported Field Trial
at B’s and Leah Kleshna at 12’s. The winner, Con-
cave, got credit for a 12 to 1 starting price, and this
likewise was two points higher than the average
quotation roared in the ring. I wonder how many
backers of Concave on the course had the joy of
getting more than 12 to 1 ?
Old Melbourne print, J. J. Miller’s
Racing Chronicle, is dead, and J.J.M. makes his
collection appeal and funeral notice in one:—
“IN MEMORIAM.
DIED, at the Age of 16 years. From want of
sufficient sustenance,
J. J. MILLER’S RACING CHRONICLE.
We mourn our loss (financially).
There are liabilities —Arrears for the unfortunate
Editor’s Salary, Funeral Expenses, etc. An early
response for the above amount, now due, will very
much oblige.”
Maoriland’s Grand National Steeplechase,
at Christchurch, was notable for the win of the 12-
year-old Paritutu, who, after a strenuous career on
the flat, was put to the jumping biz. The favorite,
Corazon, was second; Antarctic, highly fancied, was
third. Paritutu has 19 firsts and 24 seconds out of
125 tries, or alleged tries, in 10 seasons. G. N.
Hurdles fell to a strong favorite in Continuance.
* * * *
The Stevenson-Lindrum billiard match in
Melbourne ended in an easy win for Lindrum
(receiving 4000 in 18,000). The Englishman
gained only 436 points on the young player,
and had thus scored 14,436 when the other ran out.
A match on the same terms is being played in Syd-
ney this week.
There is now no apparent reason why the
world’s billiard championship, whereof Stevenson is
the nominal holder, should not be contested in Aus-
tralia before Fred Lindrum leaves for England. To
those who watched them in the latter stages of their
game in Melbourne, it was hard to believe that the
veteran visitor had actually scored a lot more points
than Lindrum during the first week, and had at one
time been actually ahead of him on their handicap
terms. Towards the finish Stevenson’s pretensions to
give 4000 in 18,000 to the young ’un were reduced
1.0 ridicule. He sat disconsolate, for instance, whilst
Lindrum made breaks of 265 and 166 (unfinished) in
two successive cues; and this without resorting to
the “Gray stroke,” which, if he had been at liberty
to play it for its full scoring worth, would have put
the English nominal champion into the shade. He
played the “3 ball game,” as it is called, and
equalled or beat Stevenson in every department of it
save one. Thus far he doesn’t manage a nursery of
cannons with the delicate touch and old experience
of the specialist, but in all other respects he is as
clever as the other man, and less conservative. Ste-
venson seems to break down repeatedly through try-
ing to keep within a groove of play at the top of
(.he table. He botches the situation for himself by
his niggling tactics, whereas Lindrum is ready to
sacrifice a difficult chance to do something wonderful
in favor of a more simple game which may offer
further possibilities of a big break. In fact, the
Australian is a more resourceful all-round player than
the champion, and could be backed to play him on
level terms to-morrow if the restriction on the
“Gray stroke” were somewhat relaxed. Ordinary
championship rules put no restriction on the “Gray
stroke.”
The 87th football tussle between N.S.W.
and Queensland supplied the Rugby Union bill of
fare at University Oval (Sydney) on Saturday; and
8000 patronised the affray. The players’ luck was
in, as a shower of rain bad softened the bumps on
the ground, thereby enabling all hands to side-step
the scars that are generally the prizes of football.
It was an even game in the first half, which ended
8 all; but in the second section N.S.W. climbed
over the Bananalanders, and lifted the tally to 34
points to 14. In the second half, therefore, the
home team amassed 26 points against the Northerners’
6. The N.S.W. three-quarters out-manoeuvred their
opponents badly, and Mayo and Dean piled up three
tries each, the other tries being scored by Griffin
and Pugh (of the forwards) ; while Reynolds booted
three goals over the bar, and Ward Prentice kicked
two. On Queensland’s behalf, Tucker and Pill
(what’s in a name!) were the most useful; but the
team, as a whole, was below the Bananaland
standard of late years.
The other Union matches of the day were spark-
ling titbits. City and Suburban (Sydney) were a
trifle too good for Central Western, and demolished
them by 11 points to 6 ; while the argument between
Metropolis and New England ended with the scores
level, 6 points each.
League Rugby in Sydney on Saturday
didn’t shift the order of the top-dogs. The match
on which most depended was South Sydney v.
Western Suburbs; Souths trampled Wests out of
recognition by 20 points to 7. In the other strug-
gles, Glebe made a lunch of Balmain (41 to 2) ;
Eastern Suburbs squeezed out ahead of Annandale
by 8 points to 5 ; and North Sydney defeated New-
town by 17 to 12. The result of the play leaves
Glebe and South Sydney level with 20 points each,
Eastern Suburbs close on their heels with 18, New-
town 13, and the others in the soup. Next Satur-
day will conclude the second round; after which
the leaders will battle in the final on September 2.
Up to last week-end, “Dally” Messenger’s
kicking in this season’s Sydney Rugby League pre-
miership matches, on behalf of Eastern Suburbs, had
resulted in 44 goals out of 48 recorded by the club.
This booting energy yielded 88 points out of 205
scored —over 40 per cent, of the^total.
After being held up since July 15 by inter-
state matches and other excuses, the Australian Rulers
kicked vigorously in Sydney on Saturday. Sydney
downed East Sydney by 69 points to 29; Y.M.C.A.
raged like the heathen, and slew Newtown by 78
to 25; and South Sydney wiped out North Shore
by 73 to 32. As the outcome of the day’s turmoil,
Y.M.C.A. (last year’s premier club) creeps into the
final four —Sydney, East Sydney, Newtown and
Y.M.C.A.
This season’s Soccer in Sydney can’t pro-
vide much more excitement. Balmain has pushed its
claim to the Rawson Cup still further, by beating
Canterbury, 3-0. The competition table stands:
Balmain 22 points, Granville 18. So Balmain has
now only to tie the next match to win the Cup.
Granville, by downing Newtown (2-0), has qualified
for the third round in the Gardiner Cup, for which
the final will be played about September 9. The
Association pannikin already belongs to the coal-
mine suburb.
* * * *
After the carnival interlude, Vic. League
’bailers resumed their contests, the chief episode
being the collision of ’Roy and South. The latter
had to win to have a hope of being on top at the
end of the round, and ’Roy likewise needed victory
to reach the final four, wherefore a strenuous and
somewhat discourteous game ensued. It was even
pegging till the last quarter, when Souths set out
on the business of the day, promptly won two goals
and a few behinds, and then played for safety. The
Maroons stuck to it gamely, and snatched one goal;
but time and South’s defence blocked further efforts,
and the disturbance ended—South 36 points; ’Roy,
27. An unseemly item was the fact that ’Roy’s bar-
rackers persistently jeered the umpire—a " former
Collingwood player—on the presumption that he
was favoring South in the interests of ’Wood. The
point was that, if ’Wood and ’Roy could win the
three remaining matches, ’Roy would beat ’Wood for
the final on percentages of points scored ; but ’Wood
had already grasped this fact, and was piling up 98
points to 9 against ’Varsity. That game was of
no interest to critical spectators, but was a joy to
one-eyed barrackers. The other episodes were but
side-shows, Essendon beating St. Kilda by 158 to
38 ; Carlton outing Geelong by 71 to 51, and Rich-
mond (51) and Melbourne (60) indulging in a skir-
mish that mattered to nobody.
In the Ass. the long-drawn struggle be-
tween Brunswick and Port, for fourth place, still
continues, and will only end when the round ends
this week. Both won on Saturday, ’Wick beating
Brighton by 75 to 45, and Port downing ’Seray by
58 to 43. This leaves ’Wick some 20 points to the
good on their scores, and, as the Brickies meet Pres-
ton next Saturday, while Port tackles Brighton, the
odds are a shade in favor of ’Wick. Essendon got
its second beating this season, this time at the feet
of North —60 to 34. The winners—last season’s
premiers, by the way—are in such financial straits
that the club has been passing round the hat. The
players see, however, that the only thing that brings
in oof is success, and plenty of it, and they are
bogging in accordingly. P’ran and Preston and
’Town and ’Cote are understood to have collided
somewhere, but the results are of no account.
The 10-days’ football carnival in Adelaide
(Australian rules) drew a gate of £2038, which is
more than was taken in Melbourne on the last oc-
casion. The visiting teams (every State but Queens-
land) and council delegates (every State and Maori-
land) get their expenses paid. That meant £1025.
Another £ 500 was spent in entertaining them ; the
five States that competed get a dividend of £6O
each; and the small balance goes to the ‘ Oval, where
all the matches were played.
* * * *
Adelaide (0.A.) football, on Saturday,
had a record in close finishes. Port Adelaide
crawled out ahead of Sturt by 66 points to 65 ; South
Adelaide defeated West Torrens by 57 to 56; and
Norwood accounted for North Adelaide by 47 to 42.
It was an afternoon too strenuous for weak hearts
and sore heads.
Langford and Johnson, who are to fight
in Australia for the world’s championship, leave
America for Sydney on September 6. Make-the-
Splosh is promoter. The white race will not get
much satisfaction out of the match, as both com-
petitors are coons.
* * * *
Hughie Mehegan intends to make a dash
after the boodle during the next few months. Fol-
lowing the fight with Charlie Griffin, he is to argue
the point in the following 13 weeks with Keys, Read,
Picato, McCoy and Cullen. By the time the sturdy
Australian gets through this menu he will probably
be quite ready to ask some gentleman to say grace
after meat.
* * # *
Charters Towers (N.Q.) has a lightweight
boxer named Barney Saunders, who is a bit above
the ordinary. He met Rev. Hulton Sams in the
final of the amateur lightweight championship of
North Queensland at the Towers the other night,
and at the end of the prescribed four rounds the
judges couldn’t give a decision, so they ordered an-
other round. The parson got the better of this, but
the committee was so pleased with Saunders’s show-
ing that it awarded him a special medal. This is
the first time that the Rev. Sams has been shaken
up to any extent in this country by a man of his
own weight.
Word comes from Noo Yark that the
grievously miscalled “Cyclone” Johnny Thompson,
who whirled with moderate success in Sydney last
season, has been beaten on points by Frank Klaus,
of Pittsburg, in 10 rounds.
Io put an end to the wrangle which
started when Donaldson failed to toe tne mark in
the recent footrace, he and Yankee C. E. Holway
will meet for the world’s 130yds. sprint champion-
ship at Sydney Sports Ground on September 23.
The Rugby League will father the event, and fill in
the afternoon with side-shows; and, as a. substanti ..
deposit is put up by each athlete, a genuine race cu.i
probably be expected. Other professional matches
fixed up for the near future include one between Hol-
way and Postle, and another between Donaldson and
Postle; and the desire to catch the elusive “gate”
while the sport is lukewarm is therefore being well
attended to.
* * * *
As a lung-stretcher for the N.S.W. cross-
country championship race, to be held next Satur-
day, East Sydney A.A.C. controlled a five-mile cross-
country event last Saturday. A host of athletes
faced the starter, and, at the other end, T. J. Wood
maintained his consistent form by romping in 20yds.
ahead of C. Weyman and F. Terry. As 16 athletes
finished within two minutes of the winner, the cross-
country struggle promises to be more interesting than
usual.
Queensland Amateur Athletic Assoc, last
week upheld the protest of the Toowong Harriers
against the victory of Redfern (N.S.W.), in the
recent Bananaland cross-country championship, owing
to some of the team being missed in the count by
the judges; and the re-shuffle pushes Toowong into
first place, while Redfern and East Brisbane drop
into the second and third holes. As the Banana-
landers join in the N.S.W. cross-country race on
Saturday, the Redfern band will have its chance of
revenge.
The Queensland tennis tourney was con-
cluded on Saturday. The previous week N.S.W. gents
went down to Bananaland by 15 rubbers to 6, and
Queensland ladies defeated Ma’s girls by 5 rubbers
to 4. Yet, last week, when the individual champion-
ships were played, Mother won everything; Rice
(N.S.W.) and Parker (N.S.W-), the holder, were left
in the final; and Rice came out on top by 4-6, 9-7,
6-2, 6-1. The ladies’ championship went to Mrs.
Ford (N.S.W.), who beat the holder, Miss Macintosh
(Q.) —6-3, 6-1. And Rice and Parker (N.S.W.), by
beating St. John and Thorlow (Q ; .) —4-6, 8-6, 6-4,
6-3 —won the gents’ double championship also.
Australia can have the Davis Cup this
year so far as England is concerned. The Fog isn’t
sending a good team to battle for it; but if Amurka
gets the utensil, then J. Bull promises to put his
best tennis foot for’ard.
* * * *
N.S.W. Lawn Tennis Association altered
the waistcoat of its constitution last week, by in-
serting a gusset to allow a “majority” to control its
affairs; otherwise it will face the world again robed
in all its old apparel. The change will no doubt
result in the opening of the courts at Double Bay
(Sydney) for Sunday tennis. If this doesn’t save
the Association financially, the undertaker will be
called in.
* * * *
The Dunlop inter-club premiership on
the Homebush (Sydney) roadway, on Saturday,
pushed traffic on the footpath to make room for io
cyclists and a bundle of pacemakers. At the end
of a slashing 30-mile race, Redfern gathered in the
shield, with 16 points against Marrickville’s 20 ;
and the shield therefore finds a new resting-place.
The journey was done in lhr. 19min. 30sec. ; and,
as clubinates Cutcliffe and Kirk dead-heated for the
honor of first past the post, Redfern gathered all the
glory. The winning team has been the runner-up a
couple of times, so the win is a cheery reward for
pluck and consistency.
“Teuf-Teuf” : The A. T. Craig, widely ad-
vertised by the Benz car people as having made
some remarkable wins at the Brooklands track, is of
the Melbourne firm of Craig, Williamson. His car
will probably be longer remembered at Brooklands
for its performance of the aeroplane act. It was
being driven on that occasion by Wilkinson, and
after a ding-dong finish swooped up the bank, travel-
ling at something like 100 miles an hour, shot over
the edge, and soared away in a clean 150 ft. jump,
crashing through a tree in its flight. The tree
saved the driver by hooking him from the seat and
dropping him through the undergrowth, about 50ft.
from where the car “took off.” A Benz, by the
way, was amongst the first motor arrivals in Aus-
tralia, 15 years ago. It had solid tyres, tiller steer-
ing, and a speed of 10 miles per hour. The car is
still running, and was in the streets of Melbourne
recently.
* * * *
The really modern motor-cycle appears to
be just about as reliable as the one of three or four
seasons ago was erratic. In a recent 24 hours’
scamper from London to Edinburgh, of 96 starters
only eight failed to complete, and most of those
failures were through accidents. The distance is 400
miles or thereabouts.
An English-built car was recently sub-
jected to a 15,000-miles test on Brooklands track,
and some interesting figures were dug out of it. In-
cluding 83 miles from shed to track, the total dis-
tance covered was 15,008 miles. In the 14,925
miles of track-running an average speed of 34.7
rn.p.h. was maintained. The time spent in adjust-
ments—apart from cleaning sparking plugs—was
under 18min., and, at the end of the run, the worn-
out parts could have been replaced for under £2.
The engine was oiled once a day, the average daily
mileage being 300. The petrol consumption was
a gallon for every 23 miles; oil consumption one
gallon per 1184 miles. The motorist’s misfortune
is that he can’t take the Brooklands track with him
when he goes touring.
Lately Jake de Rosier, on the Brook-
lands track, put up two motor-cycle records, viz.,
flying kilometre (1093yd5.) in 26 l-ssec., equal to
about 85.3 miles per hour; and flying five miles in
3min. 43sec. (80.7 miles per hour). And at the
same time the flier equalled his own 41 l-ssec. for
the mile (=87.3 miles per hour).
Australian amateur champion TD. W.
Schneider is still riding into places in big English
cycle races, but seems to lack the final kick neces-
sary to get him over the line first. In the 10-mile
British Empire Championship on the Crystal Palace
track, he jumped away when there was a quarter of
a mile to go, but could not last out, and finished
only third, two lengths behind the winner, C. B.
Kingsbury.
* * * *
When S. Aus. and Queensland met at
lacrosse in Brisbane, on Saturday, the slaughter of
the Northerners was complete—l 4 goals to 5. The
Moffat brothers attacked vigorously for S. Aus., and
nearly all the goals were the result of their efforts;
while O. Thorpe gathered the glory on Queensland’s
behalf. On Monday, S. Aus. trampled on Bananaland
11. by 11 goals to 6, so the Crow-eaters’ long jour-
ney northwards had its reward.
* * * *
N.S.W. was crumpled up by Victoria in
the inter-State lacrosse match at Petersham (Sydney)
on Saturday—ls goals to 1. There have now been
10 tussles betwen the teams, and Victoria has
scooped the lot. N.S.W. played well throughout the
afternoon, but Gursonsky’s defence for Victoria regu-
larly obstructed the dashes when a score appeared a
certainty. The solitary goal to N.S.W. was scored
by Mallett, the reward of playing the finest game on
the side. As the Victorians defeated South Aus. be-
fore they crossed over, scoring 6 goals to 5, the Aus-
tralian championship isn’t likely to drop from their
grasp. * * *
Sydney baseball on Saturday had a big
bearing on the result of the premiership. Lane
Cove and Leichhardt (which occupied level seats at
the top of the table) clashed, and Leichhardt won
by 7 runs to 3. Leichhardt is therefore ahead now
with 18 points, while Petersham and Lane Cove are
on its heels with 16. Dethronement of Leichhardt
and a win to Petersham and Lane Cove next Satur-
day would even up three clubs; therefore the pre-
miership struggle is full of possibilities.
N.S.W. inter-State hockey team obliter-
ated the “Rest of N.S.W.” at Sydney, on Saturday, by
7 goals to 1, and departs this week for Melbourne,
where the Australian championship matches were
begun by Victoria narrowly defeating South Aus. bv
2 goals to 1. When Ma’s boys bog in, hair and skin
are expected to fly.
Major Philip Trevor discusses the hard
case of Tarrant at some length in London D.T. It
a first 11 of all the world had to be chosen, Tarrant,
he says, would certainly be in it without a dissent-
ing voice. England has the legal right to play him,
but should that right be exercised? The Major
answers the question in the negative. Suppose Eng-
land won the triangular tests with Tarrant’s assist-
ance, what would be the obvious and unanswerable
retort? “Yes, you’ve won, but you had to get an
Australian to beat the Australians.” As the Major
accurately puts it, the object of the triangular tests
is to ascertain which country produces—not procures
—the best cricketers. Consequently Tarrant, having
been born in Melbourne, and having learnt his
cricket there, should help Australia in the inter-
national competition, whereas by the laws which
control the game, he is only entitled to help Aus-
tralia’s principal opponent.
Secretary Wheeler, of the Adelaide
Y.M.C.A. being trained in American methods, in-
sisted on getting in a billiard table last year, to the
horror of some of his committee, who considered it
a kind of skating-rink to Hades. The table has
paid for itself already; the Good Young Men of
Adelaide have, in inter-State competitions, shown
themselves pretty warm with the cue; and —two
more tables have been ordered!
The Australian Jockey Club (Sydney)
has a few “old hands” on the committee. H. C.
Dangar and W. R. Hall have kept a seat thereon
since 1864, while George Lee and 11. Chisholm have
lent a hand for 18 and 16 years respectively. All
these four are “horsey” men and breeders.
* * * *
Next Saturday Miss Gertie Lewis (holder)
and Mrs. E. Woodbridge, of Port Stephens (chal-
lenger), will splash on the Parramatta River for
the ladies’ sculling championship and £25 aside.
The race is to be in light 18ft. skiffs, over a two-
mile course, ending at the monument.
*** . *
In London the towel is no longer waved
over the sitting boxer during the intervals. Instead,
his second points at him a brass cylinder, in which
reside a fan and a supply of ice.
They manage these things better in unso-
phisticated South Aus. A publican in a country
town lately organised ' a race meeting. He owned
a mare which had a big chance in one event; but,
as the course was too heavy for her to show her
top form, the fixture was postponed.
The Champs-Elysees, the great Parisian
thoroughfare, proves that motor-cars are the least
damaging species of traffic. The Champs is divided
into three tracks, the outer one for ordinary vehicles,
the middle one exclusively for motor-cars. It is
estimated that the daily traffic is 20,000 ordinary
vehicles and 30,000 motor-cars. Yet for every two
yards of repairs on the motor track there are eight on
the horse section; and the motor road needs practi-
cally no cleaning. The oil and grease drippings
from the cars protect the wooden blocks. On the
other hand, the ordinary horseshoe is a destruc-
tive weapon, and whether it lands on a man’s nose or
the surface of a wood-blocked street, it leaves its
mark all the time. #
Ever since motor-cars were invented Man
has been seeking the perfect light; and some of him
reckon that at last it has arrived. The new illum-
inant is a combination of incandescent disc, petrol
vapor and oxygen gas. The gas is mixed in a small
carburetter (the oxygen being supplied from a steel
cylinder) and projected through a special burner
on to the incandescent disc. It is known as oxy-
petrol, and is said to be a bobby-dazzler about 10
times cleaner and more reliable and brilliant than
acetylene.
“Con”: Toowoomba (Q.) footballers have
been left completely out of inter-State teams; vet,
upon meeting the N.S.W. reps, lately, the Dar-
ling Downers defeated them by 14 to 11. The
Walers are the lot that so ignominiously defeated
combined Queensland two days before, though they
had themselves been previously beaten by Central
Queensland (9 points to 6). Now, the point is
this: Hitherto the Queensland Rugby Union has for-
gotten the inland footballing centres of Queensland.
But how can it henceforward ignore the two teams
which licked Ma, when the Queensland team, com-
posed almost wholly of Brisbane players, and selected
without doubt from Queen-street, made such a dis-
graceful exhibition against the same crowd?

Another Australian Record
on the “Alcock” Table.
TO the remarkable series of record breaks by Gray, Roberts, Williams and
Lindrum, already performed on the “Alcock” Table, is added another record
by Lindrum. His latest achievement was in the Melbourne match with
Reece, when he secured the phenomenal break of 1239—1230 coming off the red
establishing two Australian records. All these champions have expressed their
high opinion of Alcock Tables, for huge breaks are only possible where the table
is “ true to angle,” with cushions, jaws and every other part absolutely perfect.
, , , Bot , h , Gray and Lindrum owe their present reputations largely to the
Alcock 1 able. It is on these tables they practised and made many of their
largest breaks. W e will gladly post you our illustrated Billiard Catalogue.

Can I Obtain a Patent ?
The quickest wav to ascertain this,
WITHOUT EXPENSE, is to write
to me describing your invention, enclosing a small sketch
where necessary. Nicety of drawing is not essential;
all that is necessary at first is to give me YOUR IDEA.

further procedure.
Persons desirous of obtaining really reliable advice or
information regarding Patent matters are cordially in-
vited to consult me personally or by letter. In all cases
a careful consideration of their plans, an honest opinion,
and a prompt reply may be expected.

THE DUMB MAID.
A beautiful and fascinating Maid
As graceful as a queen Devine,
Of high and ancestral fame
Is the dumb maid Caroline :
Her eyes are blue and sparkling,
Her cheeks like a lily fair ;
Down her back in waving tresses
Hang her glittering golden hair;
Yet at times a cloud of sorrow
Shade the brow of the queen Devine,
A yearning for the power of speech
Doth sadden sweet Caroline.

The eldest son of the lady who is usually
identified with Margaret Catchpole was given
a grant from Governor Macquarie in Tas-
mania, and settled on it, and a grandson of
the lady still lives on the property at the
advanced age of 90 years. In his prime, he
had some distinction in politics, on the turf
and in out-door sports. One of his horses
once ran second for the Melbourne Cup.
Though all his life he has been connected
with racing, it is said he has never made a
wager.
“Brass Button”: Still another captain,
whose ship was recently lost, has declined
with thanks the offer of another ship from
the same co., and has decided that it’s time
he devoted himself to the serious job of
growing potatoes or running a hen farm.
Landsmen may often have noticed how
many captains go down with their ships,
and seamen will tell you (if they know you
well enough) of captains who have deliber-
ately done the Casabianca act when they
might easily have swum clear. They will
tell you, too, of as good seamen as ever trod
a deck and held a master’s ticket who could
never get better than mate again after their
ship had tripped over a rock or rubbed noses
with another ship. The explanation is
simple. Lloyd’s keep a record of captains.
If anything happens to a ship a note is
made against her captain’s name —just a
bare note without mention of any extenuat-
ing circs, or act-of-God remarks. When a
ship comes up for insurance her captain’s
name is looked up. If any unpleasant hap-
penings are ticked off to him, the rates on
that ship are liable to go up ; and most ship-
owners have a prejudice against employing
captains who are going to cost them heavier
insurance rates. It’s a cold-blooded busi-
ness, but it explains why so many captains
go down with the ship rather than go down
without it.

The Inky Way.
Sydney Daily Telegraph advertises for an over-
seer for its mechanical department; salary, £450.
This reminds The Bulletin that the D.T. —the most
ferociously anti-Melbourne of journals, whose editor
eats a Victorian devilled on toast every morning be-
fore breakfast, in order to get proper color for his
leaders —was started by Melbourne men and capital
(what little they had), and is to a large extent
run by Melbourne men. Instead of adding to its
advertisement, “No men from Melbourne need ap-
ply ! ” King-street has generally gone to Melbourne
for its mechanical bosses. Frecker, Christian, Bowen,
E. W. O’Sullivan, Jack Jones —the succession of over-
seers—were all Melbourneites. .Evidently this pan-
dering to the cabbage-tree mob idea in Sydney pays.
It must, or the Melbourne men on the D.T. wouldn t
put their own pot on. # #
“Wodonga” : Joseph Russell Crone, who died at
Glebe (Svdney) lately, had been off the Inky Way
for some time; but he kept in touch with the craft.
He was the most versatile penman I ever met. The
son of a noted Methody minister in England, he him-
self chucked the pulpit to shake a freer leg in jour-
nalism. I don’t know how he could preach, but he
was a most eloquent lecturer; besides, he was a fine
singer, was chosen to play Roberts when the Cham-
pion billiardist visited the Ovens, captained the team
of cricketers that played against Grace’s Eleven,- and
was organist at any old church that wanted a lift.
And he was as fearless a man as was ever in a coun-
try newspaper office. The boss used to put copy
on the file in Crone’s absence. When Crone returned
the copy was overhauled, and as often as not ripped
up under the boss’s eyes. In later years he was
night sub-editor on jthe Sydney D.T. #
Guy Innes, who succeeds the late Jack Nish ,as
news editor of Melbourne Herald, has had a flashing
journalistic run. Less than 10 years ago he was
assistant to A. Holts:, secretary to the Argus general
manager. He was then principally engaged in taking
shorthand notes for various Departmental heads.
This brought him under the eye of E. S. Cunning-
ham, chief of the reporting staff at that time, who
collared him, and he was soon in charge of the State
Parliamentary staff. After a spell as assistant sub-
editor, Innes, while still under 30, jumped into
Nish’s place as chief sub. Though he had been
looked on as the “white-haired boy” of the office,
and was thought to have the Argus editor’s pen in
his portmanteau, he didn’t hesitate long over the
Herald’s offer of higher screw and day work.
PAPER TEETH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
False teeth made of paper, in Germany, are said
to retain their color as well as porcelain ones,- and
to be stronger and cheaper.— News item.
Dash-Boyang was an editor who owned a “Liberal”
sheet,
A fussy, little yappy thing that yelped in some back
street;
! It had no soul to sell for ads.—’twas hardly ever
bought—
But still to Liberalism it “extended its support” ;
For Claude Dash-Boyang hated well the common
working-class.
“Blokes” mocked his tall and shiny hat, and said
he was an ass ;
His great ambition was to know the suburb’s
“smartest set”—
Of course, for that you must not bow to common
men who sweat.
(Please note that Liberals “perspire” ; ’tis Laborites
who “sweat.”)
But somehow all the local peers went blind, when he
drew near;
Their consorts, when his name came up, screamed
faintly “Oh, my deahl
That dreadful man! I think he writes that papah
over theah!
That silly little rag, you know 1 His waife can’t do
her hair!
I met him at a meeting of the League two years ago;
Of course I’m always civil—it’s good policy, you
k-now;
He’s useful at elections when we want the women’s
votes —
We drive him round and let him ram waild state-
ments down their throats.
(He talks most fri’ful nonsense, but he rams it
down their throats.)”
Dash-Boyang worked and worried in his small and
futile way,
To burst the Labor bounders without either praise
or pay;
The yelping of his paper in the back suburban street
Brought neither ads. nor readers —’twas a most
pathetic sheet.
The hobbled dames he worked for never asked his
wife to tea;
She lived as far from Iceland as from High Sas-
siety;
Still for the “Lib’ral” party poor Dash-Boyang
fiercely fought,
Until he brought up standing in the dark Insolvent
Court.
(His organ shrill had busted—hence the dark Insol-
vent Court.)
A man of law, surmounted by a pile of horsehair
grey,
Said, “How do you explain this?” Then Dash-
Boyang blazed away:
“I’ve fought the ‘Lib’rals’ battle and I’ve drawn the
Tiger’s fangs,
But gained no thanks or credit from these snobbish
‘Lib’ral’ gangs.
The Socialistic Tiger but for me would now be
loose” —
The Registrar said sharply: “Stop! That sort of
talk’s no use!”
His little eyes like embers glowed. His clerkling
grinned beneath.
“We want a few particulars re your financial teeth !”
(A paper minion headed it: “His Own Financial
Teeth.”)
It made a good half-column, but it broke Dash-
Boyang’s heart;
He tottered from that awful place too much unnerved
to start
Once more upon the Tiger and his deeply-rooted
tusks;
That way lay ruination, flanked with cups of tea and
husks.
The sally of the Registrar kept ringing in his brain ;
He thought and spoke of naught but teeth until,
beneath the strain,
His mind gave way. He did a graceful dance upon
the tiles,
Then called a cab and hurried off with all his pre-
cious flies.
(Most editors when crazy cling like burglars to their
flies.)
The months went by (a process this that Man can
never stay) ;
Dash-Boyang once again was seen upon the Inky
Way;
He had a suit-case in each fist; he wore an eager
air,
And asked if anyone has seen “the Tiger” any-
where.
“I’ve got a bag of teeth,” he said ; “enough to fill a
shark!
They said my paper had no bite to back its little
bark 1
Well, now it’s nothing else but bite! Here! open
this and seel
Then whistle up that Tiger and just keep your eyes
on me!”
(They rapidly encabbed him for a madhouse by the
sea.)
N.s.w.
NATHANIEL LOBSTER.

APOLOGY
The Daily Telegraph Newspaper Co., Ltd.,
v. Australian Workers’ Union.
WE, The Australian Workers’ Union, hereby
publicly Apologise to The Daily Telegraph
Newspaper Co., Ltd., and its Directors and
Conductors, for having printed to the order of a
customer a Leaflet attributing the political advocacy
of “The Daily Telegraph” in the recent Referendum
Campaign to grossly• corrupt motives. We now
place it on record that we have no sympathy with
the defamatory statements contained in the leaflet
complained of, and hereby admit that there never was
any foundation for making the same ; and in considera-
tion of The Daily Newspaper Co., Ltd., foregoing its
claim for damages and discontinuing this action, we
(The Australian Workers’ Union) hereby agree to
publish, at our own expense, this apology in such
papers, in such conspicuous places therein, as The
Daily Newspaper Co., Ltd., shall direct, and will also
pay such costs and expenses as between Attorney and
Client as The Daily Telegraph Newspaper Co,, Ltd.,
has been put to in the matter.
(Signed) THOS. WHITE,
Acting President,
Australian Workers’ Union.
Pa ted this 18th day of August, 1911.
