IN BAN ANA LAND
“L. Lonesome”: Tlease kill “C.R.W.” (P».
3/8/’ll) before he does any damage.
He suggests running ostriches on the prickly -
pear infested country in Queensland. Seeing
that the spread of the pest is very largely
due to the emu, the introduction of the
ostrich would be a good deal like inoculat-
ing a man with plague to cure him of two
broken legs.
The Irish bull is still good for a roar or
two. Hazelton, M.P., the Irish envoy
(whose wonderful socks and “Vandorian’s”
Bulletin paragraph about them gave Car-
dinal Moran about the last laugh of his
life), was the animal’s showman this time.
At his first meeting in Brisbane, talking of
the Veto Bill, he wound up a fine burst by
remarking that happily the arms of the
House of Lords were now tied hand and
foot.
The sugar dispute has resulted in the
spoils of victory for both parties. The
workers have got their 30s. a week, and an
eight-hour day in the mills. The C.S.R. is
getting £2 per ton extra for its sugar. The
only sufferer is the Australian public, which
is paying at the rate of over half a million
a year extra for its sugar. And it almost
deserves to do it, for having been such a
fool as to vote “No” on the question of the
Commonwealth control of monopolies.
“N.,” about a little devil: —
Professor Ross, of Liverpool, has almost destroyed
the mosquitoes in Port Said, where they were a ter-
rible nuisance—-and Queensland ought at once to be
all ears. So thoroughly has the reform been accom-
plished that people there have given up sleeping
under mosquito curtains. These mosquitoes are not
the anopheles, or malaria-carriers, but the familiar
sort that we have in Australia. The anopheles had
been destroyed in the great yellow fever centres of
Havana and Panama, and Yellow Jack went. Ross
simply applied the same physic to the Port Said
dague, pouring petroleum on the stagnant water.
The painstaking work involved a visit to every
house in Port Said. The ignorant natives resisted
at first, but most of them soon learned to co-operate.
The cost in the first and second years of treatment
was only a little over £2OOO altogether, and £BOO
for the third and last year. And this latter expendi-
ture will be necessary every year. The mosquito lays
about 300 eggs each time, so a little mosquito goes
a long way. And yet not very far in another sense,
for they only travel a few yards from home —the
males in particular.
On the Pillow.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ihe nation slept on the Treaty and woke a dupe.
—Chevalier D’Eon.
The Jap has been signing his Nipponese name,
And swearing that fighting is none of his game;
He’s promised to keep for a decade so low
That none would suspect he was ever a foe ;
And we sing willow, tit willow!
We sigh with relief
At a respite so brief,
And put down our heads on the pillow!
We’d just made a start with our drum and our drill,
And the marching and wheeling made some of us ill ;
■ We wrote to the papers as “Mother of Ten,”
And wailed that our boys should be turned into
men ;
But now —-sing willow, tit willow !
Our troubles are o’er,
And we’re ready to snore
In peace and content on the pillow!
We never had much of a taste for the gun
And war, if it threatened— why couldn’t we runt
if Togo or Nogi should visit our coast,
They couldn’t do much to a runaway host;
But now —sing hooray for the willow !
It’s football and cricket,
And hey! for the wicket,
And then for a.snooze on the pillow!;
With blood and with iron old Bismarck would say
He’d.build up a folk that would front any fray;
But we — we are hoping with paper and pen
To bind the wild beasts on the bosoms of men,
To spread a sweet peace on the billow,
To send off the Jap
In a Rip Winkle nap,
Without any dreams on his pillow.
Of course when the Asian is ready to smite,
He’ll give a year’s warning in fashion polite!
But somewhere I’ve read of a land “in the soup,”
"It slept on a Treaty and tooke as a dupe l”
But now —sing willow, tit willow !
Forebodings are dead,
And we creep off to bed,
And snuggle our heads on the pillow!
The Land of Go Easy is close to our coast,
Its people they cling like’ a wreath to a pqst;
Some day ’twill be whispered (in pity, I ween),
“'Twas the Land of the Loan and the Land of the
Lean!”
But leaning—O willow, tit willow!
It comes to an end
With the people who spend
A decade asleep on the pillow.
QU KENSLAND.
RIP VAN WINK.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, August 19, 1911.
My Dear Thargomindaii,—
Our country cousins and “paying guests”
have floated back to their home anchorages,
buoyed up with new gowns and a feeling of
satisfaction that they had made the very
most of. their Carnival opportunities. The
grand finale occurred on Friday evening,
when Mrs. Arthur Feez and Mrs. William
Collins entertained vice-regality and a couple
of hundred friends at what the enthusiasts
describe as “the best ball of the season, my
dear, and the supper” ! There the nar-
rator gasps. Mere words fail. The dance-
floor was a polished clearing in a forest of
palms. Mrs. Collins wore white, in addi-
tion to her best diamonds; her co-hostess
donned black ninon; and Lady Bosanquet’s
diamonds blazed on white satin.
Before heading for your State, the Bosan-
quet couple ran out to Ascot on Saturday
with the Macgregor clan. Lady B. finished
up a heavy week’s programme without show-
ing a sign of fag. Don’t know how she felt
when she blew the candle out o’ nights, but
I know how I'd have felt —something like
the Macgregor cliiel, who caved-in and nailed
himself to his bunk for the week-end.
A CLEAR CONSCIENCE.
MR. COLD TEA: “Your vile trade is especially denounced in this Booh: ‘He that giveth
his neighbor strong drink ’”
MR. HOT STUFF: “ Look here, ole feller , I never gave anyone a drink in vie life.”
The Irish envoys are the really important
figures now. Redmond is a replica, on a
large scale, of William Redmond, and is
rather apt to talk “Uncle Willie.” A burly,
big-hearted Irishman, “the Cause” seems to
color his whole existence. Hazleton is dis-
tinguished* as the dandy of the party, and
Donovan as the only one who stirs his ora-
tory with a brogue. Anyhow, the combina-
tion has induced all of us —or, rather,, the
best of us—to give our Home Rule principles
a dust-up, and as many of us as possible
are doing it with spare bank-notes or negoti-
able cheques.
One of the prettiest of the new fluffers is
Jean Mort, a daughter of the Franklyn Vale
House of Mort, in whose honor Miss Edie
Davidson, a warbler of note, a few days ago
gathered in several of the Very Best in Girl-
dom. Later, Mamma Mort, before retiring
to Franklyn Vale, tea’d the same company,
plus their parents and friends. Daughter
Mort will remain behind with the Wilton
Loves. Another hostess during the week was
Mrs. Fox, of Dugandan station. She is an
Englishwoman with a “truly” complexion
and exquisite furs.
On Saturday last Mr. J. D. Henry, a
brother of Mrs. A. A. Doyle, one of our most
admired matrons, persuaded Miss Mabel Sin-
clair to chance the future on her ring finger.
She took the risk in white silk and orange
blossoms, and the only note of disappoint-
ment in the good wishes of the party, of
friends who subsequently gathered at Fairy-
knowe, the Sinclair homestead, was that the
popular lady will now live so far away—a
little matter of six days from Cloncurry.
An engagement of interest to Most of Us
concerns brown-eyed Phyllis Austen, whose
mother belongs to the Douglas clan, and a
Mr. Macartney, of your parts. Next month,
too, Bertha Morrison, of the Titian hair and
contralto voice, will make the usual promises
to Mr. Geary. And still the tally is not
complete until the coming nuptials of pretty
Maude Alexander and a gentleman named
Dixon have, been added.
I intended to write you an appreciation of
the Culchaw in our midst, my dear, but per-
haps you will understand it better if I re-
late a little incident which happened when
one of the Most Scholarly gathered her
friends and treated them to a Shakspearian
lecture. Said one charmer to her neighbor,
“Poetry is so delightful. Who is your fav-
orite poet?”- “I think I like Matthew’ Ar-
nold best,” replied the questioned one. “Ah,
yes,” cooed the first; “I do so love ‘The
Light of Asia’!”
At 59 Prince’s Square, London, are
gathered together quite a small colony of
Australians, among them being Mrs. Dal-
ryrnple, Miss Baird, Mrs. Ross (of War-
wick) and her two daughters, Mr. Waldron
Taylor (a son of Dr. Taylor), Mr. Andrew
Black (Brisbane’s coal king), and Mrs.
Walker (of Tenterfield) and her daughters.
Mrs. Lockhart Spence was also a member of
the colony till quite recenly, but has fled to
the Land of Not Too Many Cakes, while
Mrs. Bocock, a one-time literary light in this
city, has gone to Ireland, to renew her ac-
quaintance with various units of kinship.
The Andrew Menzies, whose resting-place
in London was also 59 Prince’s Square, at
latest information had started on a six-
weeks’ tour round the Continent. Before
leaving England they motored a party of
friends to Henley for the regatta.
Mr. and Mrs. J. A. Robertson propose to
return to Queensland from Britain some time
in August, but daughter will remain for an-
other six months, and will visit Switzerland
for the winter sports.
. The unanimous vote is that the team of
girl tennis athletes from your State can
not only play the game well, but give us
points in the manner of dressing for it.
Mesdames Ford and Sheldon, with their neat
short skirts and short-sleeved blouses, looked
specially business-like and smart when on
the courts.

At _ a cattle-throwing and tying competi-
tion in Brisbane the other day, Australian
Tom Lawton, using the Australian tail-
twisting method of throwing, got his bullock
down and tied him up in 59isec., while
“Broncho George” Looper, who used the
American method (lassoing), took Lnin.
37sec. over the job.
Died at Maryborough on August 16, G.
H. Burn, who was in the Customs service in
Bananaland 35 years, and was Sub-Collector
at Maryborough at the time of his retire-
ment two years ago. He was also returning
officer for Wide Bay, and had in that capa-
city got into the habit of declaring Prime
Minister Fisher duly elected.
Mahrigong homestead, outside Winton,
wanted a drop more water to wet itself
with. So it got to work, put a bore down
205 ft., struck water, and had a windmill up
and the moisture flowing, all inside eight
days. If it wasn’t for its pests and its re-
cords, Queensland would spend many more
lonely, silent nights than it does.
Premier Denham informed his People's
Progressive League recently that the chief
object of that association was “to maintain
the liberty of the individual, and to secure
his freedom from coercion whether applied
hy organised Capital or organised Labor.”
So, ot course, the Butterman’s League will
at once start talking earnestly to the meat
tiust, the sugar monopoly, the shipping
ring, and the timber combine!
Adamson, M.L.A., considers that the sec-
tion of' the Health Amendment Act requir-
ing chemists to state the formula! of their
proprietary medicines on the bottle or
packet “a grave injustice to a body of
highly cultured professional men.” Chemists
aren’t “professional men” any more than
cooks, nor are they of necessity more cul-
tured than navvies. But, even if they were,
why should they not be compelled to describe
correctly the articles which they sell, the
same as other shopkeepers?
Life is a strenuous business in Thatgo-
mindah. The other day the bridegroom neg-
lected to turn up at the wedding. The missing
swain was thereupon interviewed by the
father and brother of the bride—in 'vain.
Then the bride’s mamma took a hand. Or.
more correctly, she took a table-knife, and.
chased her intended son-in-law, until that
persecuted bachelor ran to the police for'
protection, followed by the injured damosel!
and her indignant relatives. Thargomindah>
evidently understands the “unwritten law"'
to mean the right of ill-treated ladies to pur-
sue the culprit with a carving-knife.

Kaiser and Cadets.
Berlin, 22nd November, 1910.
The Kaiser in addressing the Cadets urged them to become abstainers.
He said that the victory in the next war would go to the nation that had
the least consumption of alcohol.

intoxicating effect, and has the virtue when blended with Spirits of reducing
the effects of the alcohol. O.T. is the most suitable drink in the world for
abstainers, and for those who wish to be moderate.

is derived from Fruits and Herbs, recognised for their nutritive

Having the same warming, exhilarating and cheering effects as spirituous

FAMOUS BOLINDA

Within 25 Miles of Melbourne.
To
FARMERS, POTATO GROWERS, AGRICULTU-
RISTS, SHEEPBREEDERS, DAIRYMEN,

Great Subdivisional Sale
of 15,000 ACRES
Of the
FAMOUS BOLINDA VALE ESTATE,
Between Sunbury & Lancefield Junction,
SUBDIVIDED INTO FARMS
From 100 to 600 Acres.
Liberal Terms of 10 Years.
Long Frontage to the Emu and Deep Creeks,
Rich Flats and Undulating Rises.
WS. KEAST and Messrs. P. LEARMONTH and
Co. (in conjunction) have received instruc-
• tions from the owners to SELL PRI-
VATELY,
THAT MAGNIFICENT PROPERTY,
Being the first subdivision of the Bolinda Vale
Estate, consisting of 15,000 acres of rich agri-
cultural and grazing land, which has been
held by the Clarke family since 1838.
On this portion of the estate much land will be
found equal to a great deal of the Western district
land. Practically all virgin soil and numerous rich
Hats.
This estate is specially adapted for agriculture
and the growing of wheat, oats and barley, hard
to surpass, and much of it suitable for potato-
growing.
Magnificent dairying land, and is excellent coun-
try for sheep and lamb rattening, those from
Bolinda Vale having for many years realised top
prices in the Melbourne markets. It is the home
of the famous shorthorn herd of Sir Rupert Clarke,
which have always taken the principal prizes in
all the Australian shows, and are so -favorably
known throughout the Commonwealth.
This property has an average rainfall of about
24in., and is situated in one of the best agricul-
tural districts in and around Melbourne, and is
ea]table of very great production.
For the past 50 years this property has been
used for the breeding of sheep and cattle.
The situation is about 25 miles from Melbourne,
three miles from Sunbury, and runs up within half-
mile of the Lancefield junction railway station at
the other end, and joins the railway where a siding
is proposed to be constructed.
Liberal terms of 10 years will be given, namely,
10 per cent, cash, an instalment equal to 10 per
cent, of the total amount of the purchase money
in 1,2, 3, and 4 years, and the balance of the pur-
chase money at the end of 10 years.
Auctioneers beg to draw special attention to this
magnificent property, within 25 miles of Melbourne,
and would ask northern buyers who have recently
sold their properties, before inspecting elsewhere, to
allow us to show them this estate; it is the first
time an acre of it has been subdivided and put on
the market.
The situation is one of the best in Australia, with
a climate unsurpassed. Sheep and cattle can be
driven off the property in one day and put in the
market the next morning. The freight from Lance-
field Junction is only 4s. 6d. per ton.
What has taken place in other estates cut up by
Sir Rupert Clarke is bound to take place in this
estate, and there is sure to be a big increase in
value.
Its nearness to Melbourne, its low freights, its
great carrying capacities, its productiveness for
agriculture, and good rainfall, and, possessing as
it does, so many advantages over other estates, we
can with every confidence recommend an early in-
spection.
By giving two days’ notice to the auctioneers,
buyers will he met at either Sunbury or Lancefield
Junction railwav stations, and. driven over the pro-
pertv free of charge.
The vendor has agreed to allow any outside agent
introducing an approved buyer 1 per cent, com-
mission.
For further particulars apply
W. S. KEAST, Auctioneer, etc., 610 Collins
Street, Melbourne; or
P. LEARMONTH 8 CO., Hamilton, Heywood
and Casterton.

THROW AWAY YOUR PIPE
■ and
SMOKE MY CIGARS
I'VE knocked around a bit over Australasia in
my time —been in the back parts of pretty
well every State and New Zealand.
I’ve mixed with shearers and musterers and
rouseabouts at different sheds; been at cattle
camps in Queensland; up among the snow
country of Monaro- in the jarrah forests of
Westralia; in mining camps all over the Com-
monwealth; and everywhere Ive seen the boys
smoking their pipes—and seldom a sign of a
Cigar,
Then, I’ve pulled out my case and handed
some DIADEMAS around. And after all this,
people say to me—“ Oh, Australians won’t smoke
Cigars!”
gdfg
I do just get riled when I
hear this, J tell you that
nearly every p : pe-smolter,
once he gets the taste of my
DIADEMAS Cigars, swears
by them. They knock pipe-
smoking hollow!
There’s nothing but To-
bacco in them —and the best
Cigar Tobacco that is grown
-—the wrapper, finest Java
leaf; and the inside filling,
all clean, straight, long Brazil.
They draw easily and burn
freely, for they are hand-
rolled by as good Cigar-
makers as there are any-
where in the world.
The boys out-back I’ve
given them to, as I said, just
swear by my Cigars, Men
in the Cigar trade here, too
■ —men with whom Cigar-
smoking is a life habit—come
to me and say—“ Look here,
those Cigars of yours are
better than any we can get
in the city. Let us have a
box as a favor."
Now, I want you to try
my Cigars —at my ex-
pense.
I want to convert you from
the pipe to Cigars-—to my
Cigars. All I wantisfor you
to buy my Cigars as against
your pipe and favorite to-
bacco —and I know you’ll be
a regular customer of mine.
I’ll post a box of 100 DIADEMAS right to
your address for 22/6. Smoke 5 6°f them,
and if they’re not as good as the best 6d. Cigar
you can buy send the rest of the Cigars back,
and I’ll return every penny of your money. I'll
make no deduction for the Cigars you’ve smoked
There’s no funny business about this offer,
mind. I’ll keep to every word of it, and there 11
be no “back answers ” if you want your money
back. If you want to know the kind of man
you’re dealing with, write to the General
Manager, the Commercial Bank, Melbourne,
and he’ll tell you I’m a clean, straight chap in all
my dealings.
Send 22/6 to-day, and get the box of 100
DIADEMAS by return post. In ordering, say
whether you like the mild, medium, or strong
grade.
My Only Address is

OBJECTS.
THIS COMPANY has been Incorporated with the Objects stated in the Memorandum of
Association, and primarily to acquire from “Mamara Option Limited” certain Freehold Lands
and Plantations in the Island of Guadalcanal - , British Solomon Islands—known as the
Mamara Plantations—and to carry on and further develop the same.
PURCHASE PRICE.
The price to be paid for the property is £lOO,OOO, which will be paid and satisfied as
follows:—As to £40,000 by the allotment of 40,000 fully paid-up shares of this Company to
“Mamara Option Limited” or its nominees, and as to the balance by the payment of £15,000
in cash on Ist October, 1911, and £45,000 on 7th June, 1921, the latter sum with interest
thereon at the rate of 5 per cent, per annum being secured by a mortgage over the property.
The Company takes over the property from Ist June, 1911, but interest on the mortgage
does not begin to run until Ist October, 1911. The Company also pays the Vendors for their
stock in trade of the store at landed cost as on the Ist June, 1911.
All the Directors and the Promoters of this Company are shareholders in the Vendor
Company, which receives 20,000 of the above mentioned fully paid-up shares, and £lOOO in
cash, the balance of the purchase price being received by Captain Oscar Svensen and Joseph
Theodore d’Oliveyra as part of the consideration for the sale of their properties to the
Vendor Company. BRIEF PARTICULARS OF. MAMARA.
The property has been reported on by Mr. H. N. Leach, Agricultural Editor of the
“Queenslander,” and Brisbane “Courier,” an acknowledged authority on Island matters.
The following is a brief resume of his report:—
1. SITUATION—At the N.W. end of the Island of Guadalcanar, British Solomon
Islands, adjoining Lever Bros.’ largest plantations.
2. COMMUNICATION—By steamer with Sydney and Brisbane every six weeks.
3. TlTLE—Freehold.
4. RATES AND TAXES—NONE.
5. AREA—Approximately 5000 acres, having about 6 miles of sea frontage.
6. SOIL —First class for Cocoanuts and Cotton, also some excellent Rubber land.
7. RAlNFALL—Averages about 102 inches per annum, well distributed throughout the
year, and more than sufficient for all requirements.
8. HURRlCANES—Hurricanes, Cyclones and Typhoons are unknown in the Solomons,
which gives them a very great advantage over Fiji and the New Hebrides.
0. CULTIVATION.—Rubber: 15 acres, planted 1910. Cotton: 109 acres, in full bearing.
Cocoanuts: 42 acres, planted 1905; 200 acres, planted 1906-7; 300 acres, planted
1908; 313 acres, planted 1909-10; 145 acres, planted 1911.
N.B. —The Plantation Plans show about 1010 acres of cocoanuts, but Mr. Leach’s
estimate of 1000 acres has been accepted for the sake of being on the safe side.
10. POSSIBILITIES—At least 2000 acres of first-class cocoanut land and 1000 acres of
rubber land remain to be planted. The cotton area can also be increased to at
least 1000 acres.
1L PLANTATIONS—The plantations have been laid out and worked on the most
scientific lines, and are unusually clear of weeds and plant pests.
12. DRAlNAGE—Particular attention has been paid to drainage, over 4 miles of drains
having been cut through the property,
13. PLANTING—The Cocoanuts have all been grown from carefully selected seed nuts
and have been planted 27 feet apart, or 52 to the acre.
14. COCOANUTS —42 acres planted in 1905 are now in bearing, and the balance of the
3000 acres will come into bearing gradually during the next 6 years, and by the
year 1917 the entire 1000 acres will be in bearing.
15. COTTON—About 109 acres are under cotton, 64 acres of which is a special hybrid
perpetual, developed on the property by Mr. d’Oliveyra, and known as “Mamara,”
which sells readily at from 1/- to 1/4 per lb., and the seed at 10/- per lb.
16. RUBBER—A small area only is under rubber, but sufficient to prove that it
flourishes on the higher ground, which, being unsuitable for cocoanuts, can thus
be turned to account.
17. SAWMILL—The only sawmill in the Solomons is on Mamara, and the local demand
for timber is more than sufficient to absorb the entire output.
18. TIMBER —There is on the sea front a quantity of Yandilla, an ornamental timber
of tfrecedai - class, worth at least 30/- per 100 super, feet in Sydney, and costing
about 12/- to 15/- per 100 super, feet landed there. This timber is also in great
request locally at 20/- per 100 super, feet at the mill, at which price it shows a
very good profit.
19. STORE—The Store not only supplies Mamara itself, but also the surrounding
plantations, and a profit of over £lOOO accrued from this source last year,
which amount is capable of considerable increase.
20. BUILDINGS—AII necessary buildings are already in existence, but the Overseers’
bungalows require some slight expenditure to make them thoroughly suitable;
and the erection of a kiln for drying copra is recommended. The total cost of
this will not exceed £250.
21. LABOR—About 100 indentured natives are already at work, and others can be
obtained in the group at a cost of about £7 per head on two to three years’
terms. Wages and allowances amount to about £7 per man per annum.
The Islands being controlled by the BRITISH IMPERIAL GOVERNMENT, there is
no legislation tending to discourage the employment of the colored labor neces-
sary for the development of the property.
22. WHITE SUPERVISION —An agreement has been made with Mr. J. T. d’Oliveyra,
wffio has had charge, of the Plantation since 1905, to pay at least two visits a
year during the next two years, to inspect the work to be carried on under the
supervision of three white overseers, two of whom have been on the property
since 190 S, and are thoroughly familiar with the best methods of working the
plantation. f .
The Company will also have the assistance on the Board of Captain Svenson, probably
the best-known and most-successful planter in the Solomons, whose advice anil
experience will be invaluable.
DEVELOPMENT, PRODUCTS and PROSPECTS.
(1) DEVELOPMENT.
While the Company cannot be considered as a development proposition pure and
simple, seeing that without any further development whatever the area already
planted would in seven years’ time return at least £15,000 a year net profit (25
per cent, on the minimum capital, £60,000), the Directors recognise that to secure
the full benefit of the purchase they wall require to expend a large amount of
capital during the next few years in developing and extending the plantation.
They propose to plant from 250 to 300 acres of Cocoanuts a year until at least an
additional 1500 (one thousand five hundred) acres have been planted, and about
1000 acres of Rubber by sections of from 250 to 300 acres a year, as additional
labor becomes available.
While the Cocoanuts are maturing, Cotton will be grown as a catch crop between
the young trees.
The cost of planting cotton is not more than 5/- per acre, and at least twice this amount
will be saved in the reduced cost of weeding and keeping the plantation clean during the four
years or so which elapse before the cocoanut trees are sufficiently advanced to render the
growing of a catch crop unnecessary, and in the meantime large profits will be received
from the cotton crop, which is ready for picking at about nine months from date of planting,
and in the case of the “Mamara” variety gives a profitable return for four years.
The plant of the sawmill will also be extended immediately) with the object of increasing
the output to 3000 feet per day, at which rate there is at least two years’ work in hand.
(2) PRODUCTS.
(A) COCOANUTS AND COPRA —The yield of Cocoanuts and Copra in the Solomons is
mugh greater than in Ceylon and Fiji.
Mr. Leach reports that Government Returns inspected by him show that eight-year-
old trees on the Government Plantation on Shortland Island PLANTED 35
TREES TO THE ACRE ONLY, are returning 24cwt. of copra per annum, and
young (six-year-old) trees at the Rua Sura Mission are returning 13cvvt. of copra
per annum.
Mr. Leach anticipates at least an equally good return per tree from the Mamara
plantation, which, as previously stated, is PLANTED 52 TREES TO THE
ACRE, but in making up the Estimates contained in this Prospectus the yield
has been estimated as under:—
6- old trees, lOcwt. per acre.
7- trees, 15cwt. per acre.
8- trees, 20cwt. per acre.
The price of copra varies somewhat, but the general tendency is upwards owing t
increased use of late years in the manufacture of butter substitutes.
Seven factories have already been started in Germany and two in France for this nurons
and a large one is now being built in Chicago. The industry is a very profitable one and i
sure to spread to other parts of the world, which cannot fail to have the effect of advanein
the price of copra.
The price of copra in Sydney on 12th July, 1911, was £2l 10s. per ton, equal to at lenct
£l9 10s. per ton at the Plantations. This leaves a net profit of £l6 10s. after allowing the amn'e
margin of £3 per ton for the cost of manufacture.
In the calculations shown a net profit of £l5 per ton has been assumed.

.Islands will produce rubber of the best quality, the soil being volcanic and very rich.”
übber begins to make a return in the fifth year from date of planting. In the calculations

(D) TIMBER—Mr. Minge, the practical sawmiller who originally built the sawmill and
started the timber industry on Mamara, reports that, with the addition of a 20-h.p. engine
oonu y ) '7‘ p ' b ®iler and an extra saw bench, the capacity of the mill can be increased to
3000 feet per day, at which rate he considers there will be at least four years’ full work.
The cost of production is estimated by Mr. Minge about 4/- per super, feet at the mill, and the
selling price locally is 20/- per 100 super, feet, at which price the entire output can
probably be disposed of, leaving 16/- per 100 super, feet profit, Any surplus can be
dlspose(i of m Brisbane or Sydney at a profit of at least 10/- per 100 super, feet.
When the new machinery is installed and the output increased the net profit from timber
aione tor the first three or four years should approximate £5OOO per annum, but for the
purpose of this prospectus has been estimated at £2500 for two years and £lOOO for the
third year. A two years’ contract has been made with Mr. Minge to take charge of the
.... ™^L at a sa ? ar y, of f 5 per week and a royalty of 3d. per 100 super, feet sawn.
(E) STORE—The Store is. a highly profitable undertaking, and the business done is capable
of considerable extension. The profit from this source for the year 1910 exceeded £llOO,
tae ® mpl °y ment °f additional labor should quickly increase to from £l5OO to
± 000 a year. In the calculations included in this prospectus the expected profit has been
taken at £lOOO a year throughout.

On these estimates it will be seen that the Company should be able
1. To earn on an average 20 per cent, per annum for the first 12 years on the entire
Capital of £80,00(1.
2. To expend on development work £25,000 out of profits in the same period.
3. To bring the value of the property up to nearly £300,000 on a 10 per cent, basis by
the end of that period.
r 4. To earn over 35 per cent, per annum net thereafter.
The plantation should reach its maximum development in about 20 years’ time, when the

Intending: investors should bear in mind that altliougfli koine what slow in development
with respect to the area not already under cultivation, a plantation well managed is one of
the safest forms of investment.
On this subject the Financial Editor of LONDON “Truth,” writing under date Ist June.
1910, said:—
The prospects of the leading producing Companies are more rosy and established
than any other industrial investments in the world Neither home rails, mining,
land, oil, or any other form of investment can forecast the future on such satisfac-
tory lines as the best rubber estates. Even with rubber at 2/- per lb. the produc-
ing companies will earn profits beyond the dreams of any mining promoter in the
heyday of the Australian and Kaffir booms.”
That this is equally true of Cocoanut plantations is shown by Mr. Bremner’s figures
already quoted.
This property offers a good immediate return on capital invested, with the certainty of
something very much better in the near future.

the Memorandum and Articles of Association, and of the Reports above mentioned, may he
inspected at the office of the Company’s Solicitors.

Brokers, or to the Secretary of the Company at the Registered Office, 143 George Street,
Brisbane.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will
be glad to hear of a simple and scientific de-
vice which will restore your hearing, no mat-
ter how old you we. There is no mystery
or secrecy about it; we describe it fully and
explain just how it acts.
You use spectacles as sight magnifiers, and
in the same way you may use this sound
magnifier as an aid to weak hearing. But,
unlike spectacles, the Wilson Common-sense
Ear-Drums are invisible and comfortable.
You can’t tell you are wearing them. No
one else can, either, because they are not
seen.
They rest the ear nerves by taking the
strain off them—the strain of trying to hear
dim sounds. What a blessed relief to have
done with that tiring strain!
Being made of the purest and softest Para
rubber, they cannot be felt, even when the
head rests on a pillow. For the same reason,
a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. In fact, they protect the ear, the
tender inner parts that are so sensitive, from
cold winds, dust, sudden and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand why
these facts are true, and why the Sound Mag-
nifier restores the hearing, we give an illus-
tration of one as it lies in the ear. You will
notice that the Sound Magnifier is shaped
like a trumpet, and so conveys even the
faintest sounds right to the natural ear-
drum. Being of the softest rubber, it cannot
sdfsd
hurt the ear. It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort. The division
that runs across the mouth is to allow the re-
moval and insertion of the Ear-Drum, and to
brace it.
The shape of the Wilson Ear-Drum has
been chosen after years of experience as the
best adapted to carry the faintest sound right
down to the natural ear-drum.
To persons who are nearly deaf, it is not
only trying to have people shout at them,
but it hurts the ear-drum. When age comes
the ear-drum sometimes gets thickened, or
loses its tightness, and shouting does not act
properly on it. It only makes a confused
and tiring noise. But the Sound Magnifiers
concentrate the sound in the best spot for
perfect hearing.
Of course, if you have lost the hearing-
nerve completely, or were born deaf, we can-
not pretend to cure you. Only a miracle
could do that, in spite of some of the absurd
claims made to-day. But if you can hear the
faintest sound, do not despair. You will
never regret trying the Wilson Ear-Drums.
We have letters to show you from deaf people
in Australia and New Zealand who have been
cured, and you can be cured, too. You may
wear the Ear-Drums day and night, and take
them out whenever you wish. Children can
wear them (we have special sizes for children
from six to twelve). Even a child can insert
and remove them without the least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s
single visit). This includes a pair of Drums
(they are only sold in pairs), a fine nickel
remover and also an inserter. After the first
pair is bought you may purchase a single
drum at any time for Bs., but the pair you
get at first will last quite two years. Do not
ask for a trial pair. We do not send them
out on trial, and so they are never second-
hand. Use the Order Form below, and the
Outfit will be sent to you at once, with the
fullest instructions. Don’t delay, but have
the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can.
ORDER FORM.
STAR NOVELTY CO.,
103 Premier Buildings,
229 Collins Street, Melbourne (Vic.).

serter and a Remover, for which I enclose
One Pound.

CURED AT 76
Three Years’ Suffering from Complication of Biliousness and Gastritis —
Could Keep Nothing Down —Gave Up All Hope of Recovery.
NOW HALE AND HEARTY AT 85
-THANKS TO BILE BEANS.
MRS. A. FULLERTON, residing in Allen
Street. Woollongabba, South Brisbane,
says“ Some 12 years ago I had a
terrible illness, which afflicted me for three long
years. An excess of bile got into my system, and
I was hardly expected to live. I grew so weak
that I was utterly helpless, ray son having to
help me about; in fact, I was a perfect wreck, and
a shadow of my former self. The symptoms
seemed to point to a complication of biliousness
and gastritis, for I used to retch up everything I
ate or drank, the straining being terrible, and the
pains in my stomach unbearable. I also suffered
agonies from indigestion. The pains in the chest
and back and between the shoulders were dreadful
Housework was out of the question, and I gradually
grew worse and worse, until all hopes were given up for
my recovery. I tried every description of medicine and
so-called remedies in my search for a cure, but I could
not get relief from any of them. They did me absolutely
no good whatever, and my money was simply thrown
away. My son, reading that Bile Beans were a proved
cure for ailments similar to those that I was suffering
from, purchased a box of this splendid medicine and
persuaded me to try them- After the first few doses I

realised that Bile Beans were doing me a lot of good, so I continued taking them, and
began to pick up strength, the biliousness entirely abating, and my stomach being

Yamba.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HIGH Northern suns their brazen
shields,
Like warriors, hung above the fields
Where Sultan Summer frankly yields
His plenteous boon;
Like nautcli girls dance the naked days
Down avenues of cane and maize,
While on his pipes the North-Wind plays
A careless tune.
From ranges walled, through gates ajar
At Copmanhurst and Yugilbar,
Pours down the Clarence from afar
His noble tide;
And, spreading forth in creek and arm,
Enriches with his waters warm
A fertile land of field and farm
In deltas wide.
And, when this vassal task is done,
With all his winding courses run,
He seeks reward for service won ;
And to his queen,
The shining Sea, whose silver shores
Are musical with rhythmic oars,
At last his singing soul outpours
By Yamba green !
The depths of noon are rarely stirred
By restless foot or roving bird ;
But in the topaz morn are heard —-
Like lutes afar,
Of suitors calling from the green
Recess of gardens Florentine—
The reed birds in their nests unseen
By Yamba bar.
As tuneful heralds of a feast,
When Morning flaunts the conquered East,
With scarlet regiments released
From Night’s duress,
Beyond the lawns, where fall in showers
Of snow the great magnolia flowers,
The magpies from their leafy towers
Their joys express.
By rolling marsh and rainbowed stream
The opal-tinted dewdrops gleam •
And cluster in a rajah’s dream;•
First spoils of Day
From hyacinth and- lily swept,
They pearl the vouches where they slept,
As purple princes who bewept
The Night’s delay.
Oh, Land of drowsy days sublime
Where dwelled my Heart, in rest and rhyme,
Through all a golden summertime!
In dreams again
I see thy sunlit splendors blaze ;
And, walking in a moonlit maze,
I hear the night wind where it plays
Among the cane.
Lsee, untrammelled of the town,
Young Chloris in her skirt of brown,
Ip lifted coyly, urging down
The spotted kine.
White petticoats, like homing sails,
' '"tne fltitt’ring o’er the stockyard rails,
And, by the polished milking pails,
Bring up aline.
Where netted jungles green the blue
Distfactions of the hillward view,
The crested pigeons call and coo
With ruffled throats;
And Chloris, was it right or wrong
That all the burden of their song
Was “I—love —you” the whole day long
In pleading notes?
tlis floral torch in upward blaze
To light the darkened jungle ways,
Ih spring the Northern flame-tree lays;
And Chloris, fain
I'd play, as in sweet hours agone,
That quiet woodland stage upon
A warm, impassioned, Corydon
To you again.
Alas, the dramas that have been!
The ravished musk and myrtle green
That crowned a comely country queen
Are dead and sere:
But Mem’ry, folded like a rose
Long pressed between book pages, throws
A fragrance from the Past that shows
Our hearts how dear
Were tho£e enraptured hours agone,
When through the woodlands, on and on,
Sweet Chloris strayed with Corydon
Love’s pathway far;
When by the pen of seeming chance,
Was written in the Book, “Romance,”
A line that flashes like a lance
By Yamba bar.
A, gallant, fed with swift desire,
Uplifted then his crest of fire
And smote upon a burning lyre
_ The Northern sun;
A lady in her laces white
To be the bridesmaid of delight
Came forth the ivory-bosomed Night,
Besought and won.
By silver sea and golden sand
We twain have wandered hand in hand,
W hen all the world was lovers’ land,
My Chloris dear;
Nor shall the years our mem’ries cheat
Of love that triumphed in defeat,
Though long the musk and myrtle sweet
Be dead and sere.
VICTORIA.
E. J. BRADY.
The Earthquake at Carson’s.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WE were discussing wire-strainers
when Lignum Bill broke in.
“Y’ can have all yer patent
strainers for me,” he says. “Give me a fork
stick, and I’ll bust any wire that was ever
run through a post. When I was on Car-
son's, down west of Young, I put up a per-
formance with the fork which ain’t never-
been beat.
. Carson believed in big posts and big
wire. A lot of his fencin’ was No. 6; but
when I got the job there the posts was
pretty, old, an he set me ah’ another bloke
puttin in new strainin’ posts on an east-and-
west, line runnin’ off the boundary. We
had em all up when he sent for Jim to go
into the homestead for a few days black-
smithin , an I had to go on strainin’ wire
until he came back.
I started on a Friday—blazin’ hot day,
too an blowed if ever I saw wire stretch
like that did. At every strain I had yards
of slack. I went back to see if any of the
strainers had give; but they were well stayed
an’ couldn’t shift. An’ the blanky wire
didn’t seem to be pullin’ out fine anywhere,
either. So I kep’ on strainin’, an’ the slack
kep’ on cornin’ —yards of it. When I
reached the end of that line I must have cut
off about three chain from each wire—an’
the whole line wasn’t more’n a mile long!
Started on the opposite side of the paddick
nex’ day, an’ the same thing happened.
“On Sunday I was doin’ a bit of washin’
when up drives the boss along with Bill
Smith, who was sinkin’ a prospectin’ shaft
in the nex’ paddick, about 40 chain over
the boundary on Dawson’s selection.
“ ‘Mornin’, Bill,’ sez the boss. ‘Feel any
earthquakes out here?’
“ ‘Earthquakes!’ I sez. ‘Why, has there
been a bust-up somewhere?’
“ ‘There must have been something of the
kind over at Smith’s shaft, from what he
says. Jump up an’ we’ll drive over an’ see.’
“All the way across the paddick things
seemed kind of altered. Here an’ there a
tree had fell, an’ in places the ground was
cracked. When we got to the boundary fence,
blowed if it hadn’t shifted, too! In place of
bein’ straight, it was boomeranged out west
’bout a couple of chain.
“ ‘The disturbance must have gone west,’
sez the boss. ‘Everything is pointin’ that
way.’
“Then we went to look at the fence I’d
strained on Friday. The wires was as tight
as a drum; but the boss called my attention
to the panels. At the east end of every
strain they was only 9ft. to 10ft. long, an’
I was dead-sure they was 12ft. when I
started strainin’. We drove along to the end
of the line, where I'd finished the first day,
has a look at it, when the boss sez: ‘Damn!’
sudden, an’ flogs the horses across to the
other line, an’ lias a squint at that. Then
he drove back to my camp, an’ just as I was
gettin’ out, he spots me strainin’ fork lyin’
agin the tent.
“ ‘ls that what you was usin’?’ he asks,
pointin’ with his whip.
“ ‘Yes,’ I sez.
“ ‘Bill,’ he sez, solemn, ‘that's the blanky
earthquake!’ ”
THE WASTER.
A Tea-room in Hiding.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I FOUND it by accident. Some fresh-
looking cheese in the window of a small,
new grocer’s shop drew me in where a
well-scrubbed grocer, with a smooth
white dome of head like an egg, and pursed-
up lips, waited grimly behind a spotless
counter for customers.
“Good morning,” I muttered. “Half a
pound of your tenpenny cheese, if you
please.”
As he wrapped up the segment of food
neatly in white paper, he gently broke the
news that he had opened a tea-room at the
rear of his shop. “That is the entrance,
sir,” lie explained, with a proud wave of the
hand at a curtain of colored beads in a par-
ticularly dark corner, between a pyramid of
biscuit tins and a pile of kerosene eases. 1
paid him for the cheese, and, impelled by
curiosity, I entered a gloomy, deserted
apartment, filled with round marble-topped
tables and the vicious smell of an angry
kerosene stove. There was a partition in
one corner, and an intermittent hissing and
spluttering indicated that the infernal
machine was concealed there. .
I sat down in a corner seat and waited
to be discovered and fed. The smell of
kerosene got steadily worse. I looked about
for a bell to give the alarm; but, finding
none, coughed in a half-hearted way.
“Oh, dear! I believe someone’s waiting!”
cried an agitated female over the border,
and did something to the stove that enraged
it so that it started to go on like a Chinese
stinkpot.
I was wondering if the angry thing was
getting ready to burst, when a lady in ordin-
ary street dress, and wearing that most ad-
mirable of disguises, an extinguisher hat,
came hurriedly from behind the partition.
“I am very sorry to have kept you,” she said
nervously, and her voice seemed strangely
familiar. “The fact is we are doing practi-
cally nothing to-day, and I was just going-
out to do a little shopping when you”
“WHAT!” I cried, as the light from
the window fell on her chin and mouth.
“Is it—Good Heavens !•—Mrs. Dodgewick !”
I stood up and hurriedly placed a chair for
her. She collapsed into it with a little cry,
and sniffed disconsolately.
“What are you doing here?” I asked
wildly. “You’re not running this place, are
you ?”
She nodded miserably.
“Is Dodgewick?” I began.
She sniffed again and dabbed at her eyes
with an absurdly small scrap of lace. "Mr.
Dodgewick and I have parted for ever,” she
sobbed.
I recognised that I had struck the Dodge-
wick family again at one of their critical
periods, and braced myself for revelations.
When Mrs. Dodgewick had calmed down a
little they came along. “He has started
going about with That Woman again! Last
week we had a dreadful scene, and I told
him I would go back to live at mother’s and
earn my own living. I saw Mr. Vane
spang’s advertisement for a refined lady to
take charge of a tea-room, and started here
last week; but the stove smells dreadfully,
and we’ve only taken Is. 3d.”
A mad hissing from the kerosene stove
made Mrs. Dodgewick jump up and run
behind the scenes to calm the dreadful
thing. I fanned vigorously with my hand-
kerchief at the clouds of stinking smoke roll-
ing over the partition, and wondered how I
could best make my escape.
The extinguisher hat appeared with start-
ling suddenness round the corner of the
partition. “I’m so sorry. I didn’t think of
it before. Did you come in for a cup of
tea ?”
“If it’s not too much trouble,” I answered.
She laughed mirthlessly. “Oh, don’t talk
about trouble,” she replied in the dreariest
possible way. “I’m here to take trouble,
you know. Now, if you don’t mind waiting,
I’ll pump up the stove; it appears to be dy-
ing down.”
In something less than a quarter of an
hour Mrs. Dodgewick, still in full regalia,
brought out a pot of tea, a plate of biscuits,

and two cups; then, in strict accordance
with business traditions, sat down to pour
out. She handed me one cup, and, disre-
garding my start of surprise, took the other
herself.
The stove here tried to commit suicide by
holding its breath ; but, finding that if was
too strong to burst just then, relieved its
feelings by blowing off the accumulated gas
with redoubled fury.
“He can do what he likes with That
Woman now! I don’t want to see him
again, now that I have found a means of
making a living and working up a good
business. Do have another bit of cake!”
I grimly helped myself to cake. Mrs.
imogewick stirred her tea excitedly.
“Where did you say this place was?”
called a liorribly-familiar voice in the shop
outside.
Mrs. Dodgewick half rose and sat down
again. “That’s him!” she hissed, regardless
of grammar.
“Straight on, if you please, sir,” replied
the toothless grocer.
Mrs. Dodgewick huddled over her cup; I
shrank back into the shadow; a hand swept
back half the bead curtain, and That Woman
rustled in and selected a seat in the opposite
corner. Dodgewick followed eagerly, took a
seat on the opposite side of the table, seized
one of the printed menus, and shoved it into
her hands. “What are you going to have,
Kid?” he mumbled in a shamefaced way.
Mrs. Dodgewick was quite effectively con-
cealed by her tremendous hat and the gloom
in which she sat; but in her terror lest the
pair should recognise her, she held the little
menu up against her face and pretended to
be reading it.
That Woman —her real name is Lottie
Something-or-other—put down the menu
Dodgewick had given her and said decisively,
but with a little break in her voice: “I don’t
want anything to eat at all!” It was plain
they had quarrelled.
Dodgewick started to fuss distractedly.
“Oh! but you must have something to eat,
Kid! What is there?” He grabbed the
menu and ran a trembling finger down it.
“What do you say to tea and fruit-cake, eh?
Yes, that’s what we’ll have! Where’s the
bell?” He looked round for a bell.
“He calls her ’Kid’!” hissed Mrs. Dodge-
wick tearfully behind the menu card.
“Sli-li-h! they’ll hear you!” I whispered
warningly, and, picking up another menu
card, glued my eyes to the line, “Stewed
fruits, 3d. extra.” I read it over 35 times
while Dodgewick was winning a reluctant
promise from Lottie that she would have a
cup of tea.
“Where’s the girl?” exclaimed Dodgewick.
He rapped on the marble-topped table with
his walking-stick, and made a futile effort
to capture Lottie’s left hand. She snatched
it away and fanned harder than ever. Be-
hind the partition, the stove hissed steadily
and the kettle commenced to boil over.
“Straight in, if you please!” I think we
all jumped when the bald grocer’s words of
direction broke the silence.
Two factory girls of the quiet variety
came doubtfully in. One of them was wear-
ing smoked glasses; the other bore in her
hand a folded novelette. They established
themselves at the table nearest the window,
and laid their heads together over the menu.
“Rot o’ tea and cake, tlirippence,” read the
one with the novelette in a hoarse whisper
to her short-sighted friend. , “Tea an’ scone,
thrippence. Pot o’ tea an’ ” _
“Look here,” interrupted Dodgewick, * I
wouldn’t wait if I were you ; the girl may
not be back for some time,” . .
“Where’s she gone?” demanded the girl in
smoked glasses aggressively.
“I don’t know,” snapped Dodgewick.
“Nice sorter place!” grumbled the girl.
Then, to her friend: “Allie, go an’ ask the
old bloke in the shop.”
The meek novelette reader rose silently
and went out. Mrs. Dodgewick, wide-eyed
with terror, shrank further back into the
shadow; Dodgewick made another vain at-
tempt to engage Lottie in conversation.
“You must be mistaken, my girl!” Mrs.
Dodgewick and I half rose as the bald
grocer, muttering irritably, dived through
the cascade of beads and looked round. “I’m
sure the lady— Eli?” lie must have been a
very nervous man, judging by the way he
sprang back when he caught us waving at
him.
“SH-H-H-H!” we hissed together, and
pointed at Dodgewick and Lottie.
The round-eyed factory girl, fearing the
worst, made for the door. The girl with
smoked glasses hurried after her.
The bald-headed grocer stood in an agony
of indecision. He was evidently impressed
by our hisses, for he put his hand to his
mouth and whispered hoarsely: “ What’s the
matter? I don’t understand!”
Dodgewick whirled round and made the
awestruck grocer jump by barking at him:
“What the devil are you whispering about?”
“It’s all right, sir,” muttered the grocer,
now thoroughly flustered, fiddling with his
apron.
“It isn’t all right!” shouted Dodgewick.
“I bring a lady in here”
“A LADY ! !” moaned Mrs. Dodgewick.
Dodgewick stopped dead and shrivelled
like a man who had put his foot upon a con-
versational rattlesnake. His eyes bulged in
a peculiar way, his face became purple, and
his head turned slowly from side to side like
that of a beast at bay.
The distraught grocer, seeing his oppor-
tunity, sidled forward. Mechanically—
almost unconsciously—he spoke his here-
ditary and accursed lines: “What can I do
for you, sir?”
Dodgewick gibbered at him; there is no
other way to describe it. “Pah !” he hissed
and flicked his fingers in the grocer’s face.
The grocer without a word turned in the
blue kerosene mist as a frightened fish turns
from a jerked fishing line, and wriggled
through the glass screen into the shop, where
he leant on the cheese with his back to-'
wards us, and stared immovably out of the
door, with his ears laid well back.
I shoved my chair aside and stepoed out
like the hero-fool in the third act. “Dodge-
wick,” I cried —assuredly I must have been
temporarily insane—“Dodgewick, we are
here!”
I looked in my madness to see the man
shrink with shame. To my. utter amaze-
ment after the first shock his eyes blazed
with triumph, and he pointed an accusing
finger dead at my crumb-powdered waist
coat. Caught! he howled. Caught like
rats m a trap .
“Who’s caught?’ I yelled defiantly.
“Who!” he screamed. “Why, you! You
blackguard ! WHAT ARE YOU DOING
HERE WITH MY WIFE?”
I have some recollection of striking an
attitude; of finding Mrs. Dodgewick cling-
ipg tearfully to my arm in the approved way
pf distressed females: of seeing Lottie adopt
a suitable pose (Virtue Dumbfounded); oi
Dodgewick, swollen with unjustifiable
wrath, acting the Stern But Just Husband.
Acting is the word. I realised somewhere
within me that we were all acting that_ it
was just cheap melodrama at the back of a
grocer’s shop which was rapidly going in-
solvent. Still I knew that the scene had
to be plaved out to its absurd conclusion,
whatever that might be.
“Dodgewick!” I cried, “you are making
a terrible mistake!” (“Terrible!” very
heavily accented.)
Pi?, eyes flashed. I lia\e long suspected
this! he cried, in deep, ringing tones.
“You, cur. What have you to say lor your-
se ß*
Then I woke up to the drivelling un-
reality of it all, and snapped hysterically,
• “Oh, don’t be such a d—d fool !”
It was like smashing the window of some
stuffy chamber of absurdities with an honest
brick. In a flash we were all normal
people again. Dodgewick was mouthing
curses under his breath, and trying to open
the window ; Lottie was sniffing miserably
and saying she wanted to go home; and
Mrs. Dodgewick was wailing about the lone-
liness of life without him and the explosive
capacities ot keiosene stoves.
Dodgewick strode magnificently to the
bead screen and roared at the grocer’s back :
“Turn this d—d thing off, do you hear!
Turn it off!”
The grocer came running, and dived
through the screen. The hissing merci-
fully ceased and he tip-toed back to the
shop through the haze.
Then Dodgewick caught his collapsing
wife by the arm, and, looking sternly at me,
spake these solemn words in a thrilling voice
at Lottie, his faithful but superannuated
barmaid, at that moment engaged in dabbing
•i flier eves with a small handkerchief - “Take
at nei eyes witn a small nancu tiune . raise
care of her, Johnson. See that she wants
for nothing, you understand?”
“All right,” I answered without enthusi-
asm>
Dodgewick then tenderly conducted his
wife from the room. I noticed he paused a
moment to say something to the petrified
grocer as they passed slowly out.
Then I turned to Lottie and found her
gazing at me with great, big, mournful eyes.
“Oh, Kid,” she said, “that Woman has no
Soul!”
I rose hastily. “For Heaven’s sake let
us get out into the air.” I groped for my
hat and threw my half-pound of cheese under
thd table. Then, with my head bursting, I
held the bead curtain aside for Lottie to pass
out
KODAK.
The Swimmers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.)
C'OR all the ease of the spring-swung car
r And the cushioned air in the rubbet
wheel,
The world moves heavily , all ajar,
On grinding axle and pounding heel.
Yet here is rest when the toil is done.
Buoyant water around , below —
The wind biotas out to the setting sun,
And soft as velvet the currents flow.
The sand is cool to our shoe-burned feet,
The wind is warm on our sun-burned
limbs —
Very beautiful, aye, and sweet,
The flash of the feathering foam that rims
The sweeping curve of the dreaming bay;
So white the foam on the sea’s dark blue—
The blue that stretches away, away,
Where the wind and the sun are going to.
Roars the surf as our heads go down,
Down, and our feet throw water high,
And eyes see clear, where the sunbeams
drown,
The sands of silver whose ripplings try
To raise each ridge to the shape of the sea-
That, marching ever by ways well-trod,
Crush the sand with their heavy knees
And lift their glorious plumes to God.
Up to the sun and the wind again
With a brown arm lifted, a pent breath
blown—
The sea for men and the sons of men—
May every man come into his own!
One shake of the head to clear the eyes
Of the sparkling green of the under-tide,
Then low, stretch low, till the waters rise
On heads that tumble the waves aside.
At every stroke of the sweeping limbs,
The sea gives ever her kind, cool kiss.
Oh ! sea, no lady of moods and whims
Has aught to offer so sweet as this.
So soft you swing us, as light as down,
So lightly hold us, and leave us free
To rob a wave of his snow'-white crown,
Or seek the heart of the under-sea.
Low, swim low till the bubbles blind.
Slow, swim slow lest the muscles tire.
Leave the path to the west behind,
The path for the wind, all paved with fire,
And let us swim to the beach again
With brown arm lifted and slow breath
blown. '
The sea for men and the Sons of men,
Yet the land calls ever to claim her own.
The sand is warm to our sea-cool feet,
The wind is warmer than sun or sand.
Oh ! Freedom glorious, strong and sweet,
We cannot hold you by voice or hand.
We can but steal from you one brief hour
And drain it swiftly of all it holds,
As bees slip into a closing flower
And take the honey before it folds.
Up from the sea where the red sun sank
Blaze all the colors the sunset, sends.
And Night is mustering rank on rank
To fill the world to its utmost ends.
The flower is closing—the dreaming bay
Is robbed of its white and its rich, bright
blue—
Ah r What's to the westward, away, away,
Where the sun and the ivind are going io.
MAOItII.AM).
WILL LAWSON.

10,000 FREE TRIAL BOXES.
Those who are troubled with falling hair, dandruff,
baldness, itching scalp, ' etc., know full well the
humiliation that they cause. There is no need, how-
ever, of such conditions. These troubles can be
quickly overcome by my True Hair Grower.
I am not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. My hair began to grow thin about the time
I reached the age of thirty. When I had reached the
age of thirty-five the top of my head was quite bald.
I had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. 1 invested in every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of, and here let me
say that not only did I try all the English so-called
restorers, but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and considerable time —yet the hairs came
not. I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoying- a brief busi-
ness respite by an Alpine trip, I fell in with an old
collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly plen-
tiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished he could
.spare some of his hair for my benefit. This led him
to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suggested that 1 have a quantity of the
compound prepared. I did this a few days later. I
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered bv a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair
Since then the hair on my head has been as luxuriant
as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, I have for some time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people
who have actually grown new hair through the
medium of my true hair grower. I do not pretend
to say it is the only hair grower in the world—l am
not interested in decrying others —but I do assert
that it is the most reliable, and in the end by far the
cheapest.
